Preface
This research paper represents a master thesis in Geotechnical Engineering, being the last necessary
condition to acquire the Master Degree of Science in Cold Climate Engineering at Norwegian
University of Science and Technology (NTNU) and the Master Degree of Science in Civil Engineering
at Technical University of Denmark (DTU).
The thesis was elaborated during a time interval of six months, from January 2023 to June 2023,
constituting an academic recognition equivalent to 30 ECTS. The research was performed under the
supervision of Prof. Dr. Gudmund Reidar Eiksund (NTNU), Assoc. Prof. Ivan Depina (NTNU), Asoc.
Prof. RenГ© Wugt Larsen (DTU), Senior Researcher Hamid Nick (DTU), Researcher Mohammad
Reza Hajiabadi (DTU).
The topic of study was selected based on the established cooperation with University Centre in
Svalbard (UNIS), bringing a contribution to PermaMeteoCommunity project. A workshop meeting
was organized by the project coordinators at UNIS in February 2023, emphasizing the focus points
of the workload. The beginning of data processing was correlated with the specialization project
attended at NTNU during the fall semester of 2022. Most of the field measurements and laboratory
data were provided to me thanks to the PermaMeteoCommunity partnership.
For a suitable understanding of this thesis, it is assumed that the reader possess a basic background
knowledge of geotechnics, geophysics, and thermodynamics.
Trondheim, 11.06.2023
Marian-Bogdan Ciorescu

II
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Slope stability assessment can represent a highly complex topic of investigation, requiring a consider-
able amount of field information in order to provide consistent predictions or to reconstruct historical
events. In the Arctic regions, additional structural related components should be integrated into the
stability analysis, due to the unique features encountered on the sites. A relevant influence can be
connected to the active layer development starting from the end of freezing season until the final
part of thawing period. The combination of thawed soil with significant infiltration events, in the
form of powerful rainstorms or prolonged precipitation intervals, can considerably affect the structural
integrity of a slope system. The complexity of the analysis can be further escalated by expanding the
stability model from an individual slope case to a regional scale, because of the spatial variability
characterizing the soil geotechnical properties.
The studied area in this paper is represented by Longyeardalen, Svalbard. A considerable amount of
field measurements, laboratory testing results, analytical determinations were processed for the region,
with the purpose of generating a suitable input configuration for the stability assessment model.
Essential data, related to the landslide vulnerability of a slope system, can be divided generally into
thermal parameters, mentioning soil heat capacity, thermal conductivity, grain density, unfrozen water
content; hydrological parameters such as soil permeability, hydraulic diffusivity, SWCC properties,
water table level, porosity; and strength parameters including soil cohesion, internal friction angle,
specific weight, rigidity moduli.
A numerical thermal analysis was performed in PLAXIS V22, a finite element computational software,
aiming to predict the evolution of active layer around Longyeardalen with respect to time. The
numerical model was calibrated based on the existing thermal data series collected along the valley,
the output was validated with thermal information from different projects conducted in the proximity
of Longyearbyen between 2019-2022, along with the analytical solution proposed by modified Berggren
equation. The last step of the thermal analysis was the prediction of soil thermal regime during the
thawing season in 2016, important input for the stability assessment. Apart from thermal monitoring,
PLAXIS was used to create a simplified hydrological model, targeting to identify the water table
location along the slope profile. The implemented configuration was intended to reproduce the
hydrological context on 14th of October 2016, when a significant mass movement event was recorded
in Longyeardalen.
The stability assessment was conducted in TRIGRS, an infinite slope based computational software,
with the intention of generating landslide susceptibility maps consisting of safety factors distribution
for the zones surrounding Longyeardalen. The results interpreted from the thermal and hydrological
numerical analysis were incorporated in the simplified stability model, providing a realistic initial
setup to reconstruct the event in October 2016. In addition, a sensitivity analysis was performed for
several input factors which manifest an influence over the structural integrity of the slope systems,
including soil cohesion, soil friction angle, water table location. The variation of the computed safety
factors was observed to be relatively proportional to the measured change on the assessed parameters,
given that the values are referenced to the initial conformation. The stability model was created in
a simplified form, and it is particularly sensible to the initial implemented data, nevertheless, the
reconstruction process presents promising results.

Schematic representation of possible hydrological consequences related to the freez-

table ascension according to the information presented in [Terlien, 1998]; (b) Shallow
landslide mechanism development, associated with thaw front advancement, in regions
with a frost susceptible upper soil layer, based on the discussion in [Tsai et al., 1999]. 22

XVII
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 1
Introduction
Stability assessment processes can be perceived as a highly complex research topic, particularly taking
into consideration the level of uncertainty prevalent in geotechnical investigations or the spatial
variability describing soil properties over extensive regions of interest. A wide variety of data should
be collected and interpreted from the targeted sites, in order to acquire a realistic reproduction of the
natural conditions on a slope system. The essential components required for a consistent stability
analysis in the Arctic regions, as discussed in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], can be classified as
thermal data, incorporating soil thermal conductivity, heat capacity, grain density, unfrozen water
content, gravimetric water content, soil latent heat, thermal expansion or contraction coefficient,
temperature profiles recorded from boreholes drilled on the investigated site; hydrological data,
consisting of soil permeability, hydraulic diffusivity, saturated pore fluid content, residual pore fluid
content, soil porosity, pore pressure distribution, soil-water characteristic curve parameters, saturation
profiles recorded from boreholes drilled on the investigated site; strength data, in the form of soil
cohesion, internal friction angle, specific unit weight, rigidity moduli, soil degree of homogeneity
and isotropy; topographic data, expressed as elevation maps for the area of study, bedrock outcrop,
terrain inclination; respectively meteorological data, including precipitation levels, air temperature,
snow cover depth, air relative humidity.
The investigated area is represented by Longyeardalen, a region situated in Svalbard archipelago.
A comprehensive map, describing the topographical context surrounding Longyeardalen, can be
observed in Figure 1.1. As it can be seen in Figure 1.1, Longyeardalen is delimited on the north side
by Adventfjorden, the steep slope systems on the left side of the valley are part of Sverdruphamaren
and Naestefjellet, eastwards the valley is bordered by Sukkertoppen and Gruvefjellet, while on
the south part Sarkofagen mountain can be observed, along with the two glaciers Larsbreen and
Longyearbreen. Multiple slope systems can be visualized in Figure 1.1, indicating a justified stability
concern for the community living in the region, especially due to the increased steepness describing
most of the inclined terrains located in the proximity of the valley. Furthermore, with the shifting
climatic trends monitored during the last decades in the Arctic, an escalation of air temperatures
is expected in the future, combined with an intensification of rainstorm frequency, as discussed in
[Working-Group-I, 2013], [Gassner et al., 2015].
In the Arctic regions, the thermal regime describing the soil profile can be correlated with a particular
zonal classification, according to [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], along with structural implications
for the studied location. Therefore, based on [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], the distinctive soil
areas can be denoted active layer and permafrost, depending on the temperature recorded in the
ground. The active layer represents the topsoil layer, developing a temperature variation above
and below 0в—ӢC during a full year cycle, while permafrost can be defined as a soil layer maintaining
temperatures below 0в—ӢC during consecutive freezing periods and the adjacent melting season
[Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. In general, the active layer can manifest an increased vulnerability
to external disturbing factors, while the permafrost presents a consistent rigidity, unless it is subjected
1
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 1. Introduction
to degradation processes in time.
The stability assessment model should integrate all the components described above, covering the
entire region of Longyeardalen. The importance of such research can be emphasized by understanding
the slope structural response to external destabilizing factors, along with localizing the possible risk
zones susceptible to landslide initialization.
Figure 1.1: Descriptive topographic map for the region of Longyeardalen, comprehending the most
important location names and land-form features discussed within the stability assessment. The
ortofoto map was accessed from the Norwegian Polar Institute database.
1.1
Research purpose
The mass movement event recorded in the area of Longyeardalen in October 2016, as discussed in
[Christiansen et al., 2016], leaded to extensive and local collapses associated with a large number of
slopes. Following this event, the necessity of providing a suitable monitoring system, together with a
valuable prediction tool for estimating the structural integrity of the surrounding slopes, emerged as
an important focus point for the community of Longyearbyen. The relevance of this investigation
can be further emphasized by the lack of landslide susceptibility maps dedicated to Longyeardalen
region. Thereafter, the development of a simplified regional stability model is prioritized within the
current paper.
A broad range of field investigations and laboratory measurements have to be integrated in the design-
ing numerical models. For this purpose, a large data source containing thermal, hydrological, graphical
information is provided by the PermaMeteoCommunity project [Tveit, 2022] for interpretation.
2
==PAGE:22==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Following the data processing stage, a thermal numerical model and a hydrological numerical model
should be implemented for computational purposes. The selected software for both analysis is
represented by PLAXIS V22, a finite element based program. The main target for the thermal
analysis can be represented by monitoring and predicting the active layer thickness variation, as a
function of heat influx propagation from the ground surface level. The model should be calibrated
with the temperature profiles accessed from [Tveit, 2022], validated with the temperature trumpets
available in the region for 2019-2022 [NVE, 2017], [RambГёll, 2017], followed by the implementation
of the model for year 2016, aiming to assess the thermal background regime prior to the landslide
initialization in October. For validation purposes, an additional analytical solution is considered
in the analysis, being represented by the modified Berggren equation [Aldrich and Paynter, 1966].
For the hydrological numerical model, the purpose is directed to identify the water flow pattern
through the slope profile, particularly being focused on establishing the approximate location of the
water table. The relative determination of the hydrological steady state condition, governing the
slope systems around Longyeardalen, can be a valuable information to be further integrated into the
regional stability model. It should be mentioned that the position of the underground water level is
associated with the pore pressure development inside the soil material, thereafter, this detail should
be provided to the stability simulation.
The stability model should integrate the thermal and hydrological output generated from the previously
described analysis, along with soil strength data, topographic information, meteorological data. For
the regional stability assessment TRIGRS is selected for the simulation of landslide susceptibility
maps, being a computational software based on the infinite slope method. The maps consist of a
safety factor distribution, generated accordingly to the defined grid of the region. Within this research,
the reconstruction of the mass movement event indicated in [Christiansen et al., 2016] is targeted,
creating a simplified model capable of comprehending the consequences of a consistent infiltration
situation. Furthermore, a sensitivity analysis is performed for several input parameters, emphasizing
the induced impact on the structural integrity of the slope systems. The chosen parameters for the
analysis are represented by soil cohesion, internal friction angle, water table location. The sensitivity
analysis is intended to record the obtained safety factor variations, characterizing the computational
process and distribution patterns. It should be mentioned that according to [Baum et al., 2008],
TRIGRS can manifest an elevated sensitivity to the numerical fluctuations related to the initial
input data, therefore, the sensitivity analysis can contribute significantly to the enhancement of the
model, along with the improvement of the calculating precision within the assessment. Based on the
proposed simplified model, further adaptations can be developed, in order to include more complex
computational scenarios and initial configurations.
1.2
Main objectives of the thesis
Considering the lack of research dedicated for the slope stability assessment in the region of
Longyeardalen, combined with the concern regarding the structural integrity of the slopes impacted
by powerful rainstorm events, the necessity of a landslide susceptibility investigation emerged. In
order to assure a comprehensive analysis, the aim of this report focuses on the following objectives:
вҖў Investigating landslide processes and challenges related to the Arctic environment.
вҖў Processing and interpreting the available geological and geotechnical data collected from the
region of Longyeardalen.
вҖў Implementation of a thermal numerical model, using Plaxis as a finite element computation
software, along with the calibration and validation of the model based on the thermal historical
data available for the area of Longyeardalen.
3
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 1. Introduction
вҖў Implementation of a hydrological numerical model, using Plaxis as a finite element computation
software, with the purpose of analyzing the ground water flow conditions along the slopes
surrounding Longyeardalen.
вҖў Implementation of a spatially distributed landslide model using TRIGRS, aiming for the
assessment of landslide susceptibility describing the slope systems around Longyeardalen.
вҖў Generating a landslide susceptibility map of the region, based on the case study of Longyeardalen,
by combining the available field and laboratory measurements, thermal output from the
modelling scenarios, hydrological behavior observed in the simulated approach.
1.3
Structure of the report
The structure of the report consists of 9 chapters, being further divided into sections and subsections.
In addition, an Appendix is also provided at the end of the paper. The general content for each
chapter can be observed below.
Chapter 1 вҖ“ Includes the introduction to the researched topic, the motivation of conducting the
presented investigation, the precise objectives intended to be acquired during the research, and the
specific structure of the report.
Chapter 2 вҖ“ Intended to familiarize the reader with the essential knowledge concerning landslides
nomenclature and concepts, being structured as a schematic literature review. It covers classification
systems adapted to the Arctic environment, a list of the factors influencing the landslide susceptibility,
common computational methods used in stability assessments, particular stability challenges regarding
the cold regions.
Chapter 3 вҖ“ Describes the geological and meteorological background characterizing Longyeardalen
area, prior information for the stability analysis. The mass movement event affecting the region in
October 2016 is also presented within this chapter.
Chapter 4 вҖ“ Incorporates all the field and laboratory data processed for modelling purposes, along
with the boreholesвҖҷ location and description. The main datasets contain geotechnical information
about the extracted soil samples, as well as measurements regarding the thermal regime around
Longyeardalen.
Chapter 5 вҖ“ Describes the necessary input requested by the thermal numerical model such as slope
geometry, materials definition, boundary conditions, scenario descriptions. Moreover, it includes
the theoretical background used by Plaxis for the simulation part, together with details about the
calibration process, validation process, and the analytical solution suggested for comparison purposes.
Chapter 6 вҖ“ Describes the necessary input requested by the hydrological numerical model such as slope
geometry, materials definition, boundary conditions, scenario descriptions. It further incorporates the
theoretical background regarding water flow propagation in Plaxis.
Chapter 7 вҖ“ Includes the theoretical background implemented in the regional stability assessment
model in TRIGRS, along with a complete description about the initial input data generation. Most
of the information required by TRIGRS have to be in a grid format, hence, QGIS functionality was
consistently used. In addition, a description of the defined parameters in TRIGRS and TopoIndex
initialization scripts is also presented.
Chapter 8 вҖ“ Presents the results generated by the thermal analysis, hydrological analysis, and regional
stability assessment.
Chapter 9 вҖ“ Contains the summarized ideas resulting from the assessment process, together with
the limitations associated with the conducted research. Recommendations for possible further
investigations on the studied topic are also included.
4
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 2
Landslides - conceptualization
The concept of landslide can be perceived as a comprehensive topic, approachable from multiple
professional perspectives, therefore, considering the stability interest of this research, landslides
should be correlated with extensive geotechnical and geological implications. As it is discussed in
previous research [Highland and Bobrowsky, 2008], [Highland and Johnson, 2004], this phenomenon
describes a complex series of occurrences, resulting in a gravitational movement of the material mass,
downwards and outwards the slope profile. A suitable understanding of the landslide characteristics,
such as classification types, triggering mechanisms, factors of influence, mechanical behavior, can
represent a valuable prediction tool, contributing to the mitigation and limitation of the failure
repercussions over the habitable areas located in the risk zones.
Figure 2.1: Idealized landslide diagram illustrating the elementary constituive components, based on
the information in [Turner and Schuster, 1996] and [Varnes, 1978].
Considering the broad dimension of the available technical literature dedicated on this topic
[Highland and Bobrowsky, 2008], [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], [Turner and Schuster, 1996], or
[Varnes, 1978], multiple classification systems and key parameters can be interpreted for the analysis.
However, it is worth mentioning that the physical principles, governing the dynamics of slope systems,
are to a large extent similar, regardless the filters applied for the categorization. Furthermore, taking
5
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
into account that the area of interest is represented by Longyeardalen, the focus should be directed
to the arctic environment, along with the most relevant notations and explanations associated with
the cold regions.
The common nomenclature encountered on the landslide topic, applicable particularly for the case
of earth slide or earth flow, is illustrated in Figure 2.1 as an idealized detailed diagram, based
on the knowledge provided in [Turner and Schuster, 1996], [Varnes, 1978]. The definitions for the
previously mentioned failure modes will be elaborated in the following paragraph, nevertheless the
visualization of the elementary landslide components can pose a significant advantage in the further
understanding of slide behavior. As it can be seen in Figure 2.1, the features are well delimitated
geometrically, describing a specific zone or detail. Therefore, according to [Turner and Schuster, 1996]
and [Varnes, 1978], the crown represents the remaining material located on the superior side of the
main scarp; the main scarp is described as the visible part of the surface of rupture, formed by the
inclined undisturbed soil material located on the upper edge of the landslide; the top symbolizes the
highest connection point established between the steep main scarp and the dislocated material; the
head emphasizes the contact edge at the upside of the landslide delimitating the main scarp to the
displaced material; the minor scarp implies the abrupt surface formed by differential displacement
inside the landslide dislocated mass; the toe represents the lowest delimitation margin of displaced
material in the landslide at the furthest distance from the main scarp; the tip is a point located on
the toe curve at the maximum measurable length from the top; the original ground surface expresses
the existed slope surface prior to failure; the surface of rupture forms the lower boundary for the
mobilized mass underneath the original ground surface; the toe of surface of rupture represents the
common curve situated at the intersection between the surface of rupture and the original ground
surface; the surface of separation illustrates the area included in the original ground surface which is
covered by the dislocated material above; the main body describes the part of the mobilized mass
overlying the surface of rupture from the main scarp at the upper side to the toe of the surface of
rupture beneath; the foot symbolizes the downside segment of the landslide which overpasses the toe
of the ground surface overlaying the separation interface; the flank consists of the undisturbed mass,
part of the original ground surface, in the proximity of the surface of rupture limit, being denoted
with respect to the direction of displacement; the zone of depletion represents the region along the
landslide profile where the elevation of the dislocated material surface is beneath the elevation of the
original ground surface; the zone of accumulation signifies the region along the landslide profile where
the elevation of the dislocated material surface is above the elevation of the original ground surface.
2.1
Classification system
The most important characteristics contributing to the classification of landslides are described
by the constitutive materials composing the slope structure and the pattern of movement for
the mobilized mass [Highland and Bobrowsky, 2008] [Highland and Johnson, 2004]. Even though
the United States Geological Classification (USGC) appears to be one of the most widespread
classification systems, based on the documentation presented in [Highland and Bobrowsky, 2008],
the arctic regions should be evaluated individually. The reason behind this consideration should be
attributed to the extreme climatic environment inherent to these locations and the unique geological
formations developed and preserved in the arctic circumstances. Consequently, the system suggested
by [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004] should be a suitable alternative, subdividing the material into
naturally deposited soil, rocks, artificial fill generated by human activity or a mixture between the
previously mention types. The movement trends can be expanded into flows, slides or falls. Moreover,
the soil composition should be interpreted as a four-component system, including the soil particles,
water, ice and air, and a specific distinction have to be emphasized between thawed state and frozen
state [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004].
6
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
Starting from the indexation indicated in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], landslides can be identified
as flow dominated, slide dominated or fall dominated, as it can be seen in Figure 2.2. Furthermore,
the ramification can be extended to solifluction, skin, bimodal, multiple retrogressive for the flow type,
block, rotational, multiple retrogressive for the slide class, respectively rock fall, topple connected to
fall failure mode. Each class has its own particularities, defining geotechnical challenges correlated
with the movement pattern. According to [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], flow landslides exhibit a
spatially continuous movement similar with a viscous fluid being pulled gravitationally, preserving a
relatively evenly distributed mobilization throughout the soil mass. Slides are related to downward
displacements along one or multiple surfaces of rupture, characterized by excessive shear strains
developing across these surfaces. In general, the material mass can maintain its coherence during the
movement, manifesting a comparable behavior with the mechanical motion of a rigid body. Fall failures
can be visualized as detachments of entire masses from slope profiles, especially when the inclination
angle has an elevated value, descending rapidly as rolling, gradual bouncing or free falling through the
air. For the permafrost rich areas the dominant failure mode appears to be flow, as the thawing of
the permafrost causes the initialization of the destabilization process [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004].
The terminology included on the last ramification level in Figure 2.2 is explained in the following
paragraphs, according to the information analyzed from [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004].
Figure 2.2: Landslide classification system associated with the arctic regions, related to the data on
[Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004].
Solifluction can be described as a slow-paced surface deformation, specifically restricted to upper
active layer influence zone, forming shallow lobes elongated downward the slope profile. It implies
extended time intervals which allow the development of the strain increment, under the continuous
exposure to the seasonal climatic conditions in the arctic. Skin flow involves the detachment of the
superficial soil layer, mainly containing small vegetation components, organic parts, and minerals,
moving gravitationally along the inclined surface. Skin flow can be present in steep terrains, as well
as in medium inclined topographies, having a dislocated soil thickness considerable lower than the
actual length of the flow. Bimodal flow represents a type of flow including two distinct displacement
patterns associated with the slope failure. A distinct feature describing this mode comprehends a
relatively steep main scarp at the top of the landslide together with a mild inclination for the toe
at the bottom of the displaced material. In the multiple retrogressive flow situation, the ridges
delimitating the main body of the landslides are well-defined and predominantly curved. They extend
from the main scarp which tends to move backwards as a result of consecutive failure events.
7
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
Block slides, also known in the technical literature as translational slides, involves the displacement
of a relatively coherent mass of material across relatively planar surfaces or slopes with slightly
undulatory features [Highland and Bobrowsky, 2008]. Depending on the extent describing the surface
of rupture, the dimension of the slide may vary considerably, mobilizing material from unconsolidated
soil to compacted slabs or rocks. As a specific characteristic, block slides are susceptible to occur in
the proximity of geological weakness zones such as bedding planes, faults or delimitation surface
between permafrost and seasonal active layer. Rotational slides can be defined as failure modes with
a curved upward orientated surface of rupture, where the displaced mass follow a rotational pattern
about a parallel axis to the slope contour. During the detachment process the lower part of the soil
mass may be displaced downwards, while the upper part of the mass will tilt backward on the main
scarp area. In the arctic regions, rotational slides can be usually observed in the thawed banks along
stream valleys, rich in river deposits or sediments. Multiple retrogressive slides can be described as
a succession of coherent material masses which tend to bend backwards, towards the main scarp.
When the constitutive material reaches its bearing capacity, the whole system of blocks is translated
gravitationally along the slope profile.
Rock fall represents the downward motion of a detached rock or soil from a relatively steep slope
profile or an overhanging cliff top. The motion implies high velocities of the free-falling material,
combined with bouncing after the bottom impact and rolling until the inclination angle prevent
further advancement of the material mass. If the impact possesses enough potential energy or the
material strength is exceeded, the coherent mass can be decomposed, aspect which may potentially
lead to different modes of failure. Topple failure can be identified as a forward outwards rotation
of a rock or soil material, where the rotational axis is located below the gravity center of the
detached mass. Multiple phenomenon and processes can contribute to the initialization of a topple
displacement, nevertheless, the hydrological impact and climatic exposure appear to represent the
dominant influence factors [Highland and Bobrowsky, 2008]. Regarding the arctic environment, fall
landslides are regularly observed on the surface drainage pathways, where the erosion can be able to
retrieve structural material beneath the frozen banks or thermal alteration of the exposed permafrost
can experience significant strength reduction, until the point of destabilization is reached.
2.2
Impact factors over landslide susceptibility
The failure mechanism, developed inside a slope structure, is the result of a successive propagation of
destabilizing processes and structural properties alteration. The influence of the temporal component
over the slope stability assessment was emphasized in previous documentations such as [Varnes, 1978]
or [McColl, 2015], suggesting that both the material strength characteristics and the evolution of the
driving forces, responsible for the disturbance of the equilibrium state, are subjected to variation over
extensive time intervals. Consequently, the impact factors discussed in the present chapter should be
associated with their temporal implication, gradually contributing to a failure triggering process.
The dominant aspects, involved in the classification of the impact factors, can be described as
the componentвҖҷs genesis and the level of contribution to the overall destabilization, related to
the material mass inside the slope structure.
Considering the prior observations, multiple in-
dexation systems can be generated, nevertheless, the structure recommended by [McColl, 2015]
or [Sayao and Ortigao, 2004] was selected for the purpose of this documentation. According to
[McColl, 2015] and [Sayao and Ortigao, 2004], three principal classes should be emphasized in the
slope stability assessment, denoted physical factors, geomorphological factors, respectively human
factors. A schematic representation of the classification system can be seen in Figure 2.3, presenting
a further expansion for each type. Hence, mechanical or chemical weathering; seismic activity; thaw-
ing/freezing cycles; intensive precipitation/rainfall should be included in the physical factors class,
for the geomorphological field the ramification includes glacial rebound; sedimentation or deposition;
8
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
fluvial, wave or glacial erosion; sheared, jointed or fissured material; while the human factors can
be correlated with artificial induced vibration; water leakage from utility pipelines; excavation of
slopes; construction related activities. As it was observed in [McColl, 2015], the actual slope failure
event should be perceived as an accumulation of multiple factors contributing to the destabilization
process, rather than attributing the outcome to an apparent principal element.
The physical factors can be described by a dynamic behavior, inducing considerable damage to the soil
system in relatively reduced time intervals. As it can be observed in Figure 2.3, some components such
as intensive rainfall or seismic activity have the potential of destabilizing the soil mass within a limited
number of occurrences, in contrast with thawing/freezing cycles which generate a gradual increase
of the landslide susceptibility. Thawing/freezing cycles can be responsible for considerable volume
variations in the ground cluster, especially when the upper soil layer contains freezing susceptible
materials, oversaturation of the top layer induced by snow melting or ideal conditions for sliding
planes propagation, considering the case of an overlaying stratification described by a thawed soil
layer above a frozen impermeable one. Mechanical weathering disintegrates the coherent structure
of the slope constitutive material into smaller segments, by a physical breakdown process, while the
chemical weathering decomposes through hydrolysis, dissolution or oxidation the mineral structure of
the soil or rock, producing a remanent alteration of the affected medium [Sayao and Ortigao, 2004].
Based on the data presented in [McColl, 2015], both mechanical and chemical weathering may cause
strength reduction along the slope profile, increasing the risk of solifluction or liquefication for the
superior part of the soil column. In the same research, the implications of the intensive rainfalls over
the stability assessment are described, outlining the extensive damage produced by the pore water
pressure uprise or variation of the ground water table. The pore water pressure can be correlated
with a negative impact over the general stability equilibrium, facilitating the mass mobilization in the
slope system by supplementing the driving loads in the close proximity of the detachment plane/shear
plane. Seismic activity generates a similar effect by propagating waves through the ground medium,
elevating the vulnerability for the initiation of rupture surfaces. Furthermore, the dynamic action
can lead to a structural rearrangement of the soil skeleton, amplifying the possibility of differential
settlements to occur, the development of tensile cracks particularly in the vicinity of discontinuities
or faults, the reduction of shear strength in the affected areas [Varnes, 1978].
Figure 2.3: Classification system of the impact factors influencing the landslide susceptibility, according
to [McColl, 2015] and [Sayao and Ortigao, 2004].
9
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
Geomorphological factors include a wide variety of natural parameters and processes, which induce a
steady degradation of the general stability of a slope system. According to [Sayao and Ortigao, 2004]
the features associated with the initialization of the soil mass destabilization can be predicted
with a certain level of confidence, as they manifest a similar recursive pattern. The most relevant
components, belonging to the geomorphological factor class are illustrated in Figure 2.3, mentioning
glacial rebound; shared, joint or fissured material; sedimentation or deposition; fluvial, wave or glacial
erosion. When the stability assessment is targeting a long term scenario the glacial rebound represents
a significant factor affecting the zonal topography, by a continuous modification of the geometrical
boundary conditions of a slope. Even though the rate of displacement can be usually reduced in
intensity, it is recommended to integrate the effects in the analysis [McColl, 2015]. Both geological
weakness zones and erosional processes can be associated with slope equilibrium perturbance, allowing
for the development of rupture surfaces inside the slope mass. Moreover, the water infiltration
increases with the lack of homogeneity in the material, leading to a propagation of the shear strength
reduction along the system. Therefore, the landslide susceptibility grows relatively proportional with
the presence of joints, fissured material, or erosional zones [Sayao and Ortigao, 2004]. A similar
behavior can be observed for the sedimentation or deposition parameter, impacting the cohesiveness
characteristic of the soil structure. With the accumulation of loose particles inside the already
existing fissures or joints, the shear strength is diminished, and the internal stress level increases
the material detachment vulnerability [Sayao and Ortigao, 2004]. Based on the research presented
in [McColl, 2015], the uncertainty connected to the geomorphological parameters cumulates with
the spatial variability feature, which describes the strength parameters of the constitutive material
along the slope profile, leading to a considerable challenge regarding the accurate determination
of the stability state. In the same paper, it was observed that partial failure can occur on isolated
local areas within the system boundaries, implying remanent deformations, even though the general
stability assessment may indicate favorable results. The previously mentioned idea has a particular
significance concerning the analysis output interpretation, requesting for a precise definition of the
desired simulation tolerance.
Excavation of slopes; construction related activities; artificial induced vibration; water leakage from
utility pipelines represent some of the principal human impact factors relevant for slope stability
investigations, as it can be seen in Figure 2.3. The factors belonging to this class can be described by
human activity operations leading to the alteration of the natural topographical or geomorphological
environment in a region, perturbing the structural stability condition. Furthermore, according to the
research conducted in [Widayanti et al., 2019] and [Zhao et al., 2010], the landslide susceptibility
appears to be more prevalent in areas with an intense level of human development, along with the
unsuitable slope preservation practices connected to the construction industry. The slope excavation
may pose a considerable landslide triggering contributor, as the in-situ stress level suffers major
changes depending on the quantity of the extracted material. Moreover, the soil surface, exposed
to environmental degrading actions, can be enlarged during the excavation process, resulting in
a reduction of the material inner strength [McColl, 2015]. When referred to the artificial induced
vibration the effects can be comparable with the damage inflicted by the seismic activity, propagating
transitory dynamic stresses through the soil structure, hence restructuring the mineral skeleton and
amplifying the sliding possibility. The over saturation of the upper residual soil layer, surge of the
pore pressure inside vulnerable subsurface zones or uplift of the ground water table can be associated
with the malfunction of the utility pipes installed in steep terrains, impacting the safety conditions
along the slope profile. As it was emphasized in [Widayanti et al., 2019] risk assessments should
be considered prior to any infrastructure development project in order to identify the possible slide
susceptible regions and the repercussions inherent to the destabilization scenarios. Consequently,
preestablished implementation procedures should be indicated for the construction projects, as well
as mitigation recommendations to prevent the downslope mass mobilization.
10
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
2.3
Stability computational approaches
The slope structural integrity should be evaluated with respect to the mechanical equilibrium principles,
considering the impact generated by the destabilizing actions, along with the favorable conditions
induced by the stabilizing forces [Turner, 1996]. Therefore, the identification of the qualitative
parameters defining the mechanical response of the slope system should be conducted and referenced
to a relevant failure criterion. Even though the slope systems can be described by a three-dimensional
spatial behavior, a pragmatical engineering simplification can be expressed by a 2D force equilibrium
problem focusing on the limitation of the existing loads, commonly quantified in the horizontal and
vertical principal directions, and the bending moment below the strength capacity of the soil mass.
Based on the observations presented in [Lambe and Whitman, 1969], the stability assessment can
be considerably affected by three soil mechanical characteristics, as it can be seen in Figure 2.4. Pore
water pressure can elevate the stress level inside the slope system, destabilizing the existing natural
balance of the soil structure. Furthermore, the free water and moist movement, caused by the gravita-
tional seepage, may be responsible for the development of sliding surfaces along with strength reduction
patterns [Lambe and Whitman, 1969]. Regarding soil geometry, [Lambe and Whitman, 1969] sug-
gests that this category should incorporate the main topographical and geomorphological parameters
which can be correlated with the initiation of a slide. As the material cluster is lacking homogeneity
or contains weakness zones, the force equilibrium may be disturbed. Finally, the soil strength can be
described as a set of geotechnical features necessary for preventing consistent structural damages in
the analyzed system. Generally, some important parameters included in this classification can be
internal friction angle of the material, interlocking effect describing the grain structural skeleton or
soil cohesion [Lambe and Whitman, 1969].
Figure 2.4:
Generic slope stability analysis scheme, including the most prevalent computa-
tional approaches along with essential key parameters, according to the theory provided in
[Lambe and Whitman, 1969] and [Turner and Schuster, 1996].
Being a wide topic of research, the stability assessment can be conducted following distinct
methodologies. Nevertheless, the most prevalent computational approaches can be considered
infinite slope method and the method of slices, well documented in the existing technical literature
11
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
[Lambe and Whitman, 1969], [Turner and Schuster, 1996]. The selection of the most suitable cal-
culation path should be considered based on individual project description and engineering judgement.
It should be mention that each computational approach implies boundary conditions or numerical lim-
itations, thereafter an appropriate acceptable tolerance should be set for the evaluation process. The
aim of the stability analyses is usually represented by the safety factor determination, value defined as
the ratio between the available shear strength of the material and the shear strength required for equi-
librium. The formulation of the safety factor is shown in Equation 2.1 [Lambe and Whitman, 1969].
It should be observed from Equation 2.1 that a safety factor value of 1 emphasizes the stability
threshold, with any result below this limit indicating the system failure, while a superior number
suggests a safe context. The mathematical approach previously described should be understood
from a theoretical point of view, because the soil cluster can be subjected to spatial variability of its
geotechnical characteristics, leading to the necessity of different safety thresholds to be established
according to the designing requirements.
F = П„c
П„ =
shear strength available
shear stress required for equilibrium
(2.1)
2.3.1
Infinite slope method
According to [Turner and Schuster, 1996], the mechanical principle behind the infinite slope method
can be described by a soil with a defined thickness moving downward a sliding surface parallel to
the natural slope surface, having a specific inclination superior to the friction angle of the material.
Furthermore, the slope length on the horizontal direction is considered to be infinite, simplifying the
force equilibrium determination. Due to its lack of complexity, infinite slope theory can be applied
for a countless number of engineering challenges regarding slope stability assessment, nevertheless
the limitations implied by this approach should be considered for each individual project case.
Figure 2.5: Generic cross section of the slope profile in the infinite slope theory, with the representation
of the elementary mass unit, based on the data from [Turner and Schuster, 1996].
Figure 2.5 illustrates the graphical interpretation of this computational method, expressing the main
features based on the data presented in [Turner and Schuster, 1996]. Thereafter, d represents the
vertical distance between the sliding surface and the ground level [m], Оё indicates the slope angle
of the inclined plane [в—Ӣ], t symbolizes the thickness of the top layer above the sliding plane [m],
Оҙx and Оҙy stands for the infinitesimal dimensions of the soil element. The reference system shown
12
==PAGE:32==

suggests the normal direction on the inclined plane. Considering that a geometrical relationship can
be established between the vertical distance on the slope and the upper soil layer thickness, it can be
seen in Equation 2.2 and this formulation will be encountered in the following definitions.

In order to apply the mechanical equilibrium over the infinite slope illustrated in Figure 2.5 a
unit element should be considered, with the dimensions described by the infinitesimal lengths Оҙx
and Оҙy. Consequently, the balance conditions can be expressed as ОЈFx = 0 on the longitudinal
direction, ОЈFy = 0 on the vertical direction, respectively ОЈM = 0 for the rotational bending moment
[Turner and Schuster, 1996]. Moreover, the bending moment condition should be specifically set in
one of the corners delimiting the elementary unit.
The force field, along with the stress field can be detailed according to the theory presented in
[Lambe and Whitman, 1969]. Hence, the explicit distribution of the forces and stresses acting on
the elementary unit mass can be seen in Figure 2.6. As it is shown in Figure 2.6, Пғxy and Пғyx stand
for the shear stress propagated at the edges of the element [N/m2], Пғxx and Пғyy represent the normal
stresses [N/m2], Оі is the unit weigh associated with the elementary unit mass [N/m3], Txy and
Tyx symbolize the shear force related to the element boundaries [N], Nxx and Nyy are the normal
forces perpendicular on the element [N], W illustrates the volumetric unit weight for the studied
component [N] [Lambe and Whitman, 1969]. Furthermore, the use of the prime symbol for some of
the stresses and forces represented in Figure 2.6 signify the opposite direction of application when
they are compared to the original ones, due to the stress dependency with depth for in-situ analysis.

for an elementary unit mass inside the slope profile, according to the information provided in
[Lambe and Whitman, 1969].

As it can be seen in the above presented equations, no specific material properties were included,
thereafter, in the general formulation of the stress field the contribution of the soil geotechnical charac-
teristics is omitted. The material mechanical component is dependent on the assumed design scenario,
mainly connected to the water flow in the subsurface environment [Turner and Schuster, 1996]. As it
can be seen in Figure 2.4, [Turner and Schuster, 1996] proposed three distinct cases for the analysis,
denoted seepage parallel to the slope inclination model, steady submerged soil model, dry soil model.
For each pattern, the most relevant information linked to the stability assessment can be summarized
by the safety factor determination, as it was formulated in Equation 2.1, and the identification of the
critical thickness of the soil layer prior to the system collapse.
When the water flow is possible though the soil mass, the pore water pressure varies along the
drainage path affecting the structural integrity of the material composing the slope. Moreover, the
seepage may be responsible of internal erosion or displacement of soil particles, reducing the shear
strength of the soil layer. Based on the information provided in [Lambe and Whitman, 1969], the

2.3.2
Method of slices
Method of slices represents an alternative solution for evaluating the stability condition of a slope
structure, when the geometrical boundaries of the system are well defined, with relatively similar
dimensions on the vertical and horizontal direction. Comparing to the infinite slope method, where
the determined value should be exact, this approach implies the computation of an approximate
solution for the stability problem [Lambe and Whitman, 1969]. A significant assumption considered
for this theory can be connected to the expected shape for the slip surface, allowing for a specific
discretization in a number of slices for the possible mobilized soil mass. As it can be seen in Figure
2.7, the slip surface is predicted to expand as a segment part of a circular cylinder, while the number
of slices chosen for the simulation begins as an aleatory number and it should be adjusted along
with the safety factor convergence, in order to validate the outcome [Lambe and Whitman, 1969].
16
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
The optimal configuration for the designed slope can be obtained when the model convergence is
considered satisfactory or through repetitive simulations. A generic scheme describing the method of
slices implementation can be observed in Figure 2.7.
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.7: Theoretical representation of the methodology applied in the method of slices approach
(a) Slip surface visualization along with the segment distribution within the possible detaching circular
area (b) Stress field illustration for an individual slice inside the system, based on the data included
in [Lambe and Whitman, 1969].
An essential component of the safety evaluation should be the identification of the available shear
strength for the slope constitutive material. As the system is divided into individual segments along the
sliding plane, as it is shown in Figure 2.7a, the shear capacity can be estimated for each part, generating
a comprehensive image about the mechanical vulnerability of the system [Lambe and Whitman, 1969].
The shear stress computation for each slide is achievable by combining the Terzaghi principle of
effective stresses with the Mohr-Coulomb criterion, as indicated in [Lambe and Whitman, 1969].
Thereafter, the formulation of the shear stress can be seen in Equation 2.23, with П„i as the shear
stress for each distinct element [kN/m2], cu standing for the undrained cohesion of the studied
material [kN/m2], Пғ representing the total normal stress acting on the slice [kN/m2], u meaning the
pore pressure level registered in the soil [kN/m2], П•u symbolizing the saturated friction angle of the
soil [в—Ӣ].

Equation 2.28 indicates the general approach, defining the safety analysis within this calculation
method. Nevertheless, two distinct perspectives can be derived from the generic expression, Fellenius
theory and Modified-Bishop theory [Lambe and Whitman, 1969]. Both of these theories follow a
similar calculation path, except for the description attributed to the normal force Ni.
Fellenius method relies on a simplified consideration of the normal force into the safety factor
formula, including only the effect produced by the unit weight of each soil segment. Therefore,
the horizontal forces adjacent on the generated slices are neglected, being assumed perpendicular
on the direction of application of the unit weight, according to [Whitman and Bailey, 1967]. This
approach may lead to computational errors, as the longitudinal loads acting along the slope profile
can be subjected to different inclinations, depending on the location of the studied point. In this
context, the accepted tolerances should be precisely defined. Based on the information included in
[Whitman and Bailey, 1967], the normal force evaluated in Fellenius method is shown in Equation

==PAGE:38==

2.29, where Ni stands for the normal force acting on an individual slice [kN], Wi represents the
unit weight concentrated force characterizing the soil segment self-weight [kN], П‘i describes the
inclination angle formed between the soil weight load and the vertical direction [в—Ӣ].
Ni = Wi вӢ…cos(П‘i)
(2.29)
As it was previously mentioned, the lateral forces acting on the assessed individual components,
denoted with the symbol Ti in Figure 2.7b, should be included in the stability evaluation due to their
considerable influence. However, even with this limitation, Fellenius approach can represent a valuable
reference tool to validate the output results obtained from a software computation. Modified-Bishop
method enhanced the complexity of the normal force expression by incorporating the contribution
attributed to the inclined shear loads over the safety factor determination. As it can be seen in
[Lambe and Whitman, 1969], the unit weight vector can be described as the summation of the
vertical force vector and the longitudinal force vector, translated to the normal direction. Hence, the
formulation can be observed in Equation 2.30, with Ti as the shear force adjacent to the evaluated
slice [kN].
Wi = Ni вӢ…cos(П‘i) + Ti вӢ…sin(П‘i)
(2.30)
Figure 2.7b illustrates the unfavorable effect induced by the pore pressure, being responsible for
the reduction of the stabilizing weight load. Consequently, in the Modified-Bishop method the
pore pressure resultant is expressed as suggested in Equation 2.31 [Lambe and Whitman, 1969]. In
Equation 2.31, Ui symbolizes the resulted force connected to the pore pressure effect [kN], Оҙxi is the
horizontal length of the studied soil segment [m], b represents the elementary width measured on the
orthogonal direction compared to the strain plane [m], ui indicates the pore pressure level distributed
along the entire slice element area [kN/m2], П‘i describes the inclination angle formed between the
soil weight load and the vertical direction [в—Ӣ].
Ui = ui вӢ…b вӢ…Оҙxi
cos(П‘i)
(2.31)
For the determination of the safety factor both normal force and shear force should be described
accordingly. Hence, starting from the Equation 2.1 the mobilized shear force can be presented as
the ratio between the available shear strength connected to the constitutive material and the safety
factor of the system. The total normal force may be expressed with consideration of the pore pressure
influence, thereafter the formula implies the extraction of the pore water pressure load from the
existing normal force parallel to the vertical direction. Equation 2.32 includes the detailed expressions
of the total normal force Nt.i and the mobilized shear load subjecting the soil mass Ti, based on the
data from [Lambe and Whitman, 1969]. All the presented parameters have been already discussed
in the paragraphs above. Finally, extrapolating the definitions provided in Equation 2.32, the safety
factor required for the stability assessment in Modified-Bishop theory is illustrated in Eqaution 2.33,
according to the indications in [Lambe and Whitman, 1969].

2.4
Freezing/thawing transition impact over slope stability
The transition between the freezing and thawing seasons can have a significant influence over the
slope stability assessment, especially for the situation when a long term analysis is targeted. For the
19
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
areas affected by these recursive cycles, the integration of the soil mechanical variation, induced
by the cyclical water phase shifting in the soil mass, should be strongly considered, as the slope
integrity can be disturbed during its lifespan. According to [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], some
soil types are more susceptible to frost action than others, depending on the granulometry of the
material and the chemical composition. The presence of a frost susceptible soil layer, subjected
to a specific inclination close to the ground surface, can lead to a gradual downward movement
in time, process known as solifluction [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. Moreover, regarding this
context, considerable amounts of heat can be transported through the soil mass, particularly in
the transition periods, disturbing the heat balance along the slope profile and elevating the risk of
permafrost degradation [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. The freezing/thawing cycle impact over
the safety factor determination can be connected to the following key elements as underground water
flow, soil mechanical properties, soil moisture content.
The regional hydrology of a slope system should be monitored and interpreted, in order to generate
a realistic simulation model for a safety analysis. The water infiltration from the ground surface,
process known as percolation, can be responsible for the increase in pore water pressure or for the
oversaturation of the top soil [Tsai et al., 1999]. The common inflow water sources in a slope may be
connected to precipitations or snow melting during the thawing season. In principle, the underground
water flow can be described by a series of components such as water infiltration level through
the ground surface, hydraulic properties characterizing the constitutive material, geomorphological
properties describing the slope system, ground water table fluctuation, slope inclination angle.
Regarding the geomorphological stratification of the soil column, [Tsai et al., 1999] observed that
a specific succession of layers may cause a significant destabilization of the slope over prolonged
time spans. Particularly for the case of a high permeable material overlaying a clayey or silty deposit,
with considerably reduced permeability values. This context can facilitate the development of sliding
surfaces at the upper part of the relatively impermeable layer, as it can be seen in Figure 2.8a,
by a continuous exposure to the water dynamic action on the drainage path. A similar behavior
was documented by [Terlien, 1998], for the transition linking the thawing period to the freezing
season. When the upper part of the soil begins to melt, the thawed region can be exposed to
oversaturation conditions, decreasing the structural strength capacity for that area. Combining the
strength reduction with the intensified detachment conditions created by the overlaying of a thawed
layer over a rigid frozen surface, the scenario may indicate a significant contribution to the landslide
susceptibility increase. The same analogy should be considered for the beginning of the freezing
period, with the soil column freezing from upside down [Terlien, 1998]. With a rigid frozen layer
laying on a thawed soil mass, the stability equilibrium should be investigated.
Figure 2.8a illustrates a typical unfavorable context with respect to the stability assessment formulation,
as the water is flowing through the permeable topsoil layer, leading to a gradual degradation of the
interface delimitating the two geological formations [Terlien, 1998]. As the erosion occurs along the
separation surface and the shear strength is subjected to a steady decrease in time, the destabilization
effects can become more pronounced until the critical threshold is reached. The previously described
methodology was particularly focused on soil dominated slopes, hence, the rock slopes should be
presented as well. The mechanical principles defining the slope system are similar, nevertheless the
hydrological impact over the general stability should be perceived slightly distinct. Based on the data
provided in [Chen et al., 2022], depending on the studied season, the existing free water inside the
rock discontinuities can manifest different conducts. When the water is in the frozen state it acts as
a plaster, assuring a rigid structural bonding inside the rock, improving the strength characteristics
within the freezing season, while in the thawing period the water movement is dictated by gravity,
causing erosion of the preexisting cracks and weakness zones, deepening the material inhomogeneity
[Chen et al., 2022]. Therefore, a long exposure of the rock mass to the freezing and thawing cycles
can cause a steady deterioration, resulting in the initiation of failure mechanisms in the form of rock
avalanche, toppling, rock fall, wedge failure [Chen et al., 2022].
20
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
Previous studies were documenting the influence of the freezing/thawing cycles upon the soil
mechanical parameters [Tsai et al., 1999], [Guo et al., 2022], indicating that consistent fluctuations
can be registered. The observed general pattern tends to suggest a diminish in the soil bearing capacity
when the material shift its state from frozen phase to thawed phase. According to [Tsai et al., 1999],
the investigations indicate that the soil cohesion may be diminished up to three times when the
transition occurs, based on laboratory testing with samples maintained at temperatures down to
-10в—ӢC, which were allowed to melt to a room temperature close to 20в—ӢC. Within the same experiments,
the internal friction angle was monitored, leading to a reduction close to 15% in the most unfavorable
conditions. The above-mentioned results can emphasize the significant effect controlled by the soil
phase over the stability conditions in a slope system. It should be highlighted that the soil cohesion is
subjected to a more pronounced variation comparing to the internal friction angle [Tsai et al., 1999].
Furthermore, considering the Mohr-Coulomb formulation for the shear strength as a function of
cohesion and internal friction angle, by decreasing the amplitude of these geotechnical parameters
the shear strength of the material will be reduced in consequence. Based on the experimental work
described in [Guo et al., 2022], the convergence towards the expected final values for the mechanical
properties internal friction angle, soil cohesion, residual deformation can be achieved within three to
seven repeated cycles of freezing and thawing, followed by a stabilization of the soil sample behavior.
Considering the melting season, the interface between the thawed soil and the frozen medium can
pose a vulnerable area regarding the structural integrity of the slope, as it is illustrated in Figure
2.8b. The connection between the material detachment risk with the hydrological flow pattern and
the inhomogeneous characteristic of the underground spectrum may generate a significant drop in
the safety factor computation. Nevertheless, the context shown in Figure 2.8b is particularly relevant
when the top part of the soil column is composed of frost susceptible material, characterized by
specific granulometric particle distributions, overlaying continuous permafrost surfaces or theoretically
impermeable layers such as clay deposits. If the upper layer consists of a dominant amount of flint or
clay, it tends to behave as a watertight barrier blocking the possible percolation from the ground
surface. However, the saturation can occur from below, as it can be seen in Figure 2.8a, being driven
by water table uplifting or infiltration sources located at more elevated points along the slope profile.
This scenario can also induce stability problems, by the mobilization of the impermeable layer in the
form of solifluction.
The connection between the recursive freezing/thawing cycles and soil moisture content should be
investigated for the purpose of the stability analysis, particularly because the pore water can have a
negative influence over the structural context. As it was discussed in [Guo et al., 2022], the existing
free water inside the ground may be susceptible to freeze and create ice crystals within the material
structure, depleting the contact surface between the soil particles together with the interlocking
effect. Therefore, a soil with a high moisture content can be more vulnerable to the phase change
process, involving detrimental effects over the stability assessment, while a low value for the water
content indicates a more predictable approach in a long-term analysis. It should be emphasized
that the negative connotation is attributed to the free water infiltrated in the soil texture, while the
chemically bond water becomes part of the structure lattice, resisting to the phase shift phenomenon
and preventing the extension or formation of structural cracks [Guo et al., 2022]. Especially in the
heavily exposed thawing/freezing environments, it should be desired to identify an optimal moisture
content for the analyzed soil, with the aim of increasing the level of confidence for the prediction
process regarding the mechanical properties of the soil. [Chen et al., 2022] suggests that the optimal
moisture content can be evaluated based on field measurements along with laboratory testing, data
which contributes to the calibration of the stability assessment simulation.
