==PAGE:41==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.8: Schematic representation of possible hydrological consequences related to the freez-
ing/thawing cycle continuity over extensive temporal intervals, emphasizing the stability assessment
concerns (a) Gradual destabilization of the top soil layer, as a result of soil over-saturation, phe-
nomenon induced by water percolation or groundwater table ascension according to the information
presented in [Terlien, 1998]; (b) Shallow landslide mechanism development, associated with thaw
front advancement, in regions with a frost susceptible upper soil layer, based on the discussion in
[Tsai et al., 1999].
22
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 2. Landslides - conceptualization
Water physical characteristics should be carefully evaluated for the safety computational purposes,
taking into account that the variation from the normal values may impact the simulation conduct.
[Tsai et al., 1999] discusses about the water PH index, because in the standard analysis the neutral
value is frequently chosen. In the case of an acidic or basic water, further chemical reactions can
develop inside the soil mass, altering the geomorphological characteristics defining the slope structure.
This context may be responsible of strength reductions or increased spatial variabilities along the slope
profile. Another significant aspect should be the salinity level considered in the model. According to
[Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], a high salinity concentration can be associated with the variation of
the freezing point inside the soil mass, together with the alteration of the thermal properties of the
considered material, particularly applicable for heat conductivity and heat capacity. Once more the
anisotropy of the subsurface medium should be assessed in order to ensure acceptable tolerances for
the simulation. In the cold environments, the estimation of the unfrozen water content can signify an
additional engineering challenge to overcome. [Cui and Qin, 2022] indicates that the ratio between
ice and water in the soil seems to be difficult to evaluate during field investigations, and even the
results obtained from laboratory testing may present a certain degree of limitation. Nevertheless, by
establishing an acceptable range of values for the unfrozen water content the contribution to the
slope stability assessment should be beneficial.
23
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 3
Regional background surrounding
Longyearbyen
A regional stability assessment requires a wide variety of observations and measurements in order
to allow a satisfactory output, therefore, for the purpose of the present analysis, the available
geological and climatic data were collected. Furthermore, a series of boreholes were drilled along the
Longyeardalen, providing valuable geotechnical, hydrological, and thermal insight information about
the region, however the discussions about the conducted boreholes will be elaborated in the next
chapter.
The geological maps represent a suitable input for a regional analysis, as they are generated with the
aim of covering comprehensive areas, in contrast with the spatially restricted climate data collected
from stationary meteorological stations. Nevertheless, even the generated geological maps can
be subjected to a certain degree of uncertainty due to the spatial variability of the studied area.
Consequently, it should be mentioned that the regional models tend to be usually generated based
on data interpolation and engineering assumptions. It is essential to precisely specify the limitations
of the designed scenario, indicating the accepted tolerance used. Along the current research, the
computational constrains will be individually illustrated in the methodology description and results
discussion.
3.1
Geological context
According to [Elvevold et al., 2007], Svalbard archipelago includes a significant geological diversity
for a relatively reduced land area, preserving and displaying the geological processes occurred in
the region at a high quality. The geological records, discussed in [Elvevold et al., 2007], propose a
specific classification of the existing geomorphological stratification encountered in Svalbard, following
a chronological timeline. The basement, composed of the oldest type of structured material between
the Precambrian period to Silurian, appears to be represented by an accumulation of metamorphic
rocks, along with igneous rocks. It seems to be the result generated by a series of geological
processes, dominated by rock alteration and folding. Beginning with the late Paleozoic until Cenozoic
era, unaltered sedimentary rocks were formed in the region, being characterized by through shape
structures with sharp edges and steep topographic features. The third type of formation can be
denoted unconsolidated surficial deposits, emerging within the Quaternary epoch. Most of the
materials composing the deposits appear to be settled after the last ice age, consisting of glacier
moraines, talus, fluvial and beach transported particles, see [Elvevold et al., 2007].
Being located on the north-east corner of the Eurasian plate, the sedimentary succession observed
in Svalbard seems to be a relative rarity, especially when referred to the up-north position of the
archipelago [Elvevold et al., 2007]. The strong link with the Caledonian Orogeny, dated approximately
24
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 3. Regional background surrounding Longyearbyen
400 million years in the past, can explain the considerable quantities of gravel, mud, sand deposited
in the alluvial plains and on the seabed. Furthermore, the depositional phase occurring during the
Carboniferous and Permian contributed to the limestone layering succession, while in the Mesozoic
and Tertiary a sandstone-shale succession could be documented [Elvevold et al., 2007]. In principle,
the continuous deposition of gravel, mud, sand, lime along extensive time intervals and under the
influence of pressure and thermal gradients facilitated the formation of stratified sedimentary rocks.
Figure 3.1 illustrates the geological distribution over the entire region, with some locations where the
data could not be processed due to the lack of field investigations or glaciers presence. It can be
seen in Figure 3.1 that Longyearbyen is situated at the edge between Tertiary formations and the
Jurassic and Cretaceous morphology.
Figure 3.1: Geological map comprehending a stratigraphic distribution of the formation encountered
around Svalbard archipelago, with respect to the time frame defining the genesis, reproduced from
[Elvevold et al., 2007].
Based on the information from [Elvevold et al., 2007], the geology characterizing both sides of
the Adventfjorden bedrock can be described by the Cretaceous and Tertiary sandstones, regularly
presenting a horizontal layer stratification. From a stability analysis perspective, the previously
mentioned feature may pose a challenge for the structural integrity of the slopes in the area, as
sandstones tend to develop an inferior mechanical strength than igneous rocks or metamorphic
rocks. Due to the abundance of sandstones and shales accumulated in the proximity of Longyerdalen,
the entire zone was included in the Central Tertiary Basin [Elvevold et al., 2007]. As it can be
25
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 3. Regional background surrounding Longyearbyen
seen in Appendix A.1, a detailed geological map is provided for Longyerdalen and the adjacent
surroundings [Rubensdotter, 2022]. According to [Rubensdotter, 2022], it can be observed that
on the fjord shore marine beach deposits cover most of the area, together with tidal deposits,
fluvial deposits and anthropogenic fill mass. Along the riverbed, glaciofluvial deposits appear to
be prevalent, with some alternations of anthropogenically disturbed sediments. At the bottom of
the slopes solifluction material seems to be dominant, along with the debris flow deposits, rock fall
deposits or snow avalanche deposits. As it was discussed in the previous paragraphs, the topography
surrounding the valley is defined by steep slopes, visible bedrock outcrops on the upper part and
erosional channels between the sandstone outcrops. Within these channels, the material is mobilized
downwards gravitationally, forming debris flow or rock fall deposits. On the superior side of the
slopes, a transition can be observed from the bedrock outcrop to the relatively flat plateaus. On the
plateaus, [Rubensdotter, 2022] suggests that the geology condition can be described by weathering
material, being a product of a continuous climate exposure. On the south side of the valley, marginal
moraines can be identified as well as till deposits, marking the delimitation between Longyeardalen
and the active glaciers located southward. Summarizing the topographical context of the region, as
it is illustrated in Appendix A.1, Longyeardalen is bordered by the Adventfjorden on the north side,
Sverdruphamaren and NГҰssfjellet in the west, Sukkertoppen and Gruvefjellet eastwards, Sarkofagen,
together with the active glaciers Longyearbreen and Larsbreen representing the south boundary.
3.2
Climate and meteorological data
SoilвҖҷs geotechnical and geological related parameters can be considerably influenced by the exposure to
the external environmental conditions, hence the climate trends should be monitored and incorporated
in the regional stability assessments, especially when the long-term analysis is targeted. Nevertheless,
according to [Choi, 2004], extensive datasets containing continuous meteorological information should
be available in order to assure a satisfactory correlation between the real behavior of the system and
the predictions generated by the simulating model. Moreover, [Gassner et al., 2015] documented
the possibility of enhancing the stability computational performance of a simulation, when climate
local models, abbreviated as CLM, can be integrated as an initial input. The engineering challenge
encountered in the common designing practice consist of the lack of such models or prolonged
meteorological observations, preventing the stability investigation of access to valuable regional data.
The concept of climate impact can be perceived as a broad terminology, comprehending multiple
factors of influence which can be discussed. Thereafter, [Gassner et al., 2015] emphasized several
stability related factors which can be responsible of altering the mechanical behavior of a slope
such as extreme events frequency, temperature variations, precipitation level in terms of storms or
rainfalls. By documenting the meteorological background in the area of study, along with the landslide
susceptibility maps, connections can be identified for the existing climate trends and predictions can
be produced based on the future scenarios, as it was observed in [Working-Group-I, 2013]. According
to [Working-Group-I, 2013], a reasonable amount of climate trend frameworks is widely available,
based on the human activity emission levels, ranging from a current amount of emissions denoted
with the scenario RCP4.5, to a more unfavorable human impact over the climate in the following
century, assigned with the notation RCP8.5.
As it was mentioned in [Patton et al., 2019], the characteristic temperature fluctuation describing the
arctic regions appears to have a more accelerated trend comparing to other locations around the globe.
The estimated discrepancy indicates a value close to 2-3 times more pronounced, process denoted
as arctic amplification [Patton et al., 2019]. The rapid temperature variation can be responsible of
inducing cracks in the upper soil layer due to the water evaporation, especially during the summer
season, while the thermal induced geomorphological alterations of the soil properties can perturb
slopes structural integrity. Moreover, with the top-soil cluster described by dry conditions, an intensive
26
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 3. Regional background surrounding Longyearbyen
precipitation wave can trigger debris flows in the form of surface layer detachment from the slopes,
or, during prolonged time intervals, it can generate solifluction patterns [Patton et al., 2019].
Apart of the temperature variation, precipitation inflow can pose a valuable predictor connected to the
landslide susceptibility. As the precipitation level amplifies, additional pore pressure can be induced in
steep terrains, contributing to the destabilization of the slope profiles. Furthermore, the oversaturation
of the vulnerable top-soil can represent a different source of soil mass mobilization. Based on
[Spizzichino et al., 2014], the percolation flow does not appear to be evenly distributed surrounding
the studied regions, leading to a differential development of stability risk zones. Nevertheless,
taking into account the long-term analysis, a surge in precipitation level can be responsible of a
general landslide susceptibility increase. Unless the appropriate mitigation measures are implemented
to prevent the possible further collapses in the exposed areas, in accordance with the observed
climatic changes, the number of populated zones affected by landslide initialization can escalate
[Spizzichino et al., 2014]. Except of the individual impact attributed to temperature or precipitation
level ascension, the frequency of the extreme events should be also discussed.
According to
[Spizzichino et al., 2014], the registered intensification in the number of extreme events manifested
in the current climate conditions can be extrapolated as a general global climate trend, particularly
applicable in the arctic regions. Even though the annual average values tend to maintain within
relatively constant ranges, the distribution along a year cycle can be highly disproportionate, with
intense rainstorms or thunderstorms more pronounced comparing to the observations of the previous
years [Spizzichino et al., 2014]. Such an event was recorded around Longyeardalen region in 2016,
when a massive rainstorm caused multiple landslides initiations, aspect which is going to be elaborated
in the following section. A similar conclusion can be formulated regarding the temperature factor, as
minimum record values can be registered during the winter season, while, in the same year, maximum
temperature milestones can be observed on the summer period.
In the area surrounding Longyearbyen, several meteorological stations are strategically located in
order to measure the climate parameters in the region, as it is shown in Figure 3.2. A detailed
description for each individual station is provided by [MET, 1976], information included in Table 3.1
for a better visualization. As it can be seen in Figure 3.2, the most favorable station to extract the
information for Longyeardalen should be SN99855, situated on the valley bottom. Nevertheless, the
datasets available on this station seem to be limited from a temporal perspective, as the instruments
were activated in September 2019 [MET, 1976]. This aspect may prevent the data processing of
generating comprehensive climate trends, required for a long-term assessment. The same observation
can apply to the stations SN99867, SN99870, respectively SN99843, all of them being installed
relatively recently. Moreover, the meteorological condition registered on the plateaus, at 464m,
respectively 450m above the sea level, see Table 3.1, may be distinct when referenced to the measured
environment inside Longyeardalen. Hence, the most suitable selections should be the meteorological
station in the airport proximity SN99840, as it is illustrated in Figure 3.2. The relevant data
collections, connected to the stability assessment process, should contain temperature, precipitation,
snow depth.

studied region is available on the Norwegian Polar Institute database.
Longyeardalen region can be described by a continuous permafrost distribution, particularly along the
valley bottom. Regarding the adjacent slopes, at the lower part the permafrost seems to be frequently
present, while on the upper side the debris flow material lays on sedimentary rocks, represented by
sandstones. According to [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], permafrost or perennially frozen ground
can be defined as subsurface material, either soil or rock, which temperature is maintained below
freezing point all year around. Furthermore, the superior part of the soil column which temperature
fluctuates below and above freezing point, usually described as 0в—ӢC, can be denoted as active layer
thickness [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. Thereafter, understanding the thermal ambient associated
with the area of study may represent a priority, leading to the development of a reliable stability
model of the terrain.
Figure 3.3a offers a good overview of the annual air temperature variation, describing the previous
decades. As it can be observed, the maximum annual air temperature appears to have a relatively
constant distribution, with regular increases and decreases along the period of measurement. Moreover,
it appears that the variation oscillates around the value of 15в—ӢC suggesting a mild warm season.
However, some extremes can be seen in Figure 3.3a, as it may be noticed for the year 1979 with a
maximum temperature of 21.3в—ӢC, and even more recent in 2020, recording a maximum value of air
temperature close to 21.7в—ӢC. The elevation of the maximum air temperature during the summer period
can lead to significant repercussions over the stability equilibrium, as the thaw penetration deepens,
hence allowing for a thicker thawed layer of top soil being exposed to gravitational mobilization,
especially in combination with the water inflow caused by precipitations. An accession trend can
be distinguished for the measured minimum air temperature and mean air temperature, as it is
shown in Figure 3.3a, suggesting a concerning warming pattern, in accordance with the climate
change challenges discussed in [Working-Group-I, 2013]. It should be mentioned that for both data
28
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 3. Regional background surrounding Longyearbyen
series the increase does not seem to be continuous, as some fluctuations can be discerned along
the time interval. Nevertheless, for the minimum air temperature the gap between the amplitude
registered in 1976, approximately -36в—ӢC, compared to the -24в—ӢC measured in 2021, can be considered
quite consistent. The concern regarding the temperature escalation in the freezing season can be
connected to the active layer condition in the steep terrains. As it was discussed, the frozen soil
properties manifest a more favorable mechanical behavior than the thawed material, therefore as
the freezing/thawing cycles diminish in intensity, the landslide susceptibility risk may be enlarged
proportionally.
The monthly mean air temperature is represented in Figure 3.3b, indicating the seasonal transition in
the area surrounding Longyearbyen. As it can be observed in the diagram, the warmest month seems
to be July, followed by August, a general context emphasized in the last 10 years. Furthermore, it
should be mentioned that from June until September the temperatures appear to be above 0в—ӢC,
facilitating the development of the active layer around the valley. Consequently, the maximum
vulnerability of the soil masses can be expected in the transition intervals, more precisely in May or
September, depending as well on the precipitation level describing the year of study and the snow
depth insulating the ground surface.

(b)
Figure 3.3: Registered air temperature dataset available at the meteorological station SN99840, see

minimum annual air temperature distribution, values recorded between 1976-2021; (b) Monthly mean
air temperature describing the last 10 years of measurements.
In principle, Svalbard archipelago can be described by a relatively dry environment, with a reduced
amount of precipitations, as it can be interpreted from Figure 3.4a. It can be observed that during
the last decades, apart of some exceptions, the general trend indicates a net inflow of around 200
mm/year or less, detail particularly applicable before 2012. Nevertheless, a distinct precipitation
tendency may be distinguished for the last 11 years, with a considerable amplification of the quantity
of meteoric water reaching the ground surface. With 5 distinct years recording 250 mm/year
or above, the recent direction can have important implications on the stability context for the
slopes surrounding Longyeardalen. As it was previously discussed, the precipitation level can be
correlated with destabilizing effects in means of pore water pressure and oversaturation of the
soil material. Hence, with the elevation of precipitation amplitudes, the landslide susceptibility
can growth accordingly, especially when it is correlated with a relative temperature warming trend
presented in Figure 3.3. The maximum milestone of 310 mm, reached in 2016, was accompanied by
several landslide initialization in the region, emphasizing the impact which can be attributed to the
precipitation inflow.

level, values recorded between 1976-2021; (b) Monthly precipitation level describing the last 10 years
of measurements.
It should be specified that the precipitation level incorporates both the liquid water phase and the
snow fall. According to Figure 3.4b, it can be outlined that some months appear to be richer
regarding the registered quantity of precipitations than the others. Considering the time period
from 2013 to 2023, it can be emphasized that from July until January most of the inflow amount is
consumed, leaving the rest of the cycle with approximately dry conditions. Based on Figure 3.4b,
it can be difficult to establish the wettest month in an year, as the maximum values varies in the
discussed time interval, however it can be mentioned that August, October and November appear to
be consistent in having elevated values for the precipitation level. Thereafter, the most vulnerable
conditions, for the structural integrity of the slope systems, should be encountered in the above
30
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 3. Regional background surrounding Longyearbyen
specified precipitation rich months. Moreover, the parameters describing these unfavorable scenarios
should be included in the slope stability model.
The snow cover may act as an insulation barrier at the ground surface, influencing the thermal
regime development in the area of study. As it can be interpreted from Figure 3.5, the snow layer
characteristic for the Longyearbyen region appears to be usually present between October and May.
This trend seems to be correlated with the melting season described in Figure 3.3b, presenting mainly
temperatures above 0в—ӢC from June until September. It can be complicated to identify the month
with the thickest snow cover, as the information presented in Figure 3.5 indicates a certain level of
variation from one year to the following one, however the illustrated values suggest that in general
April contains consistent amounts of snow. It should be observed that even with a relative reduced
amount of precipitations in the area, the snow cover seems to be well preserved during most of
the year due to the prevalent freezing temperatures. Furthermore, at the beginning of the freezing
season, the snow layer may be responsible of stability concerns, as it can prolong the requested time
for the active layer to sustain the transition between thawed conditions to the frozen state.
Figure 3.5: Maximum snow depth distribution, recorded at the meteorological station SN99840, see
Figure 3.2, representing the time interval 2013-2023.
3.3
Rainfall induced shallow landslide events 2016
An important series of landslides were observed and documented in the surrounding area of
Longyeardalen, during the autumn of 2016 [Christiansen et al., 2016]. This year seemed to be
particularly atypical compared to the previously analyzed ones, with both precipitations and tem-
peratures exceeding the normal averages recorded for the studied time interval.
According to
[Christiansen et al., 2016], the air temperatures registered between June and October 2016 appeared
to be more elevated than the monthly averages measured in the last three decades, presenting a
neglectable number of days when the temperatures dropped below the freezing point of 0в—ӢC. Moreover,
the values presented in [Christiansen et al., 2016] indicate discrepancies close to 3в—ӢC for the months
of July and September, facilitating a prolonged preservation and expansion of the active layer thickness
in the area. Regarding the precipitation level, [Christiansen et al., 2016] observed a similar pattern
as the one applied for air temperature, contrasting the measured monthly precipitation averages with
the recorded values in the past thirty years. For the temporal range between June and October the
31
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 3. Regional background surrounding Longyearbyen
highest noticeable difference was measured in July, with a value of 51.7 mm of precipitation in 2016
referenced to 18.0 mm between 1986-2015 [Christiansen et al., 2016]. Considering the moist rich
and warm environment developed in the region in the period preceding the month of October, it
should be emphasized that the active layer thickness was estimated close to its peak amplitude and
well supplied with moisture, assuring a steady degree of saturation in the soil.
According to [Christiansen et al., 2016] the forecasted precipitation level for the period 14-15 of
October 2016 was approximately 20-25 mm, a significant inflow taking into account the relative dry
environment characterizing Svalbard archipelago. The common trend in the area of Longyeardalen
can be described by downward freezing initiation before the middle of October, nevertheless with
the increased temperatures monitored during the summer season the freezing process was delayed,
resulting in a vulnerable topsoil layer susceptible to destabilization [Christiansen et al., 2016]. The
recorded precipitation levels indicated around 21 mm on the upper side of Longyeardalen, 18 mm at
the meteorological station located in the proximity of the airport, while nearby the fjord shore a value
of 14 mm was measured [Christiansen et al., 2016]. It can be distinguished that the rainfall does
not seem to be evenly distributed in the documented locations, implying a spatial variability of the
data with a powerful impact on the risk assessment procedure. Based on [Christiansen et al., 2016],
the maximum precipitation intensity recorded during the rainstorm event was approximately 2.8
mm/hour.
Following the rainstorm event, multiple slope failures were observed in the region as it can be seen
in Figure 3.6. Most of the mass destabilizations could be classified as debris flows, active layer
detachments or slumps [Christiansen et al., 2016], inflicting damage to the local infrastructure. It
should be mentioned that due to the previous meteorological conditions in the area the thaw front
depth was estimated at around 1-1.5m, when the intensive precipitation event occurred, for the
majority of the slopes adjacent to the valleys included in Figure 3.6 [Christiansen et al., 2016]. One of
the massive slides, located on the left side of Longyeardalen, was measured and analyzed by a team of
researchers from UNIS. According to [Christiansen et al., 2016], an approximated volume of 5000m3
was mobilized from the slope downwards, blocking one of the access local roads in Longyearbyen.
The estimated area affected by this collapse was evaluated to be close to 23000m2, implying
considerable financial efforts in order to reestablish the proper functionality of the transportation way.
Hence, the previously described point emphasized the necessity of developing reliable risk monitoring
systems, aiming for reducing monetary losses and increasing the safety level for the inhabitants.
[Christiansen et al., 2016] indicates that the deposition accumulated in the proximity of the slope
bottom consisted of almost 5m of displaced material, while along the slope profile the eroded areas
could be estimated at 2-3 m in thickness. No fatalities were recorded during the discussed extreme
event, however specific actions should be considered in order to prevent similar incidents to generate
further insecurity in the local community.
Along the same autumn in 2016, another powerful rainstorm was predicted in the region, as presented
in [Humlum et al., 2016]. According to [Humlum et al., 2016], on 6th November a turbulent warm
and moist airmass was transiting the Greenland Sea, advancing north on the East Greenland coast
towards Svalbard archipelago. The rainstorm, consisting of intense winds and abundant precipitation
levels, was estimated to intersect Longyearbyen area on the 7th of November. Considering the
repercussions induced by the previous extreme event recorded in October [Christiansen et al., 2016],
additional measurement gauges were installed across the town. The recorded values exceeded the
measurements obtained in October, with an accumulated precipitation level close to 75 mm, while the
intensity peak during the storm was estimated at 8.3 mm/hour [Humlum et al., 2016]. Even though
the amount of water inflow appeared to be more consistent than the event in October, the amplitude
of the mass movement initializations was considerably diminished. Based on [Humlum et al., 2016],
at the time of the rainstorm the upper 20-30 cm of the soil column were observed in a frozen state,
preventing the moist percolation into the subsurface medium and limiting the saturation surge of
32
==PAGE:52==
Chapter 3. Regional background surrounding Longyearbyen
the vulnerable topsoil layer. Without a major moist infiltration, the structural integrity of a large
number of slopes was preserved, resulting in a decreased number of shallow landslides around the
region. Nevertheless, at relatively high altitudes, above 500m, the precipitation fell in the form of
snow, accumulating in the erosional channels and at the edges of the mountain plateaus. This aspect
led to a series of snow avalanches at the specified elevations [Humlum et al., 2016].
Figure 3.6: Monitored mass movements on the days following the rainstorm event, 15-16 October
2016. The landslides are mapped with the yellow contour, being manifested in the form of active
layer detachments, debris flows or slumps. Reproduced from [Christiansen et al., 2016].
Consequently, the geomorphological context of the soil stratification can affect the stability behavior
of a slope profile in a significant manner. Referring to the above described extreme events, the
registered patterns seem to be distinct due to the phase shifting of the material composing the
system and the layering succession with depth. Taking into account these components, monitoring
the ground temperature, along with the saturation profiles, can enhance the accuracy of a regional
stability assessment model. The benefits of such approach can be expressed by predicting the failure
scenarios in correlation with possible extreme events in the future.
33
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 4
Field and laboratory measurements
Slope stability assessment requires an extensive variety of field measurements and laboratory in-
vestigations to acquire a high level of reliability. In general, the stability computational models
tend to be quite sensitive to the initial input set for the simulation, therefore the data selected
for the material properties and the boundary conditions of the model should be carefully validated.
Regarding the soil properties, the most significant characteristics can be presented as soil heat
capacity, thermal conductivity, soil grain density, permeability distribution, granulometric curves, soil
cohesion, internal friction angle, porosity, saturated and unsaturated volumetric weights, unfrozen
water content, saturation level. Particularly applied for the arctic regions, the transition between
frozen state to thawed phase should be observed and considered for the simulation, along with the
time dependency of the thaw penetration depth, because the interface between the frozen and thawed
material can become a susceptible sliding plane, as it was discussed in the previous chapters.
Figure 4.1: The complexity associated to a regional assessment, when the area is composed from
distinct topographic features and geomorphological formations, indicating the necessity of considering
the spatial variability of the measured properties within the proposed simulation.
34
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 4. Field and laboratory measurements
The majority of the data available for Longyeardalen area was collected for the PermaMeteoCommunity
project [Tveit, 2022], aiming for a better understanding of the geological and geotechnical context
of the slopes along the valley. Nevertheless, when some essential information was not available
within the current investigation, different sources were consulted to compensate for the missing
data or evaluations were conducted based on formulas provided in the technical literature. It should
be emphasized that the afferent limitations of the model can be connected to the restricted field
investigations, leading to the necessity of proposing alternative engineering assumptions. Furthermore,
most of the analyzed data reflects an individual local context, being unable to fully comprehend the
spatial variability involved in a regional assessment, as it can be seen in Figure 4.1. Hence, based on
the evaluated properties, specific patterns should be extrapolated from singular boreholes data series
or other parameters susceptible to spatial or temporal dependency.
4.1
Available boreholes
Drilling boreholes in the area of interest represents a common method to obtain valuable soil samples
necessary for laboratory testing. Moreover, different types of sensors can be installed in the generated
boreholes with the aim of long-term recording of field information such as temperature, saturation,
differential displacement patterns. For this project, several shallow boreholes were drilled in distinct
locations along Longyeardalen, targeting the slopes existent in the area, as it can be seen in Figure
4.2 [Tveit, 2022]. Nevertheless, due to the technical difficulties associated with the drilling process,
the selected locations provide information of the lower part of the slopes, as core extraction from
more elevated positions was not possible with the available equipment. The obtained data is used to
estimate realistic soil behaviors over the entire region.
Figure 4.2: Location of the drilled boreholes along Longyeardalen, emphasizing that most of the field
data collected represents the soil condition in the lower part of the slope, according to [Tveit, 2022].
Ortofoto map accessed from the Norwegian Polar Institute database.
35
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 4. Field and laboratory measurements
For the existing boreholes, thermal monitoring systems were installed in PMC-SB-03, PMC-SB-04
and PMC-SB-05, see Figure 4.2. Tracking the thermal profile of the soil is of particular interest in
the arctic regions, especially regarding the fluctuation of the active layer thickness during a year
cycle. As it was discussed about the landslide event occurred in 2016 [Christiansen et al., 2016], the
thaw penetration depth can be used as an important indicator of the landslide susceptibility risk, in
combination with unfavorable meteorological conditions. It should be mentioned that no saturation
loggers were yet to be installed in the locations showed in Figure 4.2, nevertheless monitoring systems
are scheduled to be inserted at a further date.
The precise coordinates of the boreholes are provided in Appendix A.2.2 [Tveit, 2022], together with
information about the drilling depth in each location. As the main concern in the area is focusing
on shallow landslide susceptibility, in the form of active layer detachment or debris flow, it can be
observed in Appendix A.2.2 that none of the presented boreholes exceed five meters in depth. The
variation observed in the reached depth can be associated with limitations imposed by the drilling rig
and the local topography. The drilling operation which leaded to the extraction of the soil samples
was conducted in the month of April 2022, between 20th - 29th [Tveit, 2022].
4.2
Geotechnical data
Figure 4.3: Elementary soil properties requested for a stability analysis, emphasizing the main
types of behaviors involved in a simulating scenario, as mechanical, thermal and hydrologi-
cal. The classification is based on [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], [Lambe and Whitman, 1969],
[Subramanian et al., 2020].
36
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 4. Field and laboratory measurements
Geotechnical properties of a soil material describe its structural behavior, when external actions are
applied to the system, thereafter they have to be evaluated with a satisfactory degree of accuracy
in order to provide the stability assessment with consistent input data. As it was discussed in the
paragraphs above, the geotechnical data is usually collected from individual boreholes, an aspect
which can be perceive as a limitation when a regional analysis is targeted due to the spatial variability
characterizing the subsurface medium. The landslide vulnerability of a slope can be commonly
correlated with a set of key parameters defining the system stability during external loading events,
as it is illustrated in Figure 4.3. The majority of the factors shown in Figure 4.3 will be discussed in
the current chapter.
4.2.1
Stratigraphic logs
Classifying the constitutive material of the soil profile by field observations can pose a valuable
indicator of the expected physical properties, particularly when the identification process is correlated
with the laboratory testing. Furthermore, the cryostructure description of the logs can offer important
details regarding the thermal propagation in the ground, along with water percolation patterns,
relevant aspects concerning the arctic environment. In Appendix A.2.1 the stratigraphic logs for
the boreholes PMC-SB-02, PMC-SB-03, PMC-SB-04, PMC-SB-05 can be observed, including the
distribution for both the sediments and the cryostructures encountered in the extracted samples,
according to [Tveit, 2022].
As it can be seen in Figure A.1 and Figure A.2 [Tveit, 2022], diamicton represents the prevalent
sediment in the analyzed logs, especially for PMC-SB-02, PMC-SB-03, PMC-SB-05. As diamicton
can be defined as a non-sorted to poorly sorted sediment, containing a broad range of particle sizes
originated from different sources [Harland et al., 1966], it may offer some information regarding the
geotechnical properties of the subsurface medium, reflecting a possible high porosity and a reduced
water content. Apart of diamicton, a relatively thin layer of muddy sand was encountered in the log
PMC-SB-03, at a depth of approximately 1.60m, as it is shown in Figure A.1b [Tveit, 2022]. The
location of the muddy sand material corresponds with the lenticular ice formation identified at the
same depth, which can be caused by the additional water retained in the proximity of this layer, water
which can be subjected to a specific freezing pattern. Moreover, the lenticular structure may indicate
the boundary of the seasonal thawing zone or the depth reached by the thaw front, a possibility
supported by the shift between the geotechnical properties attributed to diamicton comparing to
muddy sand. For PMC-SB-05, there are two alternation zones between diamicton and the organic
rich mud, at the top of the soil profile and at a depth of around 3,10m, as it is illustrated in Figure
A.2b [Tveit, 2022]. The organic rich mud film observed at the top can have an important impact
over the heat propagation from the surface to the bottom of the soil column, along with a consistent
water absorption tendency. In contrast with the previously discussed boreholes, PMC-SB-04 presents
quite a dispersed stratification below 2m deep, as it can be seen in Figure A.2a. The upper part
of the log appears to be dominated by diamicton sediment, while on the lower side a succession
of gravel and mud layers complete the profile. Furthermore, the correlation between the sediment
composition and the cryostructure formations appears to be well emphasized for this location.
Regarding cryostructure, four distinct formations were identified in Figure A.1 and Figure A.2
as structureless ice, lenticular ice, suspended structure and irregular reticulate ice [Tveit, 2022].
According to [Benkert et al., 2016], lenticular structure is characterized by lens-shaped, generally
continuous and horizontal ice formations in ice-rich sediments; suspended structure can be described
by a consistent volume of ice, up to 90-95% from the admissible volume, where soil aggregates
are suspended in ice; irregular reticulate structure contains a net-like pattern interconnecting the
horizontal ice lenses to the vertical ice veins; while structureless formations comprises the irregularly
distributed ice along the soil column depth, with no specific particularities.
37
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 4. Field and laboratory measurements
Structureless ice can be observed in all the available logs, prevalent in the upper side of the profiles
within 1.50m from the surface. This aspect can be explained by the thickness of the seasonal
frozen ground in the region, allowing for continuous melting and freezing cycles during the years and
consequently preventing the formation of any particular ice structures. Nevertheless, structureless ice
can be distinguished even at deeper levels, as it is shown in Figure A.1b for PMC-SB-03 [Tveit, 2022],
where the structureless development dominates the entire profile. For PMC-SB-02 and PMC-SB-04 a
consistent quantity of sediments appears to be suspended in the ice rich zones, indicating the prospect
of high void ratios for the extracted samples. In Figure A.2a [Tveit, 2022], it can be also observed
that irregular reticulate patterns seem to be formed below the gravel layers, aspect which can be
attributed to the significant permeability describing the gravel material, permitting water percolation
and accumulation at the lower part of the layer. In the case of PMC-SB-05, irregular reticulate
structures appear to be preponderant below 3m deep, while some alternations between suspended
and structureless formations can be observed above this limit. Considering that for PMC-SB-05
the main sediment is represented by diamicton, no specific connections can be emphasized between
sediments and cryostructure distribution. It should be mentioned that the cryostructure information,
provided for interpretation by [Tveit, 2022], includes a satisfactory amount of details as a result of
the selected drilling dates, in April, a freezing month for the region of Longyeardalen.
4.2.2
Water content measurements
Determination of the water content of the available samples represents a necessary input for
the stability assessment, as multiple properties can be derived from this measurement such as
thermal conductivity, heat capacity, unfrozen water content. The values for the water content,
more precise expressed as gravimetric water content, were evaluated according to the Equation 4.1
[Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], where w stands for the gravimetric water content of the analyzed
sample [%], Mw represents the mass of water present either in a frozen or unfrozen state [g], Ms
illustrates the mass of dry soil solids [g].
w = Mw
Ms
вӢ…100 [%]
(4.1)
A complete set of measurements is available for PMC-SB-02, PMC-SB-03, PMC-SB-04, PMC-SB-05
based on the work conducted by [Tveit, 2022]. Thereafter, water content profiles could be generated
for the existing boreholes as illustrated in Figure 4.4. The majority of the studied logs involves a
prevalent diamicton sediment on the upper side, with a relatively coarse constitution, this aspect can
be correlated with the distribution of the gravimetric water content as well, visualizing the first 1.5m
of soil profiles in Figure 4.4. An exception from this behavior is manifested by PMC-SB-05, see Figure
4.4d, as the top 5-10cm are composed of an organic rich mud material, retaining a considerably
amount of water. An additional similar pattern can be emphasized by the existence of water content
value spikes along each individual profile, aspect which can be associated with the observed ice lenses
or suspended cryostructures, as shown in Figure A.1 or Figure A.2 [Tveit, 2022].
PMC-SB-02 and PMC-SB-04 are located on the same side of Longyeardalen, with respect to the
main river in the area, as it can be seen in Figure 4.2. Both boreholes appear to exhibit a close
tendency for the water dispersion along the soil column, as a result of the influence manifested by
the local hydrology system and the runoff pathways created downslope on the right side of the valley.
As it is displayed in Figure 4.4a, water content varies around 37% below 2.40m deep for PMC-SB-02,
while a similar amplitude of 37% can be distinguished for PMC-SB-04 under the depth of 3.40m, see
Figure 4.4c. Nevertheless, due to the complex morphology and distinct cryostructure formations, a
strong numerical correlation cannot be emphasized between the two locations.
For the log PMC-SB-03, more elevated amplitudes describing the water content were registered on
the superior side of the soil column, when compared to the other locations, as can be seen in Figure
38
==PAGE:58==
Chapter 4. Field and laboratory measurements
4.4b. This conduct can be explained by the position of the borehole, being situated on a drainage
path from Sukkertoppen to Adventfjorden, it can be subjected to a consistent water flow through its
profile. The maximum value recorded of 52.17% at approximately 1.65m deep seems to be a perfect
match with the discussed lenticular ice formation in Figure A.1b. In contrast, PMC-SB-05 has a
more elaborated cryostructure distribution, hence the water content fluctuation appears to correlate
accordingly. In Figure 4.4d it can be emphasized that the first 2m of the profile can be described
by a low average value of around 11.90%, while the rest 3m indicates an average close to 28.70%,
excluding the noticeable isolated amplitude of 150%. Summing up the values illustrated in Figure
4.4, it can be concluded that major parts of the constitutive material extracted from Longyeardalen
indicates a coarse constitution of the soil, with a relatively high hydraulic conductivity, as the water
retained in the soil structure appears to be quite reduced. The previously mentioned remark is
particularly applicable for the first 2m of the soil column.

4.2.3
Soil density
In general, soil density can be determined as a function of weighted mass for the analyzed sample and
the estimated volume of the sample. Without advanced scanner equipment, the visual approximation
39
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 4. Field and laboratory measurements
of the volume can be subjected to small deviation from its exact value, nevertheless the errors are
considered acceptable when they are restricted to specific tolerances. Equation 4.2 includes the
explicit form describing soil density [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], where ПҒ stands for the bulk
density of the studied soil [kg/m3], M represents the total mass [kg], V represents the approximated
volume of the sample [m3], Ms illustrates the dry mass of the soil [kg], Mw stands for the mass
of water within the sample structure [kg]. According to [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], a direct
correlation can be established between the bulk density of the soil and the dry density using the
evaluated water content of the sample, as emphasized in Equation 4.2. In Equation 4.2, ПҒd illustrates
the dry density of the soil [kg/m3], w is the water content of the material [%], while the other
parameters have already been discussed.

The evaluation of soil density represents an important part of the stability assessment because it
influences the amplitude of the destabilizing force along the slope, mobilizing a certain percentage of
the soil shear strength. In combination with the pore pressure intensity, developed in the ground due to
the local hydrological system or infiltration events, it can contribute to remanent deformations along
the slope profile. Furthermore, soil density can be utilized for estimation of different geotechnical
parameters when the laboratory measurements are not available, such as thermal conductivity, heat
capacity, porosity, void ratio [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. According to [Tveit, 2022], density
measurements were conducted only for samples from borehole PMC-SB-05, as it is shown in Table
4.1. Due to the coarse and brittle texture of the extracted material, the experiments were taken for a
limited number of samples, five in total, from different depths. The studied samples can be observed
in Figure 4.5. The complete dataset used to determine the values shown in Table 4.1 is provided in
Appendix A.2.3.

the compressing factors in the area seem to be limited, as the material can be sedimented and

4.2.4
Soil porosity and void ratio
Both porosity and void ratio illustrates the volume occupied by pores within soil structure. The
available pore space in the ground can have a significant influence over the heat transport through
the subsurface, as well as a hydrological connotation regarding water flow and the possible degree of
saturation. Moreover, the internal fluid pressure built up inside the pore space may considerably affect
the structural integrity of a slope system. Based on the values presented in Table 4.1, void ratio can
be determined using Equation 4.3, according to [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], with e denoting the
void ratio of the material [-], Vv represents the volume of the voids [m3], Vs stands for the soil solids
volume [m3], ПҒs illustrates the grain density or the density of the solid particles [kg/m3], ПҒd is the dry
density of the soil [kg/m3]. According to [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], the grain density may vary
between 2500-2800 kg/m3. Furthermore, [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004] suggests a value of ПҒs =
2650 kg/m3 for the grain density of soils possessing a sandy texture. Hence, this value was considered
for the void ratio computation. Porosity is connected to the void ratio, as shown in Equation 4.4,
being derived from the dry density and solid particle density [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. In
Equation 4.4, n symbolizes the soil porosity [-], V stands for the total soil sample volume [m3], while
the rest of the parameters were presented above.
e = Vv
Vs
= ПҒs

(4.4)
Using the previously described equations, the amplitudes for void ratio and porosity were evaluated
for the available samples from borehole PMC-SB-05, as it is shown in Table 4.2. Due to the relatively
low values registered for the dry density, elevated results for void ratios and porosities were estimated.
It should be mentioned that for diamicton soil type a regular maximum porosity can reach up to
50-55% [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], however, as it can be observed in Table 4.2 all the samples
exceed the milestone of 60%. This outcome can be explained by the high concentration of ice and
massive ice through the soil profile, especially in the form of suspended cryostructures or irregular
reticulate ice. In addition, the topographic location of the borehole indicates the absence of a
significant compacting factor acting on the surface, such as thick snow accumulation or stagnating
water, thereafter the soil material appears to be in a considerable loose phase. The same observation,
as it was specified for the density property, should be stated regarding the top sample analyzed during
the laboratory testing. As the corresponding dry density was recorded with an extremely low value,
the afferent porosity and void ratio derived from it should be interpreted with cautiousness.

According to [Tveit, 2022], no grain size distribution analysis is available for the boreholes PMC-SB-02,
PMC-SB-03, PMC-SB-04 or PMC-SB-05, nevertheless, a granulometric curve is provided for borehole
42
==PAGE:62==
Chapter 4. Field and laboratory measurements
PMC-SB-06 on the uphill side of Longyeardalen. As both PMC-SB-05 and PMC-SB-06 are located
on the same side of the valley, with respect to the main river, and they were drilled at a relatively
similar elevation referenced to the slope inclination it can be assumed that the granulometric curve
interpreted from PMC-SB-06 can be applied for the log PMC-SB-05 in accordance. It should be
mentioned that due to the spatial variability of the geotechnical data, combined with a possible
different morphology of the extracted material, the results can slightly deviate from the real situation,
even so, without additional data the decision should be considered justified. Hence, the grain size
distribution of borehole PMC-SB-06 is presented in Figure 4.6a for the upper two meters, while the
rest down to 3.28m is illustrated in Figure 4.6b. Understanding the particle size distribution of the
soil material can bring an advantage regarding the prediction of the liquid flow or moisture transfer
within the slope boundaries, along with a relative indication of the soil permeability range of values.

The values observed in Figure 4.6 were obtained as a result of a laboratory sieve analysis, including
the following sizes for the sieve nets 9.5mm, 4.75mm, 2.36mm, 1.18mm, 0.6mm, 0.3mm, 0.15mm,
0.075mm, according to the ASTM standard. In order to process the percentage of passing and
retaining material for each sieve, the dry sieve, tin and dry sample were weighted prior to the
conduction of the experiment. The mass of soil retained at each level was registered in accordance,
resulting in the curves shown in Figure 4.6. As it can be seen in Figure 4.6a, the top 2m of the soil
column contains slightly more thin particles than the deeper locations illustrated in Figure 4.6b. The
most elevated passing rate was recorded for sample 13B, being located between 133-140cm deep. It
can be characterized by an almost 22.66% of grains smaller than 0.075mm, while no grains appear
to be larger than 4.75mm. At the opposite side, sample 10 between 82-90cm deep contains only
approximately 3.61% grains smaller than 0.075mm, with over 25% retaining rate for the sieve 9.5mm.
The highest retaining percentage on the widest sieve, in the first 2m of the soil column, was registered
for sample 6 identified in the range 52-67cm. For this sample close to 51.92% of the constitutive
grains seemed to have greater sizes than 9.5mm. Taking into account the International Soil Science
Society (ISSS) classification [ISSS, 1929] it can be emphasized that the soil layer delimited between
0-2m appears to be dominated by sand size grains, observation in accordance with the prevalent
diamicton soil type in the area.
Below 2m the soil appears to be looser, being described by a retaining rate of over 70% for the sieve
size 9.5mm, as it can be seen in Figure 4.6b. Grains thinner than 0.075mm contribute with less than
5% for the constitution of the soil, consideration applicable for all the analyzed samples. According
to Figure 4.6b, the sample with the most elevated value of coarse grains seems to be 14, located at
an approximate depth between 140-152cm. It contains close to 94.87% grains larger than 9.5mm,
while the fine particles represent less than 1.8% of the material. Based on the ISSS classification
system, the lower side of the soil column can be described as gravel type soil [ISSS, 1929], with
respect to the granulometric curve illustrated in Figure 4.6b.

4.2.6
Depth to bedrock
The prevalent bedrock layer characteristic for the region of Longyeardalen seems to be composed of
sedimentary sandstones, as it can be seen in Figure 3.1. The sandstone outcrop can be observed
on the upper side of the slopes surrounding the valley, see Figure 4.7, being subjected to repetitive
cycles of freezing and thawing during time. Nevertheless, the rock outcrop illustrated in Figure 4.7
can be considered as a relatively stable homogenous mass, indicating a reduced stability concern.
The landslide susceptible zones appear to be the channels formed on the superior part of the slopes,
between the sandstone formations, as it is shown in Figure 4.7. For the previously mentioned
locations the constitutive material is generally represented by debris flow deposits or rock fall
deposits [Rubensdotter, 2022], which can be subjected to consistent mass mobilization processes in
unfavorable hydro-thermal circumstances. Consequently, the depth to bedrock should be investigated
along the slope profiles in order to estimate the thickness of the soil layer vulnerable to downslope
displacements.
Figure 4.7: The geomorphological context, related to the slopes surrounding Longyeardalen, is
emphasized by the rock outcrop situated on the superior part of the slopes and the slide susceptible
channels filled with coarse material, indicating the importance of establishing the depth to bedrock
values for the stability assessment.
According to [Tveit, 2022], the bedrock was not reached during the drilling process for the boreholes
illustrated in Figure 4.2, as it can be seen as well in the stratigraphic logs presented in Figure A.1 and
Figure A.2. Moreover, due to the high spatial variability connected to this parameter the information
evaluated from individual boreholes may not be sufficient to estimate a relatively accurate general
trend. Hence, a sectional profile can generate a more valuable overview of the bedrock depth in
the region. Based on [DS-EntreprenГёr-AS, 2019], a series of boreholes was drilled in 2019 in the
45
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 4. Field and laboratory measurements
proximity of Sukkertoppen for a local development project, offering detailed data regarding depth to
bedrock in the specified area, as it can be seen in Figure 4.8. Apart from bedrock depth, the data
series contain information on surface elevation and the measured slope angles for each individual
location, see Appendix A.2.5 [DS-EntreprenГёr-AS, 2019]. Considering the steep inclination pattern
dominating the slope systems in the region, a correlation between the depth to bedrock and the
slope angle can represent a reasonable engineering assumption, allowing for further approximations
of the bedrock depth around the valley. For the data presented in Appendix A.2.5, some boreholes
indicates inconsistent values for depth to bedrock when referenced to the expected trend, decrease of
bedrock depth with elevation, particularly ST-2019-106 and ST-2019-109 from Table A.4, respectively
ST-2019-107 from Table A.5. The reason for the obtained measurements can be related to the
boreholeвҖҷs location along the slope, being situated in a depression zone close to the top, as it can be
visualized in Figure 4.8. Thereafter, excluding these results from the trendline determination can be
a justified decision.
Figure 4.8: Boreholes drilled in the proximity of Sukkertoppen, on the right side of Longyeardalen,
providing depth to bedrock information for two slope profiles, based on [DS-EntreprenГёr-AS, 2019].
The dependency connecting depth to bedrock with respect to the tangent of the slope angle can
be seen in Figure 4.9a for profile 1, while Figure 4.9b represents profile 2. It should be mentioned
that the results below were processed for the filtered data sets, after the elimination of the irrelevant
components from the series. Using Microsoft Excel functionality it was possible to generate trendlines
from the available data sets, allowing for the interpretation of a suitable match. The options
considered for the analysis were linear, exponential, logarithmic, power based, leading to different
defining equations for the trends. The output of the processing method can be visualized in Appendix
A.2.5, for profile 1 in Table A.6, respectively Table A.7 for profile 2. It can be observed in both
situations that the most accurate fit, for the existing data, should be attributed to the linear trendline.
Nevertheless, the linear pattern manifests a low accuracy for the extreme values when the depth
to bedrock fluctuates between its maximum possible value and the minimum limit. In Table A.6

4.2.7
Soil permeability
Permeability, also encountered in the technical literature as hydraulic conductivity, controls the water
flow through the subsurface medium, allowing the formation of drainage paths along the slope profile.
Moreover, soil permeability can be responsible for the delimitation of distinct saturation zones, as
water velocity amplitude through the soil material respects DarcyвҖҷs flow rule for porous mediums.
When the permeability of a specific soil layer in a slope system has an elevated value, water can
be expected to flow downslope without significant resistance and in a relatively reduced amount of
time following the infiltration event. The opposite approach should be considered for a soil being
characterized by a decreased hydraulic conductivity, leading to the stagnation of water mass inside
soil structure. Regarding stability assessment, the stationary water within the slope boundaries
can be associated with the development of pore pressure in the system. As was discussed in the
prior chapters, the surge of pore pressure along the slope can generate considerable destabilization
47
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 4. Field and laboratory measurements
effects, being one of the major factors disturbing the structural integrity of the slope profile during
an infiltration event.
According to [Tveit, 2022] no laboratory testing was conducted for the determination of soil permeabil-
ity for any of the available samples extracted from the boreholes around Longyeardalen. Thereafter,
permeability should be assessed using an empirical method relying on the existing data. It was
observed that the geomorphological stratification in the valley consists of coarse material, with a high
porosity. In these circumstances, it is expected that the hydraulic conductivity should be described
by an elevated range of values. A simple method for evaluating permeability can be represented by
Hazen formula, illustrated in Equation 4.7 [Hazen, 1892]. In Equation 4.7, k stands for permeability
[cm/s], D10 represents the 10 per cent fraction size taken from the grain size distribution curve [mm],
while C illustrates the Hazen correlation factor [-]. For the majority of applications, Hazen coefficient
can fluctuate between 0.8 and 1.2 [Hazen, 1892].
k = C вӢ…D2
10 [cm/s]
(4.7)

[West and Shakoor, 2018]. It should be mentioned that sample 14 has a retaining rate of over 90%
on the sieve 9.5mm, as it can be seen in Figure A.4, hence the permeability at this depth was
considered from technical literature [West and Shakoor, 2018], indicating a sandy gravel material
type. Moreover, the samples corresponding to depth range 52-67cm and 133-140cm have no specific
intersection for the fraction representing 10% due to the limitations of the sieve analysis experiment.
Nevertheless, an approximation of the grain diameter was considered based on the curve trend. The
relatively favorable output shown in Table 4.4 may indicate a constant water percolation outside
the slope system when the infiltration rates do not exceed extreme thresholds. However, as it was
observed during the rainstorms in October and November 2016, the landslide susceptibility can surge
as a result of significant precipitation levels.

4.3
Thermal data
Collecting and interpreting the thermal data around Longyeardalen can be of important concern
for the stability assessment in the region, as the soil behavior manifests different properties in its
thawed state or frozen state. As it was presented in the landslide conceptualization chapter, the
thawed soil seems to be more susceptible to downslope movement when compared to the frozen
ground or permafrost layer. Thereafter it can be essential to interpret the thaw penetration depth
in the studied area, assuming the interface between the active layer and permafrost as an idealized
sliding plane. Furthermore, estimating the thermal properties of the ground can facilitate numerical
simulation modelling, allowing the approximation of historical temperature profiles or future expected
conditions.
4.3.1
Thermal properties
The thermal properties associated to a soil material control the heat flow propagation with depth,
together with the energy level requested to modify the temperature values at each individual location
along the soil column. The principal characteristics necessary for a thermal numerical model can be
expressed as thermal conductivity, heat capacity, thermal diffusivity, thermal expansion or contraction
coefficient, latent heat of the soil. According to [Tveit, 2022] no field investigations or laboratory
testing were conducted to determine the thermal characteristics for the available samples extracted
from the boreholes. Therefore, alternative methods to approximate these values should be considered,
relying on the existing available data. It should be emphasized that the existing state of the soil,
either thawed or frozen, can impact the previously mentioned properties.
Thermal conductivity influences the transfer of kinetic energy through the soil texture, from the
warm area to the cooler part. It can be expressed as a function depending on the dry density
of the studied soil and the degree of saturation, being direct proportional with both parameters
[Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. Hence, if the dry density increases it will lead to the elevation of
the thermal conductivity value, the same remark being applicable for the degree of saturation of
the analyzed sample. A visual interpretation, which can be used for the estimation of soil thermal
conductivity, can be seen in Figure 4.10, based on work developed by [Harlan and Nixon, 1978].
The distinction can be observed between the diagram assessing soils in a frozen state, see Figure
4.10a [Harlan and Nixon, 1978] and the diagram allowing for the determination of unfrozen thermal
conductivity shown in Figure 4.10b [Harlan and Nixon, 1978]. It should be stated that the models
illustrated in Figure 4.10 are applicable for sands or gravel materials, indicating an average thermal
conductivity. Furthermore, apart from the dry density or degree of saturation for the sample the
water content should be identified a priori.
In order to obtain the value for the unfrozen thermal conductivity through a numerical approach,
the method suggested by [Johansen, 1975] should be considered. Based on [Johansen, 1975], the
unfrozen thermal conductivity can be expressed as presented in Equation 4.8, with О»u as the unfrozen
thermal conductivity [W/(m вӢ…K)], О»sat representing the saturated thermal conductivity of the soil
[W/(m вӢ…K)], О»dry illustrating the dry thermal conductivity of the soil [W/(m вӢ…K)], while Ke
symbolizes the Kersten number [-].
О»u = (О»sat вҲ’О»dry) вӢ…Ke + О»dry [W/(m вӢ…K)]
(4.8)

Soil heat capacity can be defined as the necessary amount of heat which will lead to the increase of
the material temperature by 1в—ӢC [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. Considering the four components
which are included in the soil structure as solid, water, ice, air, the heat capacity expression can be
seen in Equation 4.15 according to [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. Equation 4.15 is formulated
with respect to the mass fractions of each individual part, along with the specific heat correlated
to the specified part, where c represents the heat capacity of the soil [kJ/(kg вӢ…в—ӢC], cs stands for
the heat capacity of the solid part [kJ/(kg вӢ…в—ӢC], cw is the heat capacity of water [kJ/(kg вӢ…в—ӢC], ci
illustrates the heat capacity for ice [kJ/(kg вӢ…в—ӢC], cair indicates the heat capacity for air [kJ/(kg вӢ…в—ӢC],
m stands for the total mass of the soil [kg] while ms, mw, mi, mair represent the mass fractions of
the solid part, water, ice, respectively air.
c = 1
m вӢ…(cs вӢ…ms + cw вӢ…mw + ci вӢ…mi + cair вӢ…mair) [kJ/(kg вӢ…в—ӢC)]
(4.15)

4.17 shows the definition for the corresponding mass heat capacity. In both Equation 4.16 and
Equation 4.17 the contribution associated with the air component can be negligible, due to its reduced
amplitude. Apart from the terms which have been already described, cv indicates the volumetric heat
capacity of the soil [kJ/(m3 вӢ…в—ӢC)], cm means the mass heat capacity of the sample [kJ/(kg вӢ…в—ӢC)],

Thermal diffusivity can be defined as a function of thermal conductivity of the soil, heat capacity,
and bulk density, indicating the rate of the heat flow propagation through the soil mass. It can
be expressed as shown in Equation 4.20 [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], with Dsoil as the thermal
diffusivity of the studied soil [m2/s], О» means the thermal conductivity [W/m вӢ…в—ӢC], c represents the
heat capacity for the sample [kJ/kg вӢ…в—ӢC], ПҒ stands for the bulk density [kg/m3]. Furthermore, it was
observed that the diffusivity describing soils in a frozen phase seems to be more elevated than the
diffusivity attributed to the unfrozen soils, this aspect indicating a more pronounced heat transmission
associated with the frozen saturated soil material [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. Some reference

(4.20)
When the phase shift occurs in a soil material, from the frozen state to the thawed state, a
specific amount of heat has to be absorbed in order to facilitate the transition process. The latent
heat represents the quantity of heat energy requested by the unit mass of soil to convert the ice
retained in its structure into liquid [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. The expression proposed by
[Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004] can be seen in Equation 4.21, with L indicating the volumetric latent
heat of a given soil [kJ/m3], ПҒd meaning the dry density of the soil [kg/m3], w represents the total
water content of the sample [%], wu stands for the unfrozen water content [%], LвҖҷ defines the mass
latent heat associated with water, recommended at a value of 333.7 [kJ/kg].
L = ПҒd вӢ…LвҖІ вӢ…w вҲ’wu
100
[kJ/m3]
(4.21)
Within the arctic regions the freezing and thawing cycles are exhibiting their influence continuously
over time, affecting the structural integrity of the soil by a constant expansion and contraction
processes. Laboratory observations allowed for the classification of different thermal expansion-
contraction coefficients, illustrating the expected behavior for a wide range of soils, as it can be
seen in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. Furthermore, according to [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004],
a detailed formulation for the coefficient of thermal expansion-contraction can be seen in Equation
4.22, with Оұ as the coefficient of thermal expansion-contraction [1/в—ӢC], L0 represents the length
value connected to a reference temperature [m], dL indicates the measured change in length [m], T
stands for temperature [в—ӢC], while Оө illustrates the material recorded strain [-].

4.3.2
Temperature profiles
For the drilled locations, illustrated in Figure 4.2, casings containing thermal sensors were installed
for PMC-SB-03, PMC-SB-04, PMC-SB-05, according to [Tveit, 2022]. The thermal system started
recording data from 8th of October 2022, having a 20cm span between the transmitters down to 2m,
followed by a span of 50cm for the remaining distance. A denser distribution was selected for the upper
part of the soil column due to the interest for the seasonal frozen ground, more precisely monitoring
the variation of the thaw penetration depth along a full annual cycle. Nevertheless, the reduced length
of the available datasets for the discussed boreholes do not allow for a complete interpretation of the
thermal regime in the region, being limited to less than a year of measurements, mostly describing
the soil behavior during the freezing season. Considering the restraints imposed by the lack of data
for the studied locations additional information had to be collected from previous projects developed
in the Longyeardalen area. Based on [NVE, 2017], [RambГёll, 2017] a fully functional thermal system
seems to be situated in proximity of Longyearbyen, providing thermal information since late 2018.
The casing inserted in this site consists of more scattered thermal sensors, however it reaches a
depth of 15m below the surface level. Consequently, even without detailed information about the
geological and geotechnical conditions of the borehole described in [NVE, 2017], [RambГёll, 2017],
the temperature profiles can represent a valuable indicator for the heat propagation pattern in the
area and they can used as a relative reference for the results assessed from PMC-SB-03, PMC-SB-04,
PMC-SB-05.
Due to technical related problems, the available sensors had difficulties of accurately transmitting
data in some days of the measured period, causing the existence of void dates withing the thermal

27th, 28th, 29th, 30th of October, between 5th to 17th, 20th, 29th of November, 6th, 16th, 17th, 18th,
22nd to 27th, 31st of December and 6th, 10th, 11th, 12th of January. PMC-SB-04 is missing October
16th, 17th, 21st, 22nd, 23rd, 26th to 30th, November 3rd to 17th, 20th, 29th, December 5th, 6th, 15th
to 18th, 22nd to 27th, 31st, January 10th, 11th, 12th. Finally, PMC-SB-05 offers no details about 5th

uncovered periods in November and December. Hence, the dataset originating from the PMC-SB-05

Based on the registered temperatures, with respect to time and depth, a specific thermal behavior
can be distinguished for the studied area. As can be seen in Figure 4.12 and Figure 4.13, the thermal
propagation in the upper 2m of the soil profiles appears to be more pronounced than at higher depths.
For the close proximity of the ground surface, see Figure 4.12a for 0.2m together with Figure 4.12b
for 0.6m, the measured temperatures appeared to be heavily affected by the fluctuations occurring
at the surface level, following the trends accordingly. The soil layer delimitated by depth range 1-2m
seems to be subjected as well to significant thermal variations due to the thermal flux dissipating
from the top, as it is illustrated in Figure 4.12c and Figure 4.12d, nevertheless the measured values
do not oscillate at the same level of magnitude as the context described in the upper side of the
ground. Regarding depths below 2m, temperature curves are shown in Figure 4.13a for 3m deep
along with Figure 4.13b for the maximum drilled depth of the boreholes. It should be remarked
that fluctuations occurring at these depths are considerably diminished, with drops below 2в—ӢC for
the curves described at 3m, while the registered curves illustrating the maximum possible depth
indicate changes lower than 1в—ӢC for the analyzed period of time, provided by [Tveit, 2022]. The
complete thermal curves comprehending the entire datasets obtained for PMC-SB-03, PMC-SB-04,
PMC-SB-05 are included in Appendix A.2.6. The temperature variation should be interpreted as a
direct effect of the heat inflow from the surface because of the shallow characteristic of the drilled
boreholes, not allowing for a relevant contribution of the thermal gradient in the region. Thereafter,
the geothermal gradient influence can be neglected, especially that Longyeardalen is not a geothermal
active area [Elvevold et al., 2007]. The observed transition between the first 2m of the soil column
to the lower part can be justified by a difference in the soil thermal properties or the delimitation
between seasonal frozen soil zone and permafrost.

Chapter 5
Thermal numerical model
The purpose of the thermal numerical model consists of the assessment of the thaw penetration depth
over time, generating valuable observations which will be further used in the stability assessment.
The identification of the active layer thickness should be correlated with extreme water infiltration
events, as it was the case for the powerful rainstorm in October 2016, contributing to a failure
susceptible environment. For the purpose of the current project the computational software selected
for the analysis is Plaxis, version 2022. Plaxis represents a finite element-based software, which allows
for thermal calculations relying on the heat transfer theoretical approach and the input of various
parameters connected to the thermal conduct of the defined materials. Numerical modelling requires
a wide range of input data in order to assure the quality of the generated results, hence a schematic
diagram, containing different types of parameters and conditions which need to be provided, can be
seen in Figure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: Generic scheme emphasizing different information levels involved in a thermal model
simulation, with respect to the requirements demanded by Plaxis software.
It can be emphasized from Figure 5.1 that the major implications associated with the thermal
numerical model can be classified as theoretical background defined in the software, geometry
description for the model, meshing pattern, assignation of suitable material properties to reproduce
the in-situ context, defining the appropriate boundary conditions of the model, calibration using
58
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
the reference thermal data. It should be mentioned that the description summarized in Figure 5.1
focuses mainly on the thermal module in Plaxis, along with some functionalities of the hydrological
module. The strength parameters and mechanical boundary conditions are not of interest to the
current chapter.
5.1
Theoretical background
Plaxis represents a finite element program, allowing the user to define a specific type of discretization
for the defined soil layers or soil elements as 15-Node triangular elements or 6-Node triangular
elements [Bentley, 2022b], depending on the targeted level of refinement. The formulation of the
meshing generating system can be seen in Equation 5.1 [Bentley, 2022b], with le as the final element
dimension, re represents the relative element size factor approximated from the element distribution,
xmin, xmax, ymin, ymax indicates the outer geometry dimensions of the meshed component. Apart
from the general discretization pattern, Plaxis includes the functionality of adjusting the elements
size along specific geometrical boundaries.
le = re вӢ…0.06 вӢ…
вҲҡ
(xmax вҲ’xmin)2 + (ymax вҲ’ymin)2
(5.1)
In the simulated model the soil material is defined as a porous medium, incorporating water, ice, or
air inside its pore space. Each of the previously mentioned components possesses distinct thermal
properties which will impact the heat transfer through the soil layer, depending on the fraction
distribution. The reference values attributed for the distinct water phases are illustrated in Table
5.1, according to the recommendations provided in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. It should be
remarked that Table 5.1 presents constant values for the water properties, considering a reference
temperature of 20в—ӢC.
