Considering the variation of temperature, induced by the thermal analysis within the model boundaries,
it can be assumed that selecting constant values for the water substance may lead to a margin of
errors to some extent. Hence, the temperature dependency manifested by water density, thermal
conductivity, heat capacity and viscosity should be selected for the simulation. Plaxis provides the
option of attributing water properties as a function of temperature, therefore the curves illustrated in
Appendix A.3.1, Figure A.6 and A.7 should be selected. In Figure A.6a it can be seen that density
constantly decreases with the temperature increase, excluding the transition from the solid state to
the liquid phase. A similar behavior can be observed for the thermal conductivity of water in Figure
A.6b, however, around the 0в—ӢC milestone a significant drop can be distinguished. Heat capacity
seems to rise with the ascension of temperature, expressing a consistent surge in the proximity of
59
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
phase change zone, as it can be seen in Figure A.7a. For the water viscosity the value appears to be
constant while the solution exists in a solid state, followed by a decline between 0в—ӢC and 100в—ӢC as it
can be observed in Figure A.7b. By implementing the temperature dependent properties of water,
the accuracy of the thermal outcome should be enhanced.
The heat balance equation, defining the heat transport through a porous medium, can be defined as pre-
sented in Equation 5.2, according to [Combarnous and Bories, 1975], [Bories and Combarnous, 1973].

gradient operator, QT stands for the heat source term [J/m3], JAW represents the advective internal
energy flux in water [J/m2], JC is the conductive heat flux in the porous medium [J/m2].

The frozen soil transition is considered in the model through the adjusting of the storage term, along
with the thermal conductivity of the porous medium, as additional energy should be requested in
order to produce a similar effect. The modifications are illustrated in Equation 5.11 for the volumetric
heat capacity of the porous medium, while Equation 5.12 indicates the thermal conductivity fitted
expression. The new properties included in Equation 5.11 and 5.12 can be described as wu the

(5.12)
The thermal flow module in Plaxis allows for different types of computational approaches, integrating
the initial phase of the calculation together with the following phases required in the simulation
process. For the initial phase two distinct options can be selected, earth gradient or steady state
thermal flow. Earth gradient offers the possibility of establishing an initial temperature distribution
withing the model boundaries, by specifying the exact value of the temperature change with respect
to depth, together with a reference height from where the function should be applied. While this
function can represent a valuable tool for a rapid and uniform initial distribution related to flat
surfaces, it has its limitations regarding the inclined terrain where the software cannot provide a
gradient parallel to the slope surface level. Considering this context, the steady state thermal flow
seems to be a more suitable alternative to define the initial thermal conditions along the slope
system. The steady state calculation applies the heat balance condition along the defined soil layers,
validating the convergence of the flow equation for every finite element generated for the model.
Furthermore, the steady state heat propagation is dependent on the thermal boundary conditions
defined in the model. For the build up phases, connected to the initial phase, three approaches can be
implemented, earth gradient, the use of temperatures from the initial phase or transient thermal flow.
61
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
Earth gradient should be excluded from the analysis according to the discussion above. Maintaining
the temperature distribution from the parent phase can be a convenient option as the steady state
calculation should be capable of generating a stable thermal environment in the soil, nevertheless for
the purpose of determining the thaw penetration depth along the slope profile, a transient thermal
flow should be integrated in the simulation. Transient analysis permits the integration of time
dependent functions along the thermal boundary conditions, therefore historical or meteorological
thermal data can be accessed in order to monitor the soil thermal behavior.
5.2
Model geometry
The location selected for the generation of the thermal model can be seen in Figure 5.2, representing
one of the unstable slopes which collapsed during the rainstorm event occurred in 2016. Furthermore,
in Figure 5.2 it can be observed that the borehole PMC-SB-05 seems to be positioned in the
close proximity of the slope bottom side, hence the available thermal data from PMC-SB-05 can
be extrapolated along the slope profile in order to approximate the thermal regime. It should be
mentioned that extending the thermal knowledge from an individual point to the entire slope implies
a rough assumption that the thermal regime stays constant with elevation, aspect which may not
accurately reproduce the real situation on the field. Nevertheless, with the limited information
reflecting the conditions in the region, the above-specified approach can offer valuable details
regarding a general thermal trend. For the profile illustrated in Figure 5.2, topographic information is
provided on the Artic DEM platform for some of the points, including the elevation and the slope
angle in the studied location. The detailed data-set can be seen in Appendix A.3.2, Table A.8.
Figure 5.2: Selection of the slope profile for the thermal assessment model, considered based on the
landslide event impacting the area in 2016. The ortofoto map was accessed from the Norwegian
Polar institute database.
62
==PAGE:82==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
Apart from the surface elevation, the depth of the soil model should be estimated according to
the available knowledge. One aspect which should be taken into account is represented by the
depth of the thermal system installed in borehole PMC-SB-05, indicating a lower boundary 5m deep
with respect to the surface level. In was observed from previous studies in the region [NVE, 2017],
[RambГёll, 2017], that the heat propagation initiated from the soil surface to deeper locations can
influence the soil temperature down to 12-15m, followed by an approximate linear thermal gradient
trend from this depth downwards. Considering this context, it can be a challenge to estimate accurate
thermal profiles below the level of the measured data, without having reference values to calibrate
the numerical model. Consequently, the bottom boundary of the model can be assumed to be 5m,
particularly for the downslope part of the profile. It can be seen in Figure A.2b that no bedrock
was identified withing the drilled borehole, emphasising that the permafrost thickness exceeds 3m
close to the valley side. However, on the superior part of the slope the soil thickness is expected
to decrease, based on field observations and historical investigations [DS-EntreprenГёr-AS, 2019].
Thereafter, Equation 4.5 can be of great use to evaluate the depth to bedrock for high elevation
points. The slope profile, constructed on the discussed geometrical boundaries, is shown in the image
below, along with the specific details included in Figure 5.3.
Regarding layers distribution, the information provided in the stratigraphic log should be correlated
with the geotechnical properties resulted from the laboratory testing. Even though the log appears
to be composed mainly from diamicton sediments, Figure A.2b illustrates a distinct cryostructure
succession between 0-2m and 2-5m, marking the delimitation of the active layer thickness to the
permafrost zone. Thereafter, two separate layers can be assumed in the model. In addition, the
gravimetric water content profile shown in Figure 4.4d, the porosity values described in Table 4.2, the
granulometric curve distribution illustrated in Figure 4.6, along with the temperature profile recorded
on the site presented in Figure A.5c may indicate a distinctive thermal behavior within both the
top soil and the bottom soil, leading to the necessity of further dividing the main zones of the soil
column. In consequence, it can be justified to select the following layer succession 0-0.2m, 0.2-0.6m,
0.6-1m, 1-2m, 2-3.5m, 3.5-5m. This selection can facilitate the implementation of the initial thermal
regime as well, by allowing the initialization of multiple horizontal thermal boundary conditions at
different depths, aiming for a precise steady state analysis.
Beside the 2D thermal model, selected for the simulation process, it was observed that a 1D soil profile
can represent a beneficial consideration for the thermal analysis, hence the geometrical configuration
can be seen in Figure 5.4. The motivation behind the 1D model consists of the rapid approximation
of the thermal regime, convenient estimation of the initial thermal conditions for the 2D model, along
with the beneficial contribution regarding the calibration process. An identical layer distribution was
chosen for this scenario, based on the discussion elaborated in the previous paragraph. Nevertheless,
the limitations connected to the 1D model should be understood, especially for the heat propagation
pattern, highlighting the importance of a more consistent bidirectional geometry.

5.3
Material properties
Plaxis provides the possibility of implementing a wide variety of soil parameters, defining the
mechanical behavior, thermal behavior, or hydrological behavior of the processed model. As the
target of the simulation consists of observing the thermal regime within the model boundaries, the
focus will be set on establishing accurate thermal characteristics for the studied soil material. The
selected values should reflect the in-situ conditions, along with a correlated outcome between the
thermal response of the model and the recorded measurements from the borehole PMC-SB-05. The
essential properties required for the thermal modeling can be considered as soil porosity, thermal
heat capacity, thermal conductivity, soil grain density, soil dry density, unfrozen water content, water
content, saturation, salinity concentration, coefficient of thermal expansion-contraction.
66
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
As it has been mentioned in the previous section, the amount of data collected and interpreted for
the borehole PMC-SB-05 seems to be relatively limited, with the laboratory testing successfully
conducted on the determination of soil density and water content distribution along the borehole. The
remaining parameters should be estimated based on analytical or empirical expressions, incorporating
a certain degree of uncertainty. The porosities defined for the implemented layers are illustrated in
Table 5.3, according to the measurements presented in Table 4.2. Remarking the consistent data gap
between 0-2m deep, the porosity resulted from the sample located at the depth of 2m was chosen
for the upper four layers, as the interpreted value 0.671 seems to be more realistic than the outcome
from the first organic rich 10cm of the soil column. For the lower frozen layers an average porosity
was estimated between the described geometrical boundaries, leading to the porosity amplitudes
shown in Table 5.3.
Applying a similar approach regarding the soil dry density, the result included in Table 4.2, for the
top sample, does not represent the soil behavior in a relevant manner. Hence, a distinct assumption
should be taken into consideration for the targeted zone. As the organic impurities may have a slight
impact over the soil dry density at shallow depths, the values estimated for L1 and L2 were derived
as 20% lower than the measurement registered at 2m depth. The dry density recorded for the sample
extracted from 2m deep was assigned to L3 and L4, an average amplitude was considered for layer L5,
while the bottom 1.5m can be described by a dry density of 910 kg/m3, as it can be seen in Table
5.3. Furthermore, without specific measurements describing the soil grain density, a recommended
value of 2700 kg/m3 was selected for the modeling purposes [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], as it
is illustrated in Table 5.3.
The water content was incorporated based on Figure 4.4d, as a mean value between 5-23cm for L1,
23-66cm for L2, 66-105cm for L3, 105-200cm for L4, 200-350cm for L5, respectively 350-500cm
for L6. The results can be seen in Table 5.3. Having the constitutive components available for
each individual layer, soil saturation can be approximated using Equation 5.13 for the top 2m of the
soil column, while Equation 5.14 was considered for the bottom 3m of the model, according to the
theory presented in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. In Equation 5.13, Gs stands for the specific
gravity of the solid material [-], indicated as 2.70 in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], w represents
the water content of the soil [%], e stands for the void ratio [-], while in Equation 5.14 the additional
terms can be defined as wi the ice content within the layer boundaries [%], ПҒw stands for the water
density, recommended to a value of 1000 kg/m3 for water at 4в—ӢC [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004],
respectively ПҒi suggests the ice density, defined as 916.8 kg/m3 by [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004].
It should be important to emphasized that for the implemented Equation 5.13 and Equation 5.14
the assumption that the upper 2m of the soil column exists in the thawed state, while the remaining
depths can be characterized by a frozen soil phase, was applied. Consequently, the computed degree
of saturation for the existing model stratification can be seen in Table 5.3.

Soil unfrozen water content can be formulated as a function of temperature, as presented in
Equation 4.14 [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. With no specific measurements available, the fitting
parameters Оұ and ОІ should be assumed in order to represent the existing soil in borehole PMC-SB-05.
A set of reference values are included in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004] for a wide diversity of soil
types. Therefore, the selected parameters for each soil layer can be seen in Table 5.2, based on
[Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. The water contents included in Table 5.2 were defined as discussed
above. Moreover, the unfrozen water content curves, with respect to soil temperature, can be seen
in Appendix A.3.3. It should be mentioned that the studied soil appears to be quite porous, resulting

The heat capacity was evaluated in accordance with Equation 4.17, considering the requirement
presented in Plaxis software for a mass heat capacity input for the soil material. As it can be seen
in Equation 4.17, the mass heat capacity can be determined from the volumetric heat capacity,
depending on the phase describing the water infiltrated in the pore space. Hence, for the upper two
2m on the profile the soil is assumed in the unfrozen state, using Equation 4.18 for the evaluation of
the volumetric heat capacity, while the bottom part can be defined by Equation 4.19, presuming that
soil exists in the frozen state. The results can be observed in Table 5.3. For thermal conductivity,
the graphical interpretation approach was used as shown in Figure 4.10 [Harlan and Nixon, 1978].
Having the water content determined for all the layers in the model, along with the degree of
saturation, the estimated values are integrated in Table 5.3. The visual evaluation is included in
Appendix A.3.4, Figure A.9.
The thermal mechanical resistance of a soil material, as a function of temperature variation, can be
defined by the coefficient of thermal expansion or contraction. For the existing samples, no specific
laboratory testing was conducted, therefore approximated values were considered from technical
literature. The coefficient may be expressed from the composing minerals as it is presented in
[Delage, 2013], with values describing quartz as Оұquartz = 0.34E-4 [1/в—ӢC], calcite as Оұcalcite =
0.138E-4 [1/в—ӢC], feldspar as Оұfeldspar = 0.111E-4 [1/в—ӢC]. Taking into account the soil composition
as a mixture of prevalent quartz and feldspar minerals, with possible calcite dispersed intrusions
on the upper side of the soil column, the coefficients attributed to each layer in the model can be
visualized in Table 5.3.
As it was discussed in Section 5.1, soil is considered a porous medium, hence, the thermal properties
characterizing the existing materials in the pore space should be specified accordingly. The thermal
parameters describing liquid water, ice and water vapour were included in the simulation as it
can be observed in Table 5.1. Furthermore, the temperature dependent water properties were
incorporated in the modeling part as illustrated in Figure A.6, respectively Figure A.7, for density,
heat capacity, thermal conductivity, viscosity. Within the expected temperature range for the location
of Longyeardalen, it can be noticed from Figure A.6 and A.7 that the temperature fluctuation has a
limited impact over both the thermal conductivity and the heat capacity describing the water thermal
behavior.
The salinity concentration in the ground can have a significant influence on the thermal be-
havior of the designed soil material, as the freezing point for water is dependent on salinity
[Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004].
Without having relevant measurements from the area, an as-
sumption should be made regarding the expected conditions. According to [Tveit, 2022], along
68
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
with the geological processes discussed in [Elvevold et al., 2007], a low salinity environment can be
anticipated for the region of Longyeardalen, especially on high altitudes and the slope systems studied
in the current project. Thereafter, no alterations were considered for the water freezing point or on
the phase shifting mechanism.

5.4
Boundary conditions
In order to assure the quality of the simulation process the thermal boundary conditions, selected
for the model, should be considered with the purpose of reflecting the real in-situ thermal context.
The heat propagation through the soil medium will be significantly influenced by the implemented
boundaries both in the steady state analysis and in the transient flow phase. Within Plaxis software,
several thermal boundaries can be defined for the model as closed boundary, temperature, inflow,
convection, outflow, thermosyphons [Bentley, 2022b]. Each alternative may offer valuable thermal
control over the heat flow along the specified boundary. For the designed model, the following
boundaries were chosen, aiming to reproduce the thermal behavior of the soil in borehole PMC-SB-05,
on the vertical direction closed thermal boundaries at the edges of the model, while on the horizontal
direction both temperature and inflow were used during the analysis. It should be mentioned that
Plaxis does not provided any possibility of including the snow cover depth or vegetation thickness
69
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
within the simulating model, leading to the necessity of adjusting the surface boundary condition or
the soil thermal properties in order to compensate for the missing functionality.
Depending on the target of the simulation, distinct thermal boundaries were considered for the steady
state analysis or the transient flow analysis. In the steady state analysis, the aim focuses on obtaining
a ground thermal regime similar to the measured profile. Hence, horizontal temperature boundaries
were selected along each layer included in the model, at the reference depths 0m, 0.2m, 0.6m, 1m,
2m, 3.5m, 5m. This method allows for the input of the recorded temperatures in key points for the
soil model, establishing a uniform distribution across the slope section during the steady state phase.
A different option can be accessed in Plaxis, for the computation of the initial thermal regime, in
the form of a vertical thermal gradient dependent on depth. Nevertheless, the previously mentioned
alternative produces a uniform temperature distribution parallel to the horizontal plane, not parallel
to the slope surface. Therefore, the application of constant temperature boundaries along the layer
interfaces can represent a more consistent setup. If the initial temperatures, at the specified depths,
are not available from field investigations then the 1D model can be utilized to estimate the desired
temperature profile. The vertical edges of the model were assumed as closed boundary conditions,
due to the lack of geothermal activity in the region and the absence of heat sources around the
slope system. In this context, no significant heat exchange is expected on the horizontal direction
for the right and left geometrical borders. A comprehensive visualization of the distributed thermal
boundaries in the steady state phase is included in Appendix A.3.5 for both the 1D and the 2D
model.
The purpose of the transient flow analysis is represented by an accurate reproduction of the heat
propagation pattern through the soil medium. As the heat mass transport equation governs the
thermal flow in Plaxis, no thermal boundaries should be included within the model geometry, as the
designated material properties should ensure a satisfactory heat transfer. The vertical borders of the
model can be defined as closed thermal boundaries from the same considerations as discussed in
the previous paragraph. For the soil surface a time-dependent temperature boundary was selected,
offering the possibility to include extensive temperature data-sets in the simulated scenario. If
the surface temperature is not provided for the desired location, then the air temperature can
represent a reasonable replacement. Eventually, the lower boundary was set to a uniform inflow
mode, with the reference value of 0 kW/m2. This assumption was taken into account because
of the negligible geothermal activity in the area of Longyeardalen [Elvevold et al., 2007] together
with the shallow depth value of the bottom border, being defined at 5m below the ground level at
maximum. The soil column thickness is even more limited at higher elevations, as it can be seen in
Section 5.2. Furthermore, it was observed from previous research around Longyeardalen [NVE, 2017],
[RambГёll, 2017], that the temperature stabilizes at an approximate value of -2.5в—ӢC for a depth close
to 12m. Thereafter, a null heat inflow from below can represent a relatively realistic consideration
for the present simulation. The applied configuration can be seen in Appendix A.3.5 for both the 2D
and 1D model.
The discussed thermal boundaries should be perceived as an extrapolation of the temperature data
measured from PMC-SB-05 and the observations from [NVE, 2017], [RambГёll, 2017]. Without
having multiple reference boreholes along the studied slopes, the results can deviate from the real
circumstances due to the spatial variability context applicable for the ground medium. However,
relying on the existing information, the approach presented in the current section can generate a
valuable outcome concerning the thermal regime of the soil model.
70
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
5.5
Scenarios description
The evaluation of the active layer thickness constitutes the central focus point for the thermal
numerical model, as the separation surface delimitating permafrost to the unfrozen soil layer can
have a substantial implication on the landslide susceptibility of the slope system, aspect which will
be further investigated in the next chapters. The thermal assessment process can be divided into
two principal stages, starting with an initial model calibration and tuning based on the available
temperature measurements from borehole PMC-SB-05 according to [Tveit, 2022], succeeded by a
validation of the historical thermal data provided by [NVE, 2017], [RambГёll, 2017] for the years 2019,
2020, 2021, 2022. Finally, as no temperature profiles could be found for Longyeardalen in 2016,
the calibrated model can be used to generate, with a certain degree of precision, the temperature
trumpets for the soil profile, facilitating the monitoring process on the active layer thickness variation
during the entire year and establishing the approximate conditions at the time of the landslide event
described in [Christiansen et al., 2016].
Due to the limited amount of information regarding the ground hydrological environment around
Longyeardalen or the water percolation patterns defining the slopes in the region, coupling the
thermal numerical model with the hydrological numerical model can pose a significant challenge. The
wide variety of unknown parameters can lead to imprecise results or uncorrelated data. Hence, for the
thermal numerical model, two computational approaches were considered for the water table location.
The first scenario implies the water table being lowered down to the bottom of the model, at 5m
deep with respect to the surface level, while the second option included in the simulation considers
the fully saturated conditions for both the unfrozen and permafrost layer, with the underground
water being present in the whole model. Separate calibration processes were conducted for the
two positions describing the water table. Observing the grain size distribution for the assigned soil
materials, see Figure 4.6a and Figure 4.6b, along with the stratigraphic log interpretation in Figure
A.2b, it can be emphasized that the soil can be described by a granular composition, with relatively
coarse particles. Therefore, a drained behavior type should be applied to all constitutive layers in
the model, assumption justified by the discussed soil texture. Furthermore, taking into account the
mechanical action along the slope system, it appears to be generated only by the loads connected to
the water infiltration or snow self-weight. Consequently, the consolidation analysis does not represent
a point of interest for the current project.
The model geometry presents a high level of complexity, as can be seen in Section 5.2. In order to
assure a satisfactory distribution of finite elements within the model boundaries, a fined mesh should
be generated for the soil layers. Starting from Equation 5.1, a relative element size equal to 0.5 was
selected for the global discretization procedure in Plaxis. Moreover, different coarseness factors were
applied along the cluster boundaries, as shown in Figure 5.5, providing additional refinements to
the layer meshing network, with the purpose of preventing the occurrence of unconverging nodes or
numerical errors within the thermal model. In total, a number of 11361 triangular elements were
generated as a result of the meshing process. A cross section was created at approximately 115m
from the left boundary of the model, aiming for the interpretation of temperature profiles from the
lower part of the slope, as it can be seen in Appendix A.3.7, Figure A.11. The chosen location for
the cross section should reflect the soil thermal behavior in the proximity of the drilled borehole
PMC-SB-05, enhancing the model calibration quality.
71
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.5: Mesh refinement coarseness factors applied for the cluster boundaries, enhancing the
element distribution along the model, represented in (a) Detail A; (b) Detail D. The presented
configuration for the coarseness factors should be applied within the entire model geometry, respecting
the pattern indicated in the illustrated details.
5.5.1
Model calibration by reference data
The calibration process relies on the temperature measurements available for borehole PMC-SB-05
[Tveit, 2022], shown in Figure A.5c. The purpose of the calibration can be expressed by obtaining a
similar thermal behavior of the model as the situation observed on the field for each arbitrary depth
along the slope profile. As it was described in the methodology above, the thermal numerical model
includes two computational stages, defining the initial steady state thermal regime and implementing
a time dependent thermal function for the targeted period of the simulation. Furthermore, for both
1D and 2D models, distinct scenarios were analyzed depending on the location of the water table
within the ground space.
In the initial phase, the temperature boundary conditions should be selected in accordance with the
first day of measurement, which for PMC-SB-05 is represented by 5th of October 2022. The requested
temperature boundaries can be visualized in Table 5.4 for the necessary reference depths. The input
shown in Table 5.4 is applied for both the 1D and 2D model, as well as for both cases regarding the
water table position. Nevertheless, for the 2D geometry, the bottom depth varies along the slope
profile, having a thickness less than 5m. For the intermediate situations an interpolation function was
applied to estimate the temperature values between two known reference depths. Using the material
properties described in Table 5.3, the simulation can acquire the equilibrium state by validating the
heat mass transfer criterion in every node of the model. In order to assure a suitable convergence
for the initial phase, Plaxis offers the possibility of modifying the control parameters describing the
iteration process, such as maximum number of unloading steps, maximum load fraction applied for
each step, maximum number of steps allowed for the iteration process, tolerance error defining the
convergence criterion, over-relaxation factor amplitude. An elaborate explanation indicating how the
previously mentioned parameters can affect the iteration process can be found in [Bentley, 2022b].
The chosen configuration for the steady state condition is included in Appendix A.3.6, Table A.9.

The transient flow phase requires a time dependent thermal boundary condition at the ground surface.
It can be defined as the measured ground surface temperature for borehole PMC-SB-05, based on
the dataset provided by [Tveit, 2022]. The temperature variation within the registered time span
can be observed in Figure 5.6. Regarding the bottom boundary, a null equivalent heat inflow flux
was designated along the slope profile as it was discussed in Section 5.4. An identical approach was
considered for the 1D and 2D model, respectively the scenario with the water table at the top and at
the bottom. The control parameter configuration, assigned for the transient flow scenario, can be
seen in Appendix A.3.6, Table A.10.

==PAGE:93==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
It can be observed that the available time period for the temperature measurements, describing the
thermal regime at PMC-SB-05, seems to be quite limited, comprehending only the freezing period
around Longyeardalen. A complete year dataset would have had a more beneficial contribution for
calibration purposes, nevertheless, the thermal model should be able to fit the existing data. The
selected method for tunning the simulated model consists of adjusting the thermal properties of the
soil layers, being represented by thermal conductivity, heat capacity or unfrozen water content, aiming
for matching the temperature variation with respect to time, at every recorded depth from PMC-SB-
05. Considering that all the thermal parameters were evaluated based on analytical formulations or
empirical equations [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], it would be reasonable to modify the estimated
values according to the in-situ measurements. As the unfrozen water content has already been set
to a reduced amplitude with respect to temperature fluctuation, assuming the soil specific coarse
texture, the main focus should be dedicated to soil thermal conductivity and soil heat capacity.
5.5.2
Reconstruct historical thermal behavior
Succeeding the completion of the calibration part, historical data can be reproduced using the
tuned thermal model. Based on the thermal information collected for 2019, 2020, 2021, 2022 by
[NVE, 2017], [RambГёll, 2017], the model should fit relatively precisely the temperature trumpets
generated from the thermal systems installed in the area. A certain degree of uncertainty should
be expected between the simulated values and the recorded ones, due to the spatial variability of
the geotechnical properties describing the valley, along with the lack of information regarding the
local hydrology or the thermal limited functionalities available in Plaxis software. The impossibility
of integrating the effect of snow cover depth or vegetation thickness, acting as a thermal insulation
barrier at the soil surface, the omission of solar radiation contribution from the boundary conditions
or the unavailability of simultaneously defining frozen and unfrozen thermal properties for the soil
material can be several limitations incorporated in the software. However, the observations regarding
the active layer thickness should be comparable and within reasonable tolerances.
Apart from the validation context using the existing datasets, the intention is to analyze the active
layer variation during the year 2016, as no information could be accessed. The outcome should be
further incorporated in the stability assessment targeting the landslide event in October 2016, as
presented in [Christiansen et al., 2016]. An additional challenge can be noticed regarding the year
2016, as no initial temperature distribution is available. In order to overcome this difficulty, either
the 2D model or the 1D model can be utilized for determining the initial conditions, by a long term
spin up, until the temperature variation stabilizes over repetitive cycles.
In the steady state analysis, the identical approach was considered for the historical data reconstruction,
as it was the case for the calibration process. Therefore, the main thermal input can be observed in
Table 5.5, for the years 2019, 2020, 2021, 2022, based on the reports developed by [NVE, 2017],
[RambГёll, 2017]. It should be mentioned that no temperature sensors were available near the ground
surface for the studied data series, hence the air temperature can be assumed as a justifiable
replacement for the surface temperature, according to the meteorological data from [MET, 1976].
For the spin-up process, requested to obtain the starting thermal regime describing the year 2016,
the initial temperature profile does not posses a significant influence over the final outcome, because
the long-term analysis allows for the temperature stabilization within the model. Thereafter, the
boundary conditions at the beginning of the spin-up model can be assumed to be similar with the
year 2019.

be obtained for year 2016. Hence, a spin-up of the thermal numerical model is requested in order
to generate the initial boundary conditions. The output accuracy should be increased by using the

for this phase can be seen in Figure 5.7a. Following the determination of the thermal regime at
the beginning of 2016, the analysis can be further conducted for 2016 by implementing the thermal
boundary condition shown in Figure 5.7b.

5.6
Analytical alternative
An analytical alternative for evaluating the thaw penetration depth can be represented by modified
Berggren equation, developed by [Aldrich and Paynter, 1966]. The computational method identifies
the thaw or frost front advancement, being an improved adaptation from the Stefan equation. The
assessment incorporates soil geotechnical properties and thermal measurements in the region of study,
leading to the expression shown in Equation 5.15. According to [Aldrich and Paynter, 1966], X is
the thaw penetration depth [m], О» represent an dimensionless correction parameter [вҲ’], k stands
for the soil thermal conductivity [W/(m вӢ…в—ӢC)], Ist illustrates the thermal index associated with the
76
==PAGE:96==
Chapter 5. Thermal numerical model
thaw period [в—ӢC вӢ…day], L is the volumetric latent heat defined for the soil [kJ/m3].
X = О» вӢ…(172800 вӢ…k вӢ…Ist
L
)
1
2
[m]
(5.15)
The effective thermal conductivity for the soil material, which can be defined as a medium composed
by grains, ice, water, air, should be approximated using Equation 4.12, attempting to average
the geotechnical parameters influencing the thermal conductivity of the soil to a general form in
time [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. The volumetric latent heat can be expressed as presented in
Equation 4.21 [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], being a function of soil dry density, mass latent heat
for water, water content, unfrozen water content. Because the unfrozen water content is temperature
dependent, an average thermal setup should be considered for the soil column in the computational
part. According to the discussion included in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], the thaw index can be
presented as a thermal function monitoring the temperature development during the thawing season.
It includes the concept of degree-day, correlating the amount of time when the soil is subjected to
thawing processes with the temperature intensity during this time span. The surface temperature
should be use for the determination of thaw index, nevertheless, assuming the air temperature instead
can represent a reasonable substitution [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. The thawing season should
be well defined temporally in order to avoid undesirable output deviations.
The dimensionless correction coefficient can be expressed as a function of two dimensionless parame-
ters, described as thermal ratio and fusion parameter [Aldrich and Paynter, 1966]. The formulation
for thermal ratio is shown in Equation 5.16, according to [Aldrich and Paynter, 1966]. In Equation
5.16, Оұ stands for the thermal ratio [вҲ’], ОҪ0 represents the average temperature of the freezing season
expressed in degrees below freezing [в—ӢC], t indicates the time duration of the thawing period [day],
while Ist stands for the thawing index characterizing the melting season [в—ӢC вӢ…day]. For the fusion pa-
rameter, the defining expression can be observed in Equation 5.17 [Aldrich and Paynter, 1966], with
Вө as the fusion parameter [вҲ’], cv representing the volumetric heat capacity of the soil [kJ/(m3 вӢ…K)],
while the other terms have been already discussed. Finally, the dimensionless correction coefficient can
be graphically interpreted from the curve illustrated in Figure 5.9, according to the work developed
by [Aldrich and Paynter, 1966].
вҺ§вҺӘвҺӘвҺӘвҺӘвҺӘвҺЁвҺӘвҺӘвҺӘвҺӘвҺӘвҺ©

(5.17)
Even though the modified Berggren equation poses great practicality in evaluating the active layer
thickness, it has its limitations and theoretical assumptions. A relevant example can be emphasized
by the manner of how the equation treats the freezing process, from bottom to top, while in reality
the frozen layer ascension occurs from the ground surface to deeper locations. Hence, the situation
when a layer of thawed material is trapped between two frozen zones cannot be reproduced using
this analytical approach. Furthermore, it can be difficult to integrate accurately the freezing days
encountered within the thawing period only by using the formulation presented in Equation 5.9,
context which may lead to numerical errors. Nevertheless, Berggren equation can be a valuable
resource to compare the results generated by the thermal numerical model for the thawing period.
The required input geotechnical parameters, describing the soil material associated with the borehole
PMC-SB-05, were estimated as it can be seen in Table 5.7. For the values presented in Table 5.7,
the average temperature of the soil profile was computed as the mean value for the years 2019, 2020,
2021, 2022 according to the datasets provided by [NVE, 2017], [RambГёll, 2017], the water content
was estimated for the top 2m of the soil column, the quartz content was approximated at 80% based
on the soil texture and the guidance from [Tarnawski et al., 2014], the thermal conductivity of the
thawed soil material was interpreted graphically, as it is illustrated in Appendix A.3.8, Figure A.12.

Chapter 6
Hydrological numerical model
Understanding the hydrological context governing the area surrounding Longyeardalen is particularly
important from a stability perspective, especially for the steep slope systems observed in the region.
Combined with an unfavorable thermal circumstance, such as a high thickness for the active layer
during the thawing season, the water percolation through the ground can be considered an important
landslide triggering factor. A relevant example can be emphasized by the mass movement event
recorded in October 2016 [Christiansen et al., 2016]. Hence, a hydrological numerical model should
be created, aiming for the identification of the water table depth during infiltration scenarios.
A reduced amount of hydrological information could be found for the region, with no detailed data
about either the water table location along the slope systems or saturation profiles. Therefore, several
assumptions should be incorporated in the desired hydrological model. Nevertheless, based on field
observations, it was noticed that a considerable water discharge seems to accumulate at the bottom
of the slopes, either in the form of ice formations during the freezing season or as stagnating water
during the melting period. This detail appears to correlate with the relatively elevated permeabilities
shown in Table 4.4, as well as the designated diamicton geological classification describing the
dominating soil in the valley, illustrated in Figure A.2b. Thereafter, it can be expected that the
development of the drainage process to occur quite rapidly within the slope systems, particularly
taking into account the consistent slope angle values.
For the simulation part, Plaxis software was selected to compute the slope hydrological behavior. The
model was designed to reconstitute the 2016 rainstorm event, targeting a deeper understanding of
the precipitation impact over the slopes surrounding Longyeardalen. With a limited and long-lasting
amount of snow throughout the annual cycles, see Figure 3.5, the main focus should be directed to
the infiltration associated with rainfall. An extensive dataset can be accessed from [MET, 1976], as
it was discussed in Section 3.2. The proposed model, for the current research, is represented by a
two-dimensional slope geometry in the proximity of borehole PMC-SB-05, based on the topographical
information provided by the Arctic DEM platform. Therefore, both vertical and horizontal water
percolation can be analyzed, identifying the water flow drainage path through the soil material
along the slope. However, it should be mentioned that a 2D model can manifest a certain degree
of inaccuracy, due to the fact that water can spread on the lateral direction too, on the plane
perpendicular on the slope direction.
6.1
Theoretical background
The theory describing the finite element implementation, along with the element dimension definition
and nodal points distribution, is identical with the case presented for the thermal numerical model in
Section 5.1, because the same software was used for computational purposes. However, it can be
mentioned that for the hydrological analysis a higher order than triangular shape would be preferred
80

in order to assure a more efficient convergence and accuracy for the model.

while on the upper side suction will occur. Finally, infiltration boundary implies a time dependent
inflow in the system, being limited by the absorption capacity of the defined soil material. It also
incorporates the functionality of creating a ponding zone for the infiltrating water, assuming that the
soil reaches a fully saturated state. The expressions describing the infiltration boundary condition

The hydraulic behavior, describing the unsaturated soils in Plaxis computational environment, is
defined by the van Genuchten model, as presented in [van Genuchten, 1980]. This proposed model
allows for the interpretation of groundwater flow parameters within unsaturated soil areas, particularly
above the water table, defining the soil water characteristic curve (SWCC) requested for the analysis.
The expression developed for the determination of saturation levels, along the implemented model,
can be seen in Equation 6.15 [van Genuchten, 1980]. For Equation 6.15, S stands for the saturation
level in the soil [%], ПҲ represents the pressure head as a ratio between the pore water pressure and
the water specific gravity in every node of the model ПҲ = вҲ’pw/Оіw [m], pw indicates the suction pore
pressure [kN/m2], Оіw stands for the unit weight of the fluid flowing through the pore space [kN/m3],
Ssat is the maximum possible saturation for the soil, when it is subjected to saturated conditions,
recommended with a value of 1 [%], Sres illustrates the residual saturation, comprehending the
stationary pore fluid remaining in the soil structure even at elevated suction heads [%], gn is a
fitting parameter incorporating the rate of water extraction from the soil [вҲ’], gc represents a fitting
parameter developed in the generalized Van Genuchten model [вҲ’], ga is a fitting parameter describing
the air entry specific values for the designated soil [1/m].

The computational phases for the hydrological numerical model are quite similar with the approach
described in Section 5.1. Initially, a steady state phase is calculated, aiming to obtain a static
equilibrium within the model boundaries. The steady state analysis consists of implementing the flow
equation and the water mass conservation theory for all the finite elements in the system, respecting
the admitted tolerance set before initializing the simulation. Focusing on reproducing the hydrological
conditions for October 2016, a water infiltration boundary condition was created at the ground
surface, using the precipitation information for the period of time prior to the rainstorm [MET, 1976].
The steady state phase is followed by a transient flow analysis, incorporating the infiltration caused
by the rainfall and enough time afterwards to observe the water flow behavior inside the slope. For
this stage, a time dependent function was generated, along with appropriate boundary conditions
along the model. Once more it can be specified that by extrapolating the geotechnical data from
borehole PMC-SB-05 a certain amount of uncertainty is present in the Plaxis model, nevertheless,
the hydrological numerical model output can provide a reasonable insight into the flow pattern
characterizing the slopes surrounding Longyeardalen.
6.2
Model geometry
In order to keep the consistency of the analyzed slope, an identical geometry was intended to be
implemented for the hydrological model, as it was the case for the thermal numerical model. However,
due to the complexity associated with the model discussed in Section 5.2, multiple numerical errors
occurred during the simulation attempts, along with the lack of convergence in the steady state
phase. After various parameter configurations were considered, it was concluded that the hydrological
model should be reduced to a more restrictive form, hence, the geometry selected for the assessment
can be observed below.
The topographic data, describing the slope profile, was obtained from the Arctic DEM platform,
with the values illustrated in Appendix A.3.2, Table A.8. It should be specified that the hydrological
model comprehends the lower part of the slope, extending from 0 to 378m in length. Furthermore,
the chosen location for the slope system is equivalent with the selection considered for the thermal
numerical model, as it can be seen in Figure 5.2, aiming to make use of the geotechnical data
provided for borehole PMC-SB-05 by [Tveit, 2022].

==PAGE:104==
Chapter 6. Hydrological numerical model
As the water flow through permafrost is not intended to be simulated in the current analysis, the
maximum depth of the slope profile can be set to 2m, while the upper border of the permafrost
will represent an impermeable barrier. The previous consideration can be justified by the landslide
susceptibility assessment targeted within the project, especially when the failure is caused at shallow
depths as it was observed in [Christiansen et al., 2016].
The soil column was divided into four layers, seeking to accurately incorporate the soil hydrological
behavior at different depths, based on the designated permeability values and the representative
hydrological parameters describing the soil water characteristic curve. Therefore, the layer succession
can be described as L1 between 0-0.2m, L2 between 0.2-0.6m, L3 between 0.6-1m, respectively L4
between 1-2m. In addition to the geometrical boundary illustration for the model, technical details
are provided in Appendix A.4, Figure A.13 for distinct zones along the slope profile.
6.3
Material properties
The prevalent material describing the top 2m of the soil column is represented by diamicton, see
Figure A.2b, with some organic intrusions close to the ground surface [Tveit, 2022]. Diamicton is
usually described by a relatively high porosity and a consistent drainage behavior, contributing to a
favorable hydrological context for the stability assessment of the slope system. As it can be seen in
Figure A.2b, the cryostructure formed on the upper side of the profile seems to be structureless for
borehole PMC-SB-05, aspect which can be explained by the continuous seasonal cycles of thawing
and freezing. Therefore, following the discussion developed in Section 6.2, the material considered for
the hydrological numerical analysis is assumed in the thawed state, allowing the water to percolate
through its mass in accordance with the hydrological properties of the soil. The main parameters
of interest can be indicated by soil permeability, porosity, density and Van Genuchten parameters
characterizing the studied soil.
The chosen configuration for the soil properties, implemented in Plaxis model, can be seen in Table
6.1, emphasizing the necessary input requested for the simulation process. As it was previously
discussed, bulk density, porosity, and void ratio values were estimated based on the laboratory
testing, as it can be seen in Table 4.1, 4.2 while permeability was determined from the grain size
distribution shown in Figure 4.6a, by applying Hazen formula illustrated in Equation 4.7. When
the slope stability is analyzed under significant infiltration events, the importance of an accurate
estimation for permeability can be outlined, influencing the movement susceptibility of the shallow
zone along the slope. Due to the absence of more detailed measurements regarding soil permeability,
it was decided to use an equivalent value for both horizontal and vertical direction, as it can be seen
in Table 6.1.
The defining parameters for the soil water characteristic curve should be obtained based on laboratory
testing, focusing on the samples extracted from borehole PMC-SB-05, in order to assure a qualitative
reconstruction of the hydrological conditions in the slope system. Nevertheless, as no datasets
were provided for the existing samples, an alternative approach should be taken into consideration.
Extensive research was conducted on generalizing the parameters encountered in van Genuchten
model, see Equation 6.15, leading to the creation of widely accepted classification systems. From the
applicability perspective, two reliable sources can be mentioned as hydrological properties of European
soils (HYPRES) system [WГ¶sten et al., 1999] and United States department of agriculture (USDA)
system [Carsel and Parrish, 1988]. In the current simulation, HYPRES system was considered more
relevant to represent Longyeardalen region, thereafter the fitting parameters shown in Table 6.1 were
selected accordingly. Based on the options available on [WГ¶sten et al., 1999], the upper two layers
L1 and L2 were classified as topsoil coarse class, while the following areas L3 and L4 were designated
with the specific parameters for subsoil coarse class. A slight deviation, from the recommended values
85
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 6. Hydrological numerical model
in [WГ¶sten et al., 1999], was made by setting the water content in saturated conditions Ssat = 100%,
intending to simplify the computation as suggested in [van Genuchten, 1980]. By setting the Ssat to
100%, the air cannot be trapped inside the soil pore space when the saturated conditions are reached,
resulting in a maximum possible water content inside the soil structure. As no detailed measurements
are available for the area of study, the previously mentioned assumption can be reasonable.

6.4
Boundary conditions
Providing the suitable boundary conditions for the hydrological model can represent an essential
input for the simulated scenarios, having a considerable impact over the water flow through the
defined geometry. Each computational stage should be assessed individually, aiming to establish the
most realistic configurations applicable to the modelвҖҷs geometry. It should be mentioned that Plaxis
assigns by default seepage boundary conditions for all the inner interfaces, located within the model
edges, allowing the water flow to be dependent only on the governing equations discussed in Section
6.1, along with the designated material properties shown in Table 6.1.
Following the aim of reconstructing the rainstorm conditions, experienced in Longyeardalen in October
2016 [Christiansen et al., 2016], in the initial phase a uniform infiltration boundary conditions was
considered at the ground surface. The infiltration amplitude was set to 0.0015 m/day, as the
average precipitation level of the last 30 days prior to the rainfall, intending to create a realistic initial
environment for the studied slope. The selected value, describing the initial precipitation inflow, was
evaluated based on the information available in [MET, 1976], as it can be seen in Figure 6.1. The
upper side of the permafrost layer was considered as an impermeable barrier in the model, implying
the integration of closed boundary conditions along the bottom of the slope profile. For the right-side
limit, as the slope geometry is incomplete, a seepage boundary was considered, even though a certain
86
==PAGE:106==
Chapter 6. Hydrological numerical model
water inflow will exist from the upper locations along the slope system. However, by not having
detailed information about discharge values with respect to depth, it can be difficult to make suitable
approximations. Setting the right boundary of the geometry to seepage should have no impact over
water surface infiltration. Because of the steep terrain in the area, the water is expected to percolate
gravitationally down the slope. Lastly, the model left side limit was implemented as a head boundary
condition, with the water head level close to the surface, at the elevation 42.87m. The reason behind
the selected option can be explained by the river presence in the area, as it can be observed in Figure
5.2, producing a constant infiltration and water supply for the lower part of the designed profile.
Furthermore, as it was discussed in the previous paragraphs, during the field investigations it was
observed that a consistent water discharge can be visible at the bottom part of the slope, detail
indicating that a head boundary condition at the left end of the model can be justified. The initial
water table was set at the bottom of the model, allowing the steady state analysis to create the
underground water level before the transient flow phase. A detailed visualization of the hydrological
boundary conditions applied in the steady state analysis is provided in the technical drawing below.
Figure 6.1: Precipitation levels recorded at the airport meteorological station in Longyearbyen, see
Figure 3.2, including the values for the time interval September-October 2016 [MET, 1976].
For the transient flow analysis, the bottom closed boundary should be maintained, excluding the
possibility of permafrost melting due to thermal or hydrological actions on its upper surface. On
the left side of the model the head level can be preserved, contributing to the stabilization of the
water table position on the lower part of the slope profile, while the right edge should be set to
seepage, from a similar consideration as it was mentioned in the steady state analysis discussion.
The infiltration function, defined at the soil surface level, should be expressed in a time dependent
form. Thereafter, for the first day of the simulation illustrating the rainstorm event, a precipitation
intensity of 20mm was inputted according to the measurements included in [Christiansen et al., 2016],
followed by 15 days of slight water infiltration, as it can be seen in Figure 6.1. The decision to
extend the computational time by the specified amount can be explained by the required drainage
velocity. Considering that the geometry illustrated in Section 6.2 incorporates a length of 378m,
the hydrological behavior can be better understood when enough time is provided for the infiltrated
water to percolate downslope, even though the principal objective of the hydrological model targets
the identification of the water table location during the landslide initialization. The full configuration
of the implemented boundary conditions for the transient flow phase can be seen in the technical
drawing below.

6.5
Scenarios description
As was emphasized in the previous chapters, the created scenarios are aiming to reconstruct the
hydrological context for the landslide mass event in October 2016. Even though the initial intention
was to simulate the entire slope profile for the hydrological analysis, a series of multiple convergence
and numerical errors related to the geometrical complexity of the model limited the design to the
geometry illustrated in Section 6.2. The desired output from the computational analysis can be
represented by the time dependent water level monitoring with respect to the permafrost upper limit,
along with observations regarding the degree of saturation in the slope.
The meshing pattern poses an important aspect of the simulation process, defining the level of
precision associated with the model. With respect to the formulation of finite element size, described
in Equation 5.1, a relative size was input in Plaxis at a value of 0.75 for the global discretization
process. Attempts were conducted for the implementation of a finer mesh; however, the result was
the lack of convergence for the model within the steady state analysis, even with multiple control
parameter configurations. In addition to the general discretization factor, specific refinements were
applied along the slope clusters as it can be seen in Figure 6.2. The resulted mesh network can be
classified as relatively fine, with a total of 3078 finite elements inside the model boundaries. As it
was discussed in Section 5.5, the control parameters, describing the Plaxis iteration process, can be
a valuable tool to assure a satisfactory convergence for the model. Thereafter, the selected setup
can be visualized in Appendix A.4.2, for the initial steady state phase, see Table A.11, respectively
for the transient flow analysis, see Table A.12.
(a)
(b)
Figure 6.2: Selection of the coarseness factors for the clusters composing the hydrological model,
along with the delimiting edges refinements, shown for (a) Detail A; (b) Detail B.
Two scenarios are proposed for computational purposes, involving different assigned values for soil
permeability of the top two layers L1 and L2. In the first scenario, the permeabilities described
in Table 6.1 are selected, while in the second scenario both layers are defined by a horizontal and
vertical permeability equal to 8.640 m/day. Considering that no direct permeability measurements
or grain size distribution analysis were conducted on the samples originating from the upper 50cm of
90
==PAGE:110==
Chapter 6. Hydrological numerical model
the soil column, generating the previously mentioned scenarios can be beneficial for understanding
the infiltration pattern, along with soil saturation levels along the slope.
The absence of available saturation profiles, from the area of Longyeardalen, can create difficulties in
validating the output data produced by Plaxis computation. Furthermore, the limitations connected
to the 2D model should be taken into consideration. In reality, water can also flow in the lateral
direction, perpendicular to the slope profile, having a distinct dissipation behavior compared to
the pattern comprehended within the Plaxis model. Thereafter, the output information should be
assessed with a certain degree of reservation. Nevertheless, obtaining an insight regarding water
drainage paths within the slope systems, together with the phreatic level during infiltration events,
can pose a significant contribution for the landslide susceptibility analysis.
91
==PAGE:111==
Chapter 7
Regional stability assessment model
Estimating the regional slope stability for Longyeardalen and generating a landslide susceptibility map
can be a significant engineering challenge, due to multiple considerations such as the spatial variability
of the data extrapolated from individual boreholes, integration of suitable water infiltration models
in the analysis, realistic estimation of the vulnerable active layer thickness around Longyeardalen,
determination of the water table location within the slope systems at any point in time. Nevertheless,
within the current project, a general simplified stability model is intended to be developed using
TRIGRS software.
Figure 7.1: General requirements implied by regional stability assessment in TRIGRS, emphasizing the
wide variety of numerical datasets necessary for the computational purposes, based on the information
in [Baum et al., 2008].
92
==PAGE:112==
Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
The essential input data requested for the TRIGRS computation can be visualized in Figure 7.1.
Hence, it can be observed in Figure 7.1 that a combination of mechanical and hydrological features
should be provided in order to proceed with the regional stability assessment. Some specific input,
for example the water table location or the depth to bedrock, can be correlated with the output data
generated from the thermal numerical model, respectively the hydrological numerical model.
The necessary grids presented in Figure 7.1 can be generated with a geographic information system,
hence, the software selected for this analysis is represented by QGIS. The regional model targets the
evaluation of multiple slopes, aspect facilitated by the two-dimensional configuration available in
TRIGRS. The interest will be focused on precipitation shallow induced landslides, aiming to reconstruct
the landslide mass event registered around Longyeardalen in October 2016 [Christiansen et al., 2016].
With the model calibrated for the specified conditions, further scenarios can be developed, indicating
the type of field investigations or laboratory testing which would be advisable to conduct in the
future.
7.1
Theoretical background
The transient rainfall infiltration and grid-based regional slope stability model, abbreviated as TRIGRS,
represent a Fortran based program build up for the computation of shallow, rainfall-induced landslides
as a function of time, mapping the landslide susceptibility for the input area [Baum et al., 2008]. The
software can simulate the transient pore pressure changes withing the defined grid model, evaluating
the safety factor values during the infiltration events. For the stability assessment part, TRIGRS
operates based on the infinite-slope model, evaluating the factor of safety for every cell composing
the basic grid model [Baum et al., 2008].
The hydrological calculation relies on the analytical solution of the one-dimensional, vertical water flow
through an isotropic and homogenous material, in the form of extended Richard equation presented in
[Srivastava and Yeh, 1991]. Furthermore, the software integrates the evaluation approach associated
with either saturated conditions or unsaturated conditions, depending on the necessary requirements
for the analysis [Baum et al., 2008]. When the designated material describing the cell reaches its
full saturation capacity, the incorporated routing model can divert the excess water, originated from
rainfall infiltration, directing it to the downslope cells capable of absorbing the precipitation inflow
[Baum et al., 2008]. For the soil water characteristic curve parameters, the approximation included
in TRIGRS can be described by the exponential function developed by [Gardner, 1958].
The wet initial conditions are implemented in the software by combining the linearized solu-
tion for Richard equation, proposed by [Iverson, 2000], with the extended solution developed by
[Savage et al., 2003]. Based on the solution proposed by [Iverson, 2000], the equilibrium state
incorporates a steady component and a transient component. Thereafter, the steady state seepage
can be expressed as a function of initial water table location, saturated hydraulic conductivity, steady
infiltration rate, hydraulic gradient, slope angle [Baum et al., 2008], while the transient flow considers
a one-dimensional vertical downslope flow, with a time dependent infiltration rate and a zero water
flux boundary condition, at the ground surface, when no infiltration is assigned within a time period
[Baum et al., 2008].
Beginning with the rainfall infiltration pattern discussed in [Iverson, 2000], applicable for an infinitely
deep basal boundary, a finite depth impermeable basal boundary was implemented in TRIGRS,
aiming to evaluate the pore pressure variation, as it can be observed in Equation 7.1. According
to [Iverson, 2000], ПҲ represents the ground-water pressure head, t indicates the time duration, d
illustrates the water table depth measured in the vertical direction at steady state conditions, ОІ can
be expressed as ОІ = cos2(Оҙ) вҲ’(IZLT/KS), Оҙ is the slope inclination angle, Z stands for the vertical
depth direction below the ground surface, formulated as Z = z/cos(Оҙ), z indicates the slope normal

Based on [Baum et al., 2008], the computation of the water table uplift is related to the fillable
volume describing the soil material in the unsaturated zone. It should be mentioned that TRIGRS
assumes a complete saturation below the rising groundwater level for every cell of the model, as no air
is considered to be trapped within the pore space. Therefore, the fillable porosity is evaluated by the
software within the initial top of the saturated zone, up to the surface level [Baum et al., 2008]. In
general, various evenly distributed heights, referenced to the top of the saturated zone, are evaluated
during the iteration process until the static equilibrium is satisfied. The expression for the fillable
volume, consider in TRIGRS, can be observed in Equation 7.7 [Baum et al., 2008], with Оёs as the
saturated water content, Оёr represents the residual water content, and the other parameters were
described above.

According to [Baum et al., 2008], the routing method, defined for the surface runoff, can be explained
by deviating the excess surface water originating either from precipitation or upslope runoff to the
adjacent cells downward the slope systems. The excess water can infiltrate in one of the reached cells
or it can continue flowing farther downslope, if the infiltrability value of the slope material is exceeded.
The purpose of the routing method consists of preserving the excess water within the computational
model, even when water cannot infiltrate on the initial targeted cell [Baum et al., 2008]. Based on
the discussion in [Iverson, 2000], for the saturated or tension saturated areas inside the soil spectrum
the saturated hydraulic conductivity can be considered equivalent to the infiltration capacity, Ks = i.
The expression describing the infiltration rule can be observed in Equation 7.10 [Baum et al., 2008],
as a function of precipitation level P and the runoff provided by the upper cells in the model Ru. In
Equation 7.10, the infiltration intensity is controlled by the saturated hydraulic conductivity of the
soil material. The flow between the grid cells is assumed to occur instantaneously.

P + Ru > Ks
(7.10)
In TRIGRS, the slope stability assessment follows the infinite slope model, computational approach
discussed in Section 2.3.1. Thereafter, the safety factor, expressed as the ratio between the mobilizing
resisting strength and the gravitationally induced driving stress, can be observed in Equation 7.11
[Baum et al., 2008], with FS(Z,t) as the safety factor dependent on depth and time, П•вҖІ expresses
the soil friction angle for the effective stress, Оҙ is the slope angle, cвҖҷ represents the soil cohesion for
the effective stress, Оіw stands for the unit weight of groundwater, Оіs illustrates the soil unit weight,
Z indicates soil depth to the basal boundary, ПҲ(Z,t) represents the pressure head as a function of
depth and time.
FS(Z,t) = tan(П•вҖІ)
tan(Оҙ) + cвҖІ вҲ’ПҲ(Z,t) вӢ…Оіw вӢ…tan(П•вҖІ)
Оіs вӢ…Z вӢ…sin(Оҙ) вӢ…cos(Оҙ)
(7.11)
As it has be described in Section 2.3.1, the equilibrium condition can be expressed by a safety factor
value equal to 1, the failure can be described by FS < 1, while the safe condition is indicated by
FS > 1. In order to compute the safety factor on the unsaturated zone, above the phreatic line, an
approximated parameter should be considered as ПҮ = (Оё вҲ’Оёr)/(Оёs вҲ’Оёr), approach recommended in
[Baum et al., 2008]. Then, the matric suction term ПҲ(Z,t) вӢ…Оіw should be multiplied with ПҮ and
included in Equation 7.11.
