Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
7.2
Input data
Starting with the schematic diagram illustrated in Figure 7.1, the requested input data for the
stability analysis can be divided into digital elevation map, regional catchment area and routing
path, initial water table grid, depth to bedrock grid, infiltration distribution map, rainstorm temporal
features, geological zone map, geotechnical zone map. The previously mentioned datasets will be
elaborated within this section. Apart from TRIGRS, a complementary rendering software called
TopoIndex was used to convert several topographic grid networks from GIS format to the TRIGRS
configuration [Baum et al., 2008]. TopoIndex provides the possibility of adjusting the downslope
flow direction patterns, along with the quantity of water transferred between the adjacent cells and
the cell numbering system [Baum et al., 2008].
7.2.1
Digital elevation model
The digital elevation model contains topographic information describing the area of interest, by
storing the elevation values for each cell of the grid, depending on the available resolution. An
extensive set of DEM maps can be accessed from the Norwegian Polar Institute Platform for the
entire Svalbard archipelago. Focusing the analysis on the Longyeardalen region, the full scale DEM
was clipped to a reduced size, in order to comprehend only the area of study, as it can be seen in
Figure 7.2. The resolution for the map presented in Figure 7.2 is 5x5m, meaning that each pixel
covers a 5m wide with 5m long surface within the specified boundaries. The elevation difference
within the clipped surface varies between 0m near the fjord shore, up to approximately 617m on
the south-east side of the map. Furthermore, the steep slopes surrounding the valley can be well
visualized in Figure 7.2.
Figure 7.2: The clipped smooth DEM, comprehending the region of Longyeardalen, generated in
QGIS based on the original grid available on the Norwegian Polar Institute platform.
97
==PAGE:117==
Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
Considering that the DEM is further used for generating drainage paths and other hydrological maps,
no inconsistencies should be tolerated in the raster. For this purpose, QGIS provides a functional
tool which allows for smoothening the existing model, avoiding depression areas where the water can
stagnate or be deviated in an unrealistic manner. It should be mentioned that the map generated in
Figure 7.2 represents the DEM resulted from the smoothening process. Both TRIGRS and TopoIndex
include the DEM grid as an input file for the simulation part [Baum et al., 2008].
The slope inclination can be evaluated from the DEM, using the available functions incorporated in
QGIS. The slope angle estimation relies on the elevation variation between the cell borders, resulting
in the map illustrated in Figure 7.3. According to Figure 7.3, it can be observed that for the lower
part of the slopes, values between 30в—Ӣ-40в—Ӣcan be expected, while on the superior side the slope
angle may exceed 45в—Ӣ. The discussed depositional channels, delimitated by the sandstone formations
shown in Figure 4.7, appear to be described be a relative inclination ranging from 30в—Ӣup to 50в—Ӣ,
while the steep bedrock outcrop can reach inclinations as high as 74в—Ӣ. The previously stated remarks
can be applied on both right and left sides of the valley. On the southern area, the transition between
Longyeardalen and the two glaciers seems to be defined by a mild inclination fluctuating between
10в—Ӣto 20в—Ӣ. Nevertheless, at the edge of the moraine, several steep zones can be outlined, as it is
shown in Figure 7.3. Finally, the mountain located on the south side of the map, named Sarkofagen,
appears to posses similar inclination features as the rest of the slope systems surrounding the valley.
Figure 7.3: Slope inclination map computed based on the regional DEM, emphasizing the steepness
of the slope systems surrounding Longyeardalen, in contrast with the relative mild transition between
the valley and the fjord shore or the southern glaciers. The map was generated in QGIS.
98
==PAGE:118==
Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
7.2.2
Catchment area and routing model
The water catchment area can be evaluated based on the DEM shown in Figure 7.2. It should be
specified that some areas were not included in the simulation due to their diminished importance for
the purpose of the analysis, such as the extending plateau from Sukkertoppen eastwards of the map
or the remaining part of the southern glaciers, Longyearbreen and Larsbreen. The additional water
inflow, caused by the glacier melting processes, can manifest an impact over the stability assessment
on the southern area of Longyeardalen, however, the contribution associated with snow or ice melting
can be considered a fraction of the effects produced by powerful infiltration events. Thereafter, the
goal of the current model is particularly dedicated to incorporate rainfall infiltration within the soil
mass.
A sequence of steps should be followed in QGIS in order to obtain the flow direction grid, describing
the possible drainage path for the percolating water through the slope profile or the surface runoff.
The stream network in the region should be defined by selecting a suitable threshold value for the
Strahler order function incorporated in QGIS. This feature can delimitate the size of the streams
included in the computational process, focusing only on the main flow channels within the designed
watershed. Thereafter, with a threshold limit set to 4, the stream network is generated as shown in
Figure 7.4. For the created network, accumulation points should be specified along the valley, by
including their exact locations on the map. The identification process involves a certain degree of
uncertainty, as the coordinates are inserted manually based on the assumed behavior of the stream
network. Nevertheless, the previously mentioned step has a great importance for the watershed
generation, providing the software with the necessary information for the evaluation of flow direction
vectors. Lastly, the catchment area can be computed using the provided Watershed toolbox in QGIS,
followed by a transformation stage from the raster data to vectorial information.
Figure 7.4: Stream network system, generated from the DEM map shown in Figure 7.2, along with
SAGA terrain functionality package available in QGIS. The outlet points were spread along the valley
at the intersection of the main drainage paths.
99
==PAGE:119==
Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
Each pixel in the QGIS environment can be surrounded by a maximum of eight distinct pixels. Hence,
the water flow vector can select eight different possibilities of movement, within the defined catchment
area, depending on the geometrical conformation of the terrain and the governing gravitational flow
rule. The number of options considered available for the flow vector are assessed and stored by QGIS
raster generator, resulting in the map illustrated in Figure 7.5. The direction map presented in Figure
7.5 is created in the ESRI format, a numeric code based on a power two based indexing system, as it
can be seen in Appendix A.5.1, Figure A.14a. TRIGRS requires a different value allocation for the
direction flow grid, with the movement alternative numbered from 1 to 9. Thereafter, TopoIndex
application should be used to compute the raster in the appropriate D8 flow format, as it is illustrated
in Appendix A.5.1, Figure A.14b. The resulting grid can be observed in Figure 7.6.
Both the surface runoff and the gravitational water percolation through the slope system have to
follow the direction grid indicated in Figure 7.6. As it was discussed in Section 7.1, TRIGRS can
evaluate the infiltrability capacity for each cell and decide whether the infiltration can occur, or
the surface runoff should be generated, propagating the water to the downward pixels. In order to
approximate the water fraction transmission between the cells in the grid, along with the precise
number of cells which are eligible to receive the inflow, TopoIndex functionality can represent a
valuable tool for the analysis. In the current project, the selected option comprehends the possibility
of diverting the flow to a maximum of two adjacent cells, simplifying the simulation process.
Figure 7.5: Flow direction grid map, generated from the watershed computational process incorporated
in QGIS, expressed in the ESRI format. The numbers, included within the legend, illustrate the flow
available possibilities for each individual pixel, with respect to the adjacent cells.
100
==PAGE:120==
Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
Figure 7.6: Flow direction grid map, transformed by TopoIndex application from the format presented
in Figure 7.5 to the necessary input accepted by TRIGRS. The numbers, included within the legend,
illustrate the flow available possibilities for each individual pixel, with respect to the adjacent cells.
7.2.3
Water table initial depth
According to [Baum et al., 2008], the initial water table location, within the model boundaries, can
pose a significant input data, as TRIGRS tends to manifest a particular sensitivity to variations
related to the initial configuration. Due to the fact that no relevant measurements are available for
Longyeardalen, the phreatic line should be estimated based on the output provided by the hydrological
numerical model, then the grid model can be generated in QGIS for the area of study.
Based on the high values of porosity presented in Table 4.2, the relative elevated permeabilities
estimated in Table 4.4, along with the diamicton classification for the soil material in the area as
shown in Figure A.1 and A.2, a low level should be expected for the underground water, as the soil
material seems to possess a favorable drainage behavior. Furthermore, it is assumed that permafrost
can be assumed as an impermeable layer, not allowing water percolation through its mass. A similar
approach can be applied to the rock outcrop, which should be assessed as a bottom boundary
condition in the model. Therefore, the lowest admissible position describing the water table can be
designated as the minimum between the active layer thickness and the depth to bedrock. Considering
the targeted type of failure research in the current project, shallow landslides, the limitation of the
possible depth to the first couple of meters should not impact the intended simulation.
7.2.4
Depth to bedrock
The depth to bedrock is particularly important in the slope stability assessment, considering that
TRIGRS assumes an infinite slope model for computing the safety factor along the cell grid
101
==PAGE:121==
Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
[Baum et al., 2008]. Hence, it indicates the amount of soil material susceptible to mass move-
ment.
Following the discussion developed in Section 4.2.6, it was observed that the function
illustrated in Equation 4.5 can be considered a reliable option of estimating the depth to bedrock for
an extensive region. Nevertheless, the limitations connected to the extrapolation of the linear function
shown in Equation 4.5, to the entire region of Longyeardalen, should be understood accordingly.
The conditions may differ from one side of the valley to the opposite part, therefore, the simplified
model developed within this project should be enhanced by more field observations and borehole data.
Utilizing the available raster calculator feature in QGIS, Equation 4.5 was implemented at a large
scale, setting as input the slope angle map generated in Figure 7.3. Consequently, the computed
depth to bedrock map can be visualized in Figure 7.7.
As it can be seen in Figure 7.7, the deepest thickness of the sediment layer seems to be along the
riverbed, reaching values up to 22.9m for most of the valley length, while the shallowest sediment
formation can be encounter on the mountain plateaus around Longyeardalen. For the lower part of
the slopes the range of values appear to vary quite considerably, from 5m to 10m or 12m, indicating
a relatively deep level for the bedrock. This aspect can be validated by the exiting drilled boreholes
along Longyeardalen [Tveit, 2022], as can be seen in Figure A.1 for PMC-SB-02, PMC-SB-03 and
in Figure A.2 for PMC-SB-04, PMC-SB-05. Even though the bedrock was not intercepted during
the drilling process, it should be noticed that the sediment layer should be deeper than 4-5m in
the specified locations. The channels formed between the sandstone outcrop seem to be described
by a depth to bedrock below 1-2m, observation which tends to be validated by the field visual
investigations taken in the area.
Figure 7.7: Depth to bedrock generated map, expressed in meters, incorporating the linear formulation
determined by Equation 4.5 and the function of slope angle illustrated in Figure 7.3. The rendering
process was conducted based on the QGIS raster calculator.
102
==PAGE:122==
Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
Taking into account that the interest of the project is focused on the shallow landslide behavior,
the depth to bedrock condition should be adjusted in consequence. It was presented in Section 2.4
that the interface delimiting permafrost zone to the active layer can represent a vulnerable surface
within the soil column, facilitating downslope mass movements. Thereafter, the real thickness which
should be applied to the sediment layer may be interpreted as the minimum value between the
active layer depth and the depth to bedrock, computed based on Equation 4.5. From a mechanical
perspective, the previously mentioned approach can be classified as reasonable, as no displacement
can be expected to occur in permafrost during an infiltration event. The active layer thickness
considered for the raster calculation in QSIS can be selected from the thermal numerical model
output.
7.2.5
Precipitation intensity and time duration
The designed model intends to reproduce the landslide event registered in October 2016, according to
[Christiansen et al., 2016]. Thereafter, the precipitation level and the time duration of the rainstorm
will be selected in order to reflect the historical conditions on the specified day. As can be seen in
[Christiansen et al., 2016], the recorded amount of precipitation within a 24-hour interval was close
to 20mm. As TRIGRS expresses the infiltration rate in [m/s], the transformed input value for the
software should be 2.314814E-7 [m/s]. Furthermore, the time duration should be set in seconds,
leading to the implemented value for the time duration of the rainfall as 86400 [s].
It should be mentioned that the precipitation intensity can vary at a regional scale, especially when
significant elevation drops can be encountered within the studied topography. The Longyeardalen
area incorporates such geographical features, hence, the precipitation distribution should be dispersed
in zones of application. However, no specific information regarding rainfall inflow distribution could
be found about the region, resulting in the restrictive assumption that the precipitation intensity will
be evenly distributed around all the pixels forming the grid. This simplification should not impact
dramatically the stability assessment, nevertheless, it is important to be outlined.
Apart from the rainstorm infiltration intensity, TRIGRS requires the input of the steady background
infiltration rate, in order to establish the steady state conditions in the model [Baum et al., 2008].
A realistic assumption should target the period of time prior to the landslide event, in a similar
manner to the hydrological numerical model designed in Plaxis. Thereafter, the average precipitation
level, characterizing the month before the rainstorm in October 2016 was considered as discussed in
Section 6.4 and illustrated in Figure 6.1. The steady state inflow flux incorporated in TRIGRS was
set to 1.73611E-8 [m/s], according to [MET, 1976].
7.2.6
Geological and geotechnical zones
Geometrical delimitation of the existing cell grid into zones of reference can be considered an essential
part of the simulation, because of the mechanical propertiesвҖҷ allocation describing each zone. The
following parameters can be implemented in TRIGRS as soil effective cohesion, effective friction
angle of the material, unit weight, hydraulic diffusivity, saturated hydraulic conductivity, saturated
water content, residual water content, fitting parameter for soil size distribution [Baum et al., 2008].
The predefined zone will manifest its structural response to the perturbing external factors depending
on the assigned geotechnical characteristics.
A pragmatic approach can consist of dividing the cell grid according to the geological map included
in Appendix A.1. Following this selection, the surface sediment distribution will be correlated with
the expected geotechnical behavior of the soil in the area of Longyeardalen. The initial configuration,
adjusted in QGIS, can be observed in Appendix A.5.2, Figure A.15 [Rubensdotter, 2022]. Nevertheless,
the extensive dispersion of the generated zones in Figure A.15 can be difficult to simulate accurately,
103
==PAGE:123==
Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
due to the lack of geotechnical information from the field. Therefore, several layers should be merged
together in order to provide more consistent data extrapolation from the available boreholes. It
should be specified that the previously mentioned alternative will restrict the diversity of possible
input parameters, along with a decrease in the precision level describing the designed computation.
However, for the simplified model targeted within this project, it can represent the most appropriate
configuration, rather than extending the already diminished dataset with further assumptions and
estimations.
The compressed zones were generated with the aim of comprehending soil materials with comparable
geotechnical, geological, or physical properties, expecting a similar structural behavior. The final
format is illustrated in Figure 7.8, leading to six dominant geotechnical zones included in the
classification. The selected judgment, with respect to the merging process, can be observed in Table
7.1, presenting which zones were combined together.
Figure 7.8: The generated distribution for the geotechnical zones surrounding Longyeardalen region,
based on the geological map provided by [Rubensdotter, 2022]. Explanations regarding the merging
process can be seen in Table 7.1.
With the geotechnical zones defined as shown in Figure 7.8, a set of initial mechanical and hydrological
properties should be assigned for each individual zone. Due to the lack of consistent information
from laboratory testing or field investigations, the requested parameters for the TRIGRS initialization
file should be assumed from technical literature, considering soil materials with similar features.
Consequently, the selected values intending to describe the initial configuration of the model can
be observed in Table 7.2, while the appropriate references are illustrated in Appendix A.5.3, Table
A.13. The assigned values, presented in Table 7.2, should be subjected to a tunning process with the
purpose of obtaining an accurate reconstruction of the landslide event.

Considering the infinite slope model, incorporated in the stability assessment, soil cohesion and
internal friction angle can manifest a significant influence over the landslide susceptibility of the slope
systems. A particular focus should be concentrated around the steep slopes in the region, such as
geotechnical zone 5, where the friction angle appears to be at a similar amplitude with the slope
angle, indicating a relatively fragile static equilibrium. Furthermore, especially during a considerable
infiltration event, the sedimentary channels comprehended in zone 5 should be closely monitored, as
previous landslides were registered within the specified area.

Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
7.3
Computational script - parameters and grids selection
As can be seen in [Baum et al., 2008], both TopoIndex and TRIGRS programs are encoded in ASCI
format, allowing for numerical editing with an appropriate compiler. An example of TopoIndex
script code is included in Appendix A.5.4, respectively Appendix A.5.5 for TRIGRS, based on the
information provided in [Baum et al., 2008]. Apart from the illustrated computational codes, a list
containing the explained terminology encounter in the scripts is provided in Appendix A.5.6 with the
purpose of facilitating script understanding and the relevant assumptions considered in the simulation
part.
7.3.1
TopoIndex
TopoIndex program is responsible for transforming the data supplied from QGIS into the format
requested by TRIGRS software. The necessary grids imported in TopoIndex are represented by
the Longyeardalen DEM, see Figure 7.2, together with the flow direction vector map generated in
QGIS, as it is shown in Figure 7.5. The number of rows and columns, included in both DEM and
flow direction map, are generated automatically by the QGIS export function. Furthermore, the
coordinates delimiting the created map are also provided in the output file. TopoIndex can convert
the direction map, expressed in ESRI format, into the D8 flow system compatible with the TRIGRS
code, as discussed in Appendix A.5.1. For this purpose, the flow direction numbering scheme is set
to 1.
The exponent value, see Appendix A.5.4, indicates the type of flow distribution between the
constitutive cells of the grid [Baum et al., 2008]. A value of -1 was set for the previously mentioned
parameter, in order to restrict the possible water flow to a maximum of two adjacent pixels,
simplifying the runoff scheme without a considerable impact over the stability assessment. For
the number of iterations, a minimum value is recommended in [Baum et al., 2008] in the form of
1 + (rows + columns)/20. Nevertheless, in order to allow sufficient iteration time for the relative
large grids used in the simulation, a valued of 1000 was considered.
7.3.2
TRIGRS
The configuration targeted in TRIGRS, aiming of reconstructing the infiltration event from October
2016, depends on the selected input data to assure a consistent stability analysis. The information
regarding the grid dimensions should be correlated with the TopoIndex output, having the number
of grid cells imax = 539719, number of rows equal to 1100, number of columns equal to 1054,
respectively the flow routing cell grid nwf = 899382. Only one time step is of interest for the current
project, as the focus should be put on the structural condition of the slope systems at the beginning
of the storm and at the end of the event, hence tx = 1. Because tension saturated conditions are
assumed, which imply a suction manifested behavior in the unsaturated zones, the number of roots
for the series solutions describing the unsaturated zones can be considered negligible.
The number of vertical increments was selected with a representative value nzs = 10, taking into
account the relative shallow depths included in the simulation. Regarding the maximum number of
terms allowed for Eqaution 7.1, a value of 10 should be justified, expecting quite a rapid convergence
of the model for the implemented finite depth scenario. Due to the fact that the model incorporates
only one time period and a singular precipitation intensity, the day of study should not be divided
into more time increments nper = 1. The pore pressure evaluation should be conducted as close to
the ground surface as possible, thereafter zmin = 0.001[m]. For the water unit weight, a standard
value is considered as uww = 9800[N/m3]. The elapsed time set for the model is equal to one day,
representing the time interval between 14th-15th of October, resulting in t = 86400 [s], while the
number of incorporated geotechnical zones in the simulation was assumed to be equal to 6, as it is
106
==PAGE:126==
Chapter 7. Regional stability assessment model
shown in Figure 7.8. Both the depth to bedrock and water table location can be designated with
negative values, due to the spatial variability of the features across Longyeardalen. Thereafter, a
constant indexation should be avoided, being more realistic to implement a regional grid describing the
depth to bedrock thickness, along with the water table depth. For the steady background infiltration
rate such an alternative does not seem to be achievable, due to the lack of information regarding the
precipitation distribution surrounding Longyeardalen, consequently a singular number can be specified.
The background flux was estimated based on the precipitation data in the region [MET, 1976], for a
month prior to the rainstorm event, leading to the setup rizero = 1.73611E вҲ’8[m/s]. Finally, the
minimum slope angle was input as 0, permitting TRIGRS to assess all the cells within the defined
geotechnical zones.
Based on the geotechnical zonesвҖҷ distribution shown in Figure 7.8, specific mechanical and hydrological
parameters should be assigned for the constitutive soil materials. Therefore, the selection illustrated
in Table 7.2 was implemented in TRIGRS. In addition to the configuration shown in Table 7.2,
the fitting parameter for soil size distribution should also be provided. Considering that within the
simplified analysis, developed in the current project, the unsaturated zones should be defined as
tension saturated regions, it indicates the necessity of setting the fitting parameter to a negative value
as Оұ = вҲ’1. For the precipitation level during the storm, the inflow is considered to be distributed
instantaneously on all the pixels forming the grid, as only one time step was implemented in the model.
This detail may lead to a small degree of errors; however, this simplification can be considered within
the accepted tolerance. The infiltration rate value was evaluated based on [Christiansen et al., 2016],
indicating a value of cri = 2.314814E вҲ’7[m/s], applied between the defined time steps capt[0] = 0s
and capt[1] = 86400s.
Starting from the scheme illustrated in Figure 7.1, a set of input grids or lists should be integrated
into TRIGRS analysis, mentioning the slope angle grid, depth to bedrock grid, initial water table grid,
initial infiltration rate grid, rainfall intensity grid, D8 receptor cells grid, cellвҖҷs computational order
list, runoff receptor cells list, runoff weighting factor list, according to [Baum et al., 2008]. The slope
angle distribution can be seen in Figure 7.3, describing the topography around Longyeardalen. Both
depth to bedrock and initial water table grids are dependent on the numerical outcome from Plaxis
simulation, for the active layer thickness generated by the thermal numerical model, respectively
water table location interpreted from the hydrological numerical model. With a uniform steady
background flux and rainfall infiltration intensity, the grids can be neglected, as one value can define
the entire region. All the remaining grids or lists should be incorporated from the TopoIndex outcome,
providing information about the runoff path, order of computation between the adjacent cells, and
the water amount distributed on the downslope cells [Baum et al., 2008].
107
==PAGE:127==
Chapter 8
Results and discussions
Within this chapter, the results generated from the modeling simulations will be presented, along
with the relevant observations describing designing process and data interpretations. The relatively
simple infinite slope stability model computed in TRIGRS, combined with the more detailed numerical
models comprehending the thermal and hydrological behavior of the slope profile, have the potential
of providing a satisfactory overview of the landslide susceptibility context in Longyeardalen. The
previously mentioned approach can be considered of particular importance, especially taking into
account the limited amount of accessible data from the region. The implemented models can
represent a solid starting point for more complex analysis and research, particularly if more field
measurements are collected and laboratory investigations conducted.
8.1
Correlation of field and laboratory measurements
The distribution of the existing drilled boreholes, as it can be seen in Figure 4.2, appears to produce
a comprehensive overview of the lower part of the slope systems along Longyeardalen, including
geotechnical data and thermal measurements from both sides of the valley. Nevertheless, it should be
specified that additional information regarding saturation levels and salinity concentrations, describing
the soil columns, can provide a valuable support to future stability assessments. Furthermore, the
analysis can be enhanced by collecting samples and installing monitoring systems on the upper part
of the slopes, even though it can represent a considerable technical and logistical challenge. Also,
more laboratory investigations should be conducted on the extracted samples, in order to create a
better geotechnical data background for the region.
The stratigraphic log illustrated in Figure A.2b for PMC-SB-05 emphasizes the prevalent presence
of diamicton material within the soil column, leading to the expectation of a relatively coarse soil
and a permeable hydrological behavior. According to the laboratory investigations conducted on the
samples extracted from PMC-SB-05, both bulk and dry density measurements appear to indicate
low amplitudes, as shown in Table 4.1, resulting in elevated estimations regarding soil porosity, with
values over 60% as it can be seen in Table 4.2. The observed measurements seem to validate the
predicted natural condition, however, the numerical outcome should be visualized with some degree of
reservation, as several tests indicate slightly unrealistic values. A reasonable explanation, describing
the high coarse soil material, can consist of the location of the borehole, being on the lower side of
quite a steep slope which actively supply the superior part of the soil layer with new material through
debris flow, snow avalanche or rock fall. Moreover, the lack of compressive components in the area
of study can also pose a reasonable justification for the coarse characteristic of the undisturbed soil,
being slightly compacted by the snow accumulated throughout the year or the water infiltrated during
rainfall events. For the hydraulic conductivity assessment, the granulometric curve distributions were
used to estimate the permeabilities at different depths. Even though the dataset characterizes the
108
==PAGE:128==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
samples associated with borehole PMC-SB-06, as illustrated in Figure 4.6, it can be noticed that
the values obtained for permeabilities in Table 4.4, with Haze formula indicated in Equation 4.7,
outline the consistent high permeability feature of the soil column, being correlated with the previous
discussion.
The gravimetric water content curves presented in Figure 4.4, seem to also indicate a coarse type
of soil for the investigated boreholes. Assuming an approximate active layer thickness around 2m,
as suggested by the temperature trumpets included in Figure 4.14 and Figure 4.15, the distinction
between the pore water accumulated on the upper part of the soil profiles and the bottom part
can be emphasized. On the top side, it appears that the water content does not exceed 20% for
PMC-SB-02, 33% for PMC-SB-03, 35% for PMC-SB-04, 20% for PMC-SB-05, except for several
isolated samples. This detail can be associated with the lack of cryostructure formations, within the
seasonally frozen ground depth, allowing the water to flow gravitationally downslope through the soil
mass. Below 2m, where the soil is suspended in ice or irregular reticulate structures form, the increase
in the gravimetric water content can be noticed with an average estimated at 44% for PMC-SB-02,
10% for PMC-SB-03, 36% for PMC-SB-04, respectively 32% for PMC-SB-05. Nevertheless, the
presented values above can still be expected for a diamicton or gravel soil material, with a good
permeability. Some of the extremes observed in Figure 4.4, can be connected to the classified mud
or organic rich mud layers intercepted along the drilled boreholes, as shown in Figure A.1, A.2.
Considering that the thermal properties of the soil material were evaluated based on the available
geotechnical data, as described in Section 4.3.1, it can be difficult to appreciate their accuracy
or interdependency. Previous research was conducted in Svalbard regarding thermal properties
determination of permafrost soils [Eiken, 2019], [Bratlie, 2018], however the studied locations does
not seem to include significant investigations around Longyeardalen. Thereafter, future laboratory
testing should focus on evaluating the soil thermal properties, in order to provide more qualitative
input for the numerical model.
8.2
Thermal results
The thermal output can be divided into three subsections denoting model calibration, thermal regime,
analytical solution. The model calibration involves the fitting of the thermal parameters, describing
the thermal numerical model in Plaxis, in order to obtain a similar thermal regime with the available
field temperature measurements from borehole PMC-SB-05, provided by [Tveit, 2022]. For the
thermal regime part, the calibrated numerical model is used to validate the historical registered
temperature trumpets from 2019, 2020, 2021, 2022, followed by simulating the thermal regime
in the year 2016. The focus should be put on the active layer depth evaluation in the region of
Longyeardalen, generating valuable data which can be further transferred to the stability assessment
conducted in TRIGRS, in the form of depth to bedrock parameter, as discussed in Section 7.2.4. For
2016, a more detailed interpretation should be produced, by monitoring the active layer variation
during the thawing season and identifying the exact value for 14th of October, when the landslide
mass event occurred [Christiansen et al., 2016]. Finally, the analytical solution is intended to offer
an alternative method of approximating the depth of the seasonal frozen ground in different years,
implying an additional validation for the thermal numerical model.
8.2.1
Model calibration
The reference data, considered for the calibration process, is represented by the temperature
measurements, outlining the heat propagation behavior in borehole PMC-SB-05, as shown in Figure
A.5c [Tveit, 2022]. The modelвҖҷs thermal parameters, which should be tuned adequately, consist of
thermal conductivity, heat capacity, unfrozen water content. As it has been discussed in Section
109
==PAGE:129==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
5.5.1, the unfrozen water content can be difficult to evaluate accurately, however, the selected curves
seem to provide a satisfactory distribution with respect to temperature variation, especially for the
coarse texture of the studied soil. Thereafter, the calibration process should focus on identifying the
most suitable combination involving soil heat capacity and thermal conductivity.
In order to enhance the calibration quality, the temperature curves obtained from the thermal system
installed on field are compared at distinct depths along the soil column with the simulated values,
indicating the pattern of the heat flow. It should be mentioned that the recorded thermal information
from PMC-SB-05 comprehends the freezing season only, from October 2022 to January 2023, as it
can be seen in Figure A.5c, detail which can affect modelвҖҷs precision during the thawing period, by
not providing a suitable reference to contrast with. The geometrical configuration of the thermal
numerical model can be seen in Section 5.2 for the 2D geometry, while the 1D model is defined
as shown in Figure 5.4. Moreover, two distinct scenarios are computed regarding the water table
position, one with the phreatic line at the ground surface level, while the second case included in the
simulation assumes the water table at the bottom of the model.
Starting with the 1D model, the initial configuration described in Table 5.3 did not generate
satisfactory results for the soil thermal regime, neither when the water table was at the surface
level nor when it was at the bottom. Hence, several adjustments should be implemented for the
soil thermal properties. The model structure can be considered relatively complex, with 6 defined
layers within 5m deep. It was observed that by increasing the thermal conductivity for a specific
layer, the heat inflow associated with the layers below is facilitated, while the recorded temperatures
on the layers above tend to diminish. Also, the temperature fluctuation within the modified layer
seems to intensify, as more heat is permitted to propagate through the soil mass. The opposite
reaction was noticed when the assigned thermal conductivity was decreased. Thereafter, it can
be emphasized that the modifications applied on the thermal conductivity of one arbitrary cluster,
within the modelвҖҷs boundaries, impact the thermal regime of the entire system. Nevertheless, the
consequences generated by altering the thermal conductivity of one of the top layers, between 0-2m,
tend to manifest a more significant thermal response when contrasted with the layers L5 and L6 on
the lower side of the geometry. This pattern can be explained by the distance to the heat source,
represented by the time dependent surface temperature boundary condition presented in Section 5.4.
For instance, if a reduced thermal conductivity is assigned to L1, then the amount of heat allowed to
flow to the following layers in the system can be considerably limited, decreasing the effect produced
by changing the thermal properties for L2 to L6.
The heat capacity, defined for a soil material, influences the amount heat necessary to modify the
temperature within the material. Therefore, by increasing the heat capacity, more heat flux should
transit through the soil layer in order to change the temperature curve in time, while the contrary
behavior can be generated by decreasing the heat capacity. During the calibration process, it was
noticed that altering the heat capacity for an arbitrary cluster has a meaningful effect over the
specified zone, while the rest of components seem to be slightly thermally affected. Consequently,
it can be stated that the thermal conductivity alteration defines the shape of the temperature
curve in time, while the heat capacity describes the smoothness of the time dependent thermal
curve. Furthermore, it should be specified that both heat capacity and thermal conductivity are
numerically limited due to physical considerations. [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004] indicates the
maximum possible values associated with thermal conductivity, restricting the numerical range to
the thermal conductivity possessed by the combined constitutive components, represented by grains,
ice, air, ice. A similar approach should be emphasized for heat capacity of the soil medium, which
should not exceed the heat capacity specified for pure water. Following the previous discussion and
considering the initial parameters configuration included in Table 5.3, it can be noticed that more
flexibility seems to be permitted for modifying the thermal conductivity within the implemented
thermal numerical model, than modifying the soil heat capacity.
110
==PAGE:130==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
Relying on the observations described above, the calibration process leaded to the setup illustrated
in Table 8.1 for the one-dimensional geometry. The distinction between designing the soil in natural
moisture conditions or in the saturated state can be visible in Table 8.1, implying distinct necessary
adjustments for thermal conductivity and heat capacity. The discrepancy can be justified by the
specific thermal properties possessed by air, water, ice, as shown in Table 5.1. When the pore space
is filled with air, it acts as a thermal barrier for the heat flow dispersion, being characterized by
a reduced thermal conductivity. However, the air inside the soil texture requires a small amount
of heat in order to modify its temperature, facilitating the heat absorption within the designated
layer. The opposite can be mentioned about liquid water, which can be described by a more elevated
thermal conductivity than air, but it demands a considerable amount of heat to change its inner
temperature. Pore ice behaves in a different manner, presenting more thermal similarities to the soil
material compared to the other two phases. It can be described by a mild heat capacity, as shown
in Table 5.1, while the thermal conductivity is superior to water or vapour. The thermal features
presented for air, ice, vapour, can manifest a consistent impact over the thermal analysis in Plaxis,
because the soil layers implemented in the model incorporate elevated porosity values ranging from
64.40% to 67.70%, as it can be seen in Table 5.3.

In the scenario implying the water table at the bottom of the model, the unfrozen water content does
not impact the soil layers in a meaningful manner, as the cluster is assumed to be described by its
natural water content, which has relatively low amplitudes along the slope profile. The recommended
thermal properties can be observed in Table 8.1. Regarding heat capacity, it can be noticed that the
top three layers L1, L2, L3 did not require adjustments, while the lower clusters were modified with
at least double the initial value, aiming to match the variation recorded for the field measurements.
This pattern should be presented along with the consistent increase in thermal conductivity within
the upper 2m of the soil column, as shown in Table 8.1. Due to the fact that more heat is allowed
to transit through L1, L2, L3, and L4, the temperature change in the bottom side of the model can
escalate more rapidly, hence, an increase in heat capacity is demanded in order to assure a satisfactory
match with the measurements on site. The resulted fitting curves can be visualized in Figure 8.1 and
8.2 for several reference depths along the soil profile. It should be specified that especially at shallow
depths some thermal tendencies could not be reproduce accurately by the thermal numerical model in
Plaxis, as it can be observed in Figure 8.1a for the time interval 10-30 days or in Figure 8.1b for the
111

time span 15-30 days. It should be mentioned that the deviations observed in Figure 8.1 and Figure
8.2 can outline the thermal sensitivity of the top layers, especially in the case of L1 and L2, to the
temperature fluctuations at the surface, along with the time requested for the model to stabilize the
behavior to the imposed conditions and the influence manifested by the upper organic rich film over
heat propagation. Once more, the limited available dataset, which represents the freezing season
only, should be emphasized, leading to considerable challenges to obtain accurate calibrations on
the thawing days. Attempts to reduce the registered errors in the previously mentioned intervals
were performed, however they produce more pronounced deviations in different time periods in the
analysis, therefore, the best fit was targeted.

When the phreatic line is set at the ground surface, the soil is assumed to be 100% saturated,
according to the simplified option available in Plaxis. Thereafter, no air is trapped inside the pore
space, allowing only the liquid or solid phase of water to affect the thermal response of the model.
A similar calibration approach was considered for this case as it was applied in the scenario with
the water table down to the lowest geometrical boundary, however, the magnitude of the necessary
adjustments was considerably increased, as it can be observed in Table 8.1. In order to generate
a satisfactory fit, as shown in Figure 8.1 and 8.2, the thermal conductivities for the first layers L1,
L2, L3, L4, were amplified with over 27 times the initial values, reaching the highest amplitude
in L4 at approximately 29.50E-3 [kW/(m вӢ…в—ӢC)]. Because of the expected reduce values for the
unfrozen water content across the soil stratification, as illustrated in Figure A.8, most of the water
within the pore medium can maintain its liquid state, absorbing a significant amount of heat form
propagating further with depth. This observation can justify the consistent surge applied to the
thermal conductivity values, along with the increase associated with the soil heat capacity. Due
to the abundant heat flow transiting the soil material, the heat capacities should be modified to
maintain the soil temperature within acceptable limits. Therefore, all the heat capacities were at
least doubled when referenced to the initial estimated amplitudes, see Table 8.1. The bottom layers
L5 and L6 present a slower temperature fluctuation than the upper parts, because of their distance
from the surface heat source. With the changes implemented for the top 2m of the soil column,
the thermal conductivities describing L5 and L6 had to be diminished with 40%, respectively 30%.
The temperature curves can be visualized in Figure 8.1, respectively Figure 8.2 for the previously
describe scenario, along with temperature profiles along the simulated period in Figure 8.4. The
results obtained from the calibration process tend to indicate a favorable fit, nevertheless, the scenario
implying the water table at the bottom of the system seems to provide a more reliable outcome.

The geometry and boundary conditions associated with the 2D model can be seen in Section 5.2 and

were selected, as it was analyzed in the 1D situation. Due to the complex geometry describing the
two-dimensional model, the heat propagation patterns differ from the simplified version represented
by the vertical soil column. Moreover, the depth to the basal boundary fluctuates along the defined
system, hence, the thermal regime can be interpreted in various locations along the slope profile. As
described in Section 5.5, a cross section was considered in the lower part of the slope, assumed to be
in the proximity of borehole PMC-SB-05, evaluating the temperature profiles generated from this
area.
For the case describing the bottom underground water level, see Table 8.2, it was observed that the
required changes were slightly at a similar order of magnitude with the adjustments implemented for
the 1D geometry in Table 8.1. Nevertheless, due to the complex heat flow within the model, the
final values obtained for thermal conductivities are higher in the top four layers, while L5 and L6
required a small decrease in order to balance the heat circulation. The majority of heat capacities
needed to be amplified, when referenced to the initial evaluated solutions, apart from L2 and L3.
The discussions elaborated for the 1D simulation, in the natural soil moisture condition, should be
also applicable for the 2D model, justifying the implemented tuning for the thermal parameters. The
specific temperature variation, in time, can be visualized in Figure 8.5 and Figure 8.6 for various
depths, outlining the accuracy associated with the calibration process. It should be mentioned that
similar fitting challenges were encountered for the close to surface layers, indicating a higher thermal
sensitivity than the field temperature measurements, in the first part of the analysis. Moreover,
several temperature profiles are compared with the recorded field data, as shown in Figure 8.7,
presenting a relatively acceptable tolerance for the generated values.

thermal regimes for 2016, 2019, 2020, 2021, 2022.
Considering the typical dynamic hydrology, which characterizes the real slope systems, it should
be mentioned that both scenarios considered for the thermal simulating purpose can be classified
as slightly inaccurate. However, the calibration process deals with obtaining the desirable results,
allowing for elevated values for the thermal properties of the designed soil, on condition that the
reference thermal profiles are accurately reproduced. Moreover, the limitations associated with Plaxis
software should also be understood, by missing the integration of important factors of influence such
as surface radiation values, snow cover, vegetation thickness, distinct parameter definitions for thawed
or frozen soil. A complete and realistic model should incorporate a fully coupled thermal-hydrological
analysis. Nevertheless, due to the lack of available field information, particularly for the hydrological
part, the assessment researched in the current project focused on individual problems, treating
thermal analysis and hydrological analysis separately. Future work can continue the investigation on
the topic when more field datasets can be accessed.

8.2.2
Thermal regime
Following the calibration process described in the previous chapter, the historical thermal regime can
be reproduced for years 2019, 2020, 2021, 2022 using the 2D model, aiming for the validation of the
computational output with the in-situ measurements provided by [NVE, 2017], [RambГёll, 2017]. Fur-
thermore, the thermal background can be generated for the year 2016, when no initial measurements
were accessible. The interest should be focused on the active layer thickness identification for the
specified years, along with a time dependent thaw penetration monitoring dedicated for 2016. The
purpose of the detailed investigation over 2016 can be justified by the intention of reconstructing the
landslide mass event in TRIGRS, as described in [Christiansen et al., 2016]. Consequently, the exact
depth to permafrost should be established for the date 14th of October 2016, information which can
be further integrated in the slope stability assessment.
The temperature trumpets, summarizing the thermal context for the entire year, can be observed
in Figure 4.14a for 2019, Figure 4.14b for 2020, Figure 4.15a for 2021, respectively Figure 4.15b
for 2022, according to [RambГёll, 2017], [NVE, 2017]. It should be emphasized that the previously
mentioned temperature trumpets are provided from a different area than the location of the data
source used for thermal model calibration. Both computational scenarios describing the the water
table position were simulated, aiming to conclude upon the most suitable fit for the existing data.
The time dependent thermal functions, necessary to be implement as surface boundary conditions in
Plaxis, were discussed in Section 5.5 and they can be visualized in Figure 5.7 and 5.8, according to
[MET, 1976]. Because of the unavailability related to the surface temperature for the mentioned
years, the air temperature can be a reasonable replacement in the analysis. Nevertheless, this
assumption may cause a certain deviation on the generated outcome, when referenced to the recorded
field data, considering the existing distinction between air and surface temperature trends. Moreover,
additional uncertainty can be attributed to the spatial variability of the geotechnical parameters
describing the location of the recorded measurements, when compared to the reference site included
in the thermal model calibration. Therefore, rather than an exact match, a similar pattern should be
expected.
The outcome resulting from the simulated scenarios can be observed in Figure 8.9a for 2019, Figure
8.9b for 2020, Figure 8.10a for 2021, respectively Figure 8.10b for 2022. It can be observed that the
scenario with the water table at the bottom of the model seems to provide a more favorable match
for the temperature measurements from the field, than the case involving the phreatic line at the
surface, as it is shown in Figure 8.9, 8.10. The previously mentioned remark appears to be applicable
for all the years of study. Therefore, the discussion should focus on the model resembling the natural
water content in the soil, not the one in fully saturated conditions.
As it can be seen in Figure 8.9, 8.10, the general trend describing the maximum, mean, and minimum
temperature along the soil profile seems to follow the behavior monitored on the site, however, the
generated values in Plaxis appear to indicate a colder soil profile than the measured temperatures,
especially visible for the maximum temperature trumpet. The lowest deviation can be noticed for
the mean temperature trumpet, comprehending the average values for the entire year at different
depths. The fluctuations observed for the minimum and maximum curves illustrated in Figure 8.9
and Figure 8.10 can be caused by the arguments previously specified. Nevertheless, the evaluated
depth for the seasonal frozen ground can be considered reliable, with no more than 35cm deviation
from the recorded thicknesses. According to Figure 8.9, 8.10, it can be seen that the simulated
active layer thickness in the region varies from 2.15m in 2019, to 2.25m in 2020, decreasing to
1.80m in 2021, and finally reaching 2.25m in 2022. Once more it should be mentioned that the
measured distinctions between the computed and recorded active layer depths can be subjected to
uncertainties due to the distinct locations of the boreholes, spatial variability of the geotechnical
parameters, or the implemented surface boundary conditions. Hence, the real deviation can be in
121
==PAGE:141==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
reality even more limited than the situation presented in Figure 8.9, 8.10, if the location of borehole
PMC-SB-05 should have been considered and thermal data sets would have been available.

For the thermal regime describing the year 2016, the initial temperature profile should be established,
in order to present a consistent analysis regarding the studied period. For computational purposes,
the 1D model was used to generate the desired temperature profile, as discussed in Section 5.5.2.
Implementing the surface temperature boundary condition shown in Figure 5.7a, the model was spin
up for an extensive time period of 50 years, targeting a satisfactory convergence of the model. The
result of the spin-up process can be visualized in Appendix A.6, Figure A.17, indicating that heat
flow propagation stabilizes relatively rapidly within the first 5 years of the simulation. Furthermore,
the initial temperature distribution, incorporated in the 2016 assessment, can be extracted from the
simulated output at the referenced depths as Tz=0.00m = 4.211 в—ӢC, Tz=0.20m = 0.215 в—ӢC, Tz=0.60m =
-1.901 в—ӢC, Tz=1.00m = -1.313 в—ӢC, Tz=2.00m = -0.172 в—ӢC, Tz=3.50m = -0.974 в—ӢC, Tz=5.00m = -1.452
в—ӢC.
Figure 8.11: Temperature trumpets computed with the 2D thermal numerical model for year 2016,
emphasizing the active layer zone expansion.
Considering the results illustrated in Figure 8.9 and Figure 8.10 it can be concluded that the 2D
thermal model, implementing the underground water table at the bottom of the geometry, should be
selected for the analysis of the active layer development during the year 2016. However, the second
scenario was also included for comparison. The temperature trumpets can be observed in Figure
8.11, indicating a maximum depth for the seasonal frozen ground down to 2.50m. It can be noticed
124
==PAGE:144==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
that the resulted active layer thickness for 2016 exceeds the other values generated for the timespan
2019-2022. However, it can be seen in Figure 5.7b that the air temperature manifested a longer
melting trend, when compared to the time period 2019-2022 from Figure 5.8a to 5.8d, thereafter a
deeper thaw penetration should be expected for this particular year. Moreover, the landslide mass
event described in [Christiansen et al., 2016] should be also included in the discussion, outlining the
unfavorable stability context in the region caused by the elevated depth of the active layer, which
could have possibly contributed to the landslide initialization.
In addition to the maximum recorded value for the active layer thickness in Figure 8.11, a gradual
monitoring development was conducted for the thawing season, as it can be visualized in Figure
8.12. Apart from the thaw penetration depth, Figure 8.12 incorporates the frost table variation in
time, outlining the days when the soil stratification comprises a thawed soil layer delimited by two
frozen clusters at the bottom and at the top. This scenario can present a particular stability problem
for a slope system, nevertheless, the complexity of such an analysis exceeds the simplified model
developed within the current project. According to Figure 8.12 the maximum active layer depth can
be encountered on 2nd of November with an amplitude of 2.453m. However, on the specified date
the associated frost table was registered with a value of 0.182m, preventing the occurrence of a
considerable water infiltration scenario from the surface level. For the day of the recorded landslide,
14th of October, the thaw front was estimated to be 2.422m, having no frozen soil on the upper side
of the soil column. The previously mentioned result should be further implemented in the stability
assessment conducted in TRIGRS.

registered variation for the frost table, as the result of the 2D thermal numerical modeling in Plaxis.

8.2.3
Analytical solution
The analytical solution consists of the implementation of Berggren equation as described in Section
5.6, offering an alternative computational method to the finite element analysis conducted in Plaxis.
The outcome produced by the analytical approach can be compared with the active layer interpretation
illustrated in Figure 8.9, Figure 8.10, and Figure 8.11, for the studied years 2016, 2019, 2020, 2021,
2022.
With the soil material properties defined in Table 5.7, the thermal indexes evaluated in Table 5.6, along
with the time duration of the melting season and the mean air temperature of the freezing season
indicated in Table 5.6, the thermal ratio and the fusion parameters can be estimated as presented in
Equation 5.16, respectively Equation 5.17 [Aldrich and Paynter, 1966]. The calculated values are
included in Table 8.3. The two dimensionless parameters can be further used to approximate the
correction coefficient, requested for Equation 5.15 as described in [Aldrich and Paynter, 1966], by
graphical interpretation. The diagram provided in Figure 5.9 should be considered for the calculation
process.
According to the values presented in Table 8.3 for Оұ and Вө, the necessary interpretation process for
establishing the correction coefficient can be observed in Appendix A.6.1, Figure A.16. The results
generated in Figure A.16 are included in Table 8.3. Thereafter, the thaw penetration depth can be
calculated as shown in Equation 5.15 [Aldrich and Paynter, 1966], presenting the outcome values in
Table 8.3.
Comparing the active layer thickness values calculated in Table 8.3, with the field measurements
illustrated in Figure 4.14, 4.15 or with the simulated values based on the thermal numerical model
in Figure 8.9, 8.10, and 8.11, it can be emphasized that the results seem to be on the same order
of magnitude. Thereafter, the computed outcome from Plaxis simulation appears to be consistent,
being validated by an additional analytical method. It can also be mentioned that for year 2020,
the thaw penetration depth obtained with the modified Berggren solution seems to be slightly more
precise than the implemented numerical simulation, generating a value of 2.00m, instead of 2.25m
indicated in Figure 8.9b. An equally comparable situation can be observed for 2021, having the
recorded depth at 1.90m in Figure 4.15a, with the analytical solution indicating a depth of 2.00m,
while Plaxis computation generates an amplitude close to 1.80m. Overall, based on the calculated
values illustrated in Table 8.3, it can be concluded that the implemented analytical solution seems to
provide reliable results for the thaw penetration depth estimation.

Even though the modified Berggren solution can provide satisfactory results regarding the maximum
active layer thickness, it implies several simplifying assumptions which can affect the further integration
of the results into a complex stability assessment model. For instance, it can be a significant challenge
126
==PAGE:146==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
to estimate the frost table during the melting season with Equation 5.15, because of the assumption
that the soil layer freezes from bottom to top in the analytical method, while in reality the freezing
begins from the superior side of the soil. Furthermore, the average temperature of the soil profile
can be difficult to assume properly for future trends, hence, the analytical solution can have a
greater potential in reconstructing thermal regimes than generating predictions. A daily analysis was
attempted for implementation regarding the year 2016, based on the modified Berggren approach
presented in Equation 5.15, in a similar manner with the context shown in Figure 8.12. Nevertheless,
the gradual process of evaluating the active layer thickness was not successful, generating unrealistic
excessive results for the thaw front advancement. Further investigations can be attempted in future
research, about the most suitable method to adapt the general equation into a gradual monitoring
expression, preferably at a 24 hoursвҖҷ time interval.
8.3
Hydrological results
As described in Section 6.5, the purpose of the hydrological numerical model targets the monitoring of
water table variation in time, within the defined geometry, along with a summary interpretation of the
saturation level distribution along the slope profile. The infiltration event can be described according
to Section 6.5, based on the regional meteorological information interpreted from [MET, 1976] and
[Christiansen et al., 2016]. While the boundary conditions are maintained unchanged, as explained
in Section 6.4, two distinct scenarios are simulated in Plaxis, consisting of assigning different values
for the vertical and horizontal permeabilities of the top layers L1 and L2. The first scenario can be
described by the material setup presented in Table 6.1, while the second scenario involves modifying
the permeability values from 2.16 [m/day] to 8.64 [m/day] for both L1 and L2. Because of the
estimation model selected for permeability determination, Hazen formula shown in Equation 4.7,
together with the consideration that no samples were subjected to sieve analysis within the upper
50cm of the soil column, the two proposed scenarios can generate valuable information for the
purpose of the current report.
During the analysis, it was observed that the SWCC parameters defined for L1 and L2 in Table
6.1 seem to generate an unrealistic hydrological behavior on the upper part of the soil profile.
The irregularities consist of excessive suction values during the transient flow analysis, allowing for
development of a significant high degree of saturation along the top 60cm of the model, while the
expected behavior, considering the soil texture and soil geotechnical properties, may indicate the
opposite. Due to the lack of specific measurements from the field, in the form of saturation profiles,
it can be difficult to validate the interpreted results. Hence, a different parameters selection for Van
Genuchten model was adjusted for soil layers L1 and L2, indicating the configuration shown in Table
8.4. The input setup is resembling the definition selected for the bottom layers, leading to a more
consistent output.
The water table development, during the infiltration timeframe discussed in Section 6.4 and 6.5, can
be observed in Figure 8.13, Figure 8.14 and Figure 8.15 assuming that a 100% soil saturation can be
reached when the soil is located below the underground water level. The individual times selected for
representational purposes include the initial stage, after the steady state background infiltration in
Figure 8.13a, 8.13b, the day following the rainstorm infiltration event in Figure 8.14a, 8.14b and
15 days after the end of the infiltration event shown in Figure 8.15a, 8.15b . As it can be see in
Figure 8.13, 8.14, 8.15, the water accumulates on the lower part of the slope in the proximity of the
river system, allowing the water table uplift close to the surface and exiting the slope within this
zone. For higher elevations, it appears that the underground water level can be identified close to the
model basal boundary, allowing the downslope seepage near the upper side of the permafrost layer.
Furthermore, Figure 8.15b indicates a favorable drainage behavior for the defined soil stratification, a
127
==PAGE:147==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
context expected from the highly porous material classified in Table 4.2, along with the elevated
permeabilities determined in Table 4.4.
Table 8.4: Adjustment of the SWCC parameters defined for the top soil layers L1 and L2, based on the

Regarding the degree of saturation distributed along the slope profile, it was noticed that the model
seems to generate relatively more elevated values than the expected range for the studied soil material.
It appears from Figure 8.13a and 8.13b that after the initial steady state the amplitudes can reach
up to 45-55% on the top layers, at the end of the rainstorm the indicated saturation values suggest
maximums of 55-60% at shallow depths, see Figure 8.14a, 8.14b, while at the final time step the
degree of saturation within L1 and L2 can fluctuate around 45%, as shown in Figure 8.15a and Figure
8.15b. Without specific values from laboratory testing on the liquid or plastic limit for the studied
soil, it can be complicated to correlate the computed saturation levels to stability implications on
the slope systems. Moreover, a validating process should be conducted a priori, with the purpose
of establishing the reliability of the hydrological model with respect to the determination of soil
saturation, therefore, the water table location should be the main focus point for the output of the
current research.
The infiltration mode, comprehended by the infiltration function assigned to the surface level as
described in Section 6.4, implies a water flow perpendicular on the slope surface as it can be visualized
in Appendix A.6.3, Figure A.18. Furthermore, in Appendix A.6.4, Figure A.19 a suction distribution is
provided for the studied scenario, with the aim of deepening the understanding of the obtained values.
The illustration of suction zones can be beneficial for the interpretation of the relatively high values
generated for the saturation levels in the top layers. As shown in Figure A.19, the maximum suction
intensities can be encountered within L1 and L2, especially downslope the model. On the superior
side of the slope profile, the suction amplitudes seem to be decreased, indicating a lower absorption
rate for the defined layers. This detail can emphasize either a rapid water drainage from the top part
of slope profile to the bottom or a higher water content stabilized within the soil pores. Considering
that the van Genuchten model was initially designed for agricultural purposes, its applicability for
slope stability can be slightly questioned, nevertheless, the model should be adjusted and validated
with a larger extent of field investigations in future analysis.

==PAGE:151==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
With an increased permeability value for soil layers L1 and L2, from 2.16 [m/day] to 8.64 [m/day],
the water is expected to percolate at a faster rate through the soil medium, decreasing the registered
saturation levels accordingly. The hydrological model seems to indicate this theoretical approach, as
the saturation amplitudes oscillate around 42-48% in the top layers at the beginning of the infiltration
event, as shown in Figure 8.16a and Figure 8.16b, between 44-52% after the rainstorm, see Figure
8.17a, 8.17b, respectively the range varies from 40% to 45% at 15 days following the infiltration
initiation according to Figure 8.18a and 8.18b. The observed values appear to be slightly elevated for
the defined material, as it was also mentioned for scenario 1, however, without a reference dataset it
can be challenging to indicate a suitable adjustment for the SWCC parameters or other geotechnical
properties of the soil material.
Regarding the water table location during the hydrological analysis, the generated variation before
the rainstorm is illustrated in Figure 8.16a, 8.16b, at the end of the infiltration event in Figure 8.17a,
8.17b, respectively 15 days following the rainstorm in Figure 8.18a, 8.18b. A similar behavior can be
emphasized for scenario 2, as it was described in the first scenario. The inflow seems to lift the water
table approximately at the surface level in the lower part of the slope system, close to the valley
side, while upslope the infiltration does not elevate the underground water level more than several
centimeters above the assumed impermeable permafrost boundary. Considering the implemented
configuration of the model in both scenarios presented in Figure 8.13, 8.14, 8.15, respectively Figure
8.16, 8.17, 8.18, it can be concluded that the water table along the steep slope systems is not
expected to rise significantly outside the proximity of the permafrost layer or the basal bedrock.
The previously mentioned remark should be integrated into the stability assessment model designed
in TRIGRS, especially for the problematic upper zones. The initial water table location along the
valley or riverbed can be assumed close to the surface, as observed during the hydrological analysis,
nevertheless, due to the reduced value describing the slope angle, it is not expected to cause a
considerable structural vulnerability. Therefore, for the purpose of simplifying the stability model
in TRIGRS, the phreatic line characteristic for the low altitude regions around Longyeardalen, was
considered at the bedrock level.
Due to the lack of measured saturation profiles from the field, the validation of the numerical
hydrological model can represent a considerable challenge. Furthermore, the engineering assumptions
considered for the model implementation process, particularly for the soil water characteristic curve
parameters or the permeability determination, can induce additional uncertainty in the conducted
analysis. Nevertheless, a general trend can be observed from Plaxis output, improving the quality of
the simplified stability assessment model developed in TRIGRS software.
The upper part of the slope systems can be characterized by significant steep inclinations, as it can
be seen on the slope angle map in Figure 7.3. Within this context, the internal friction angle of the
soil can be in a fragile equilibrium with the existing slope angle, outlining the slopes vulnerability to
infiltration events capable of causing the increase of the saturation level or the pore pressure build
up inside the soil layers. Moreover, the degree of saturation can be correlated to soilsвҖҷ plastic and
liquid limits, as described in [CEN, 2004]. Without precise laboratory investigations for the previously
specified limits, it can be difficult to establish which saturation levels can be critical or responsible
of triggering a failure mechanism in the slope system. Once more it should be specified that the
model can be enhanced by actual saturation measurements from Longyeardalen, allowing for a more
qualitative calibration process.

8.4
Stability results
Following the discussion elaborated in Section 7.2, the majority of the input data requested for the
stability assessment has already been determined. Nevertheless, the generated output from Plaxis
simulation should be integrated in TRIGRS, in the form of depth to basal boundary and water table
initial location. Because the stability analysis is designed to reconstruct the landslide event described
in [Christiansen et al., 2016], the active layer thickness estimated for 14th of October 2016 is equal
to 2.42m, as shown in Figure 8.12. As described in Section 7.2.4, a realistic depth to basal boundary
for the model can be expressed by considering the minimum value between the depth to bedrock
computed with Equation 4.5 and the depth to permafrost, evaluated to 2.42m with the thermal
numerical model. Hence, this function should be applied for all the pixels forming the regional grid,
shown in Figure 7.7, by using the raster functionality in QGIS. The resulting map can be seen in
Figure 8.19. As illustrated in Figure 8.19, the valley bottom, lower part of the slopes, glacier moraine
area, and fjord shore appear to be described by the depth to permafrost condition, with a range of
values close to 2.20-2.42m, while the upper zone of the slopes, along with the mountains plateaus
seem to be characterize by a shallow thickness for the sedimentary soil layer.
Figure 8.19: The integrated depth to bedrock map for the region of Longyeardalen, by considering
the minimum possible thickness of the sedimentary soil, from the depth to bedrock estimated base
on Equation 4.5 and the depth to permafrost computed in Plaxis for 14th of October 2016.
The water table depth can be implemented based on the results observed from hydrological numerical
model, presented in Section 8.3. As it can be seen in Figure 8.13, 8.14, 8.15 and Figure 8.16,
8.17, 8.18, the water table uplift, caused by the steady state infiltration process on the superior
part of the slope systems, can be negligible, with the underground water level close to the basal
boundary represented either by permafrost or bedrock. On the river proximity the water level seems
136
==PAGE:156==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
to be close to the surface, as all the water flow accumulates along the valley from the steep slopes.
However, with the reduced inclination angles describing the valley bottom, the stability concerns can
be classified as minor, for the purpose of the simulating process. Thereafter, for simplicity, the water
table was set at the bedrock level for all the cells forming the grid, as illustrated in Figure 8.19. The
impact associated with different assumed scenarios, describing the initial water table position, can
be visualized in Section 8.4.2, presenting the variation of the calculated safety factor based on this
parameter. It should be specified that the infiltrating water related temperature was not included in
the current analysis, even though it can have an influence over the permafrost rich regions. When
the air temperature warms the meteoric water, the permafrost upper boundary can be degraded or
even lowered due to the seepage process, increasing the landslide vulnerability of the affected slopes.
Therefore, it can be more problematic to encounter rainstorm events during warm time periods than
when the air temperature is slightly above the water freezing point.
With the previously described assumptions, the input dataset necessary for TRIGRS implementation
can be considered complete. Regarding the targeted computational output two aspects can be
emphasized, consisting of the landslide susceptibility map for Longyeardalen, calibrated in accordance
with the landslide mass event in October 2016, and a sensitivity analysis for several important
geotechnical parameters such as soil cohesion, internal friction angle of the material, initial water
table level.
8.4.1
Landslide susceptibility map - Longyeardalen
According to [Christiansen et al., 2016], the expected mechanical behavior of the analyzed region
can be represented by the visible failure of the steep depositional channels on the right and left
side of the valley, included in the geotechnical area number 5 as shown in Figure 7.8 and Table
7.1, combined with sporadic local failures in other areas. It was observed that the initial parameter
configuration presented in Table 7.2 did not provide the desired pattern, thereafter, several properties
had to be tuned. The geotechnical zones represented by the rock outcrop, zone number 1, the
glacio-fluvial deposits, zone number 2, respectively the marine deposits in zone number 6 manifested
a suitable structural response, hence, the mechanical and hydrological properties designated for the
soil material should be maintained as described in Table 7.2. For the till and marginal moraine in
zone 3, and the debris flow, avalanche, rock fall deposits in zone 5, see Figure 7.8, the collapsing
related surfaces appeared to be unrealistically extensive, requiring a material strength increase, while
in the case of the mining dump material in zone 4, the computed safety factors seemed to too high.
The main parameters subjected to modifications were considered in connection with the safety factor
defining formulation, illustrated in Equation 7.11.
In zone 3, it was decided to increase both the cohesion and friction angle of the soil material, along
with the permeability and hydraulic diffusivity. In this context, the till layer should have a more
favorable shear strength, while the elevated permeability prevents the development of excessive pore
pressure within the soil medium. Consequently, the adjusted cohesion was set to 7.00E+3 [Pa], the
friction angle was increased by 18.03%, the permeability was changed to 2.50E-4 [m2/s], while the
hydraulic diffusivity was elevated with 0.00975 [m2/s]. For the mining dump material, the cohesion
value was diminished by 20%, while the friction angle was reevaluated to 31.50 в—Ӣ. Finally, in zone
5, the soil properties affected by the calibration process were the soil cohesion and friction angle.
The cohesion was amplified by 4%, along with a 0.2 в—Ӣincrease for the soil friction angle. The final
configuration implemented in the model can be visualized in Table 8.5.
Incorporating the numerical setup illustrated in Table 8.5, the landslide susceptibility mapping was
conducted in TRIGRS, generating the safety factor distribution around Longyeardalen, as it can be
observed in Figure 8.20a for the initial time step and in Figure 8.20b at the end of the rainstorm
event. It should be mentioned that TRIGRS limits the maximum admissible value for the safety factor
137
==PAGE:157==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
to 10, improving the calculation time of the analysis [Baum et al., 2008]. For illustrative purposes,
the maximum threshold selected for the safety factor in Figure 8.20a and Figure 8.20b was set to 2,
allowing for a better contrast between the geotechnical zones. The previously specified approach can
be considered justified, especially that the failure condition is represented by a factor of safety lower
than 1, as discussed in Section 7.1.
