In the initial phase, the minimum factor of safety computed for the model is approximately 1.57,
indicating that all the studied zones seem to manifest a static equilibrium before the simulated
rainstorm event is applied. However, as it is shown in Figure 8.20a, several areas appear to be
more vulnerable to destabilizations, including the steep depositional channels on both sides of
Longyeardalen, the abrupt river valley on the east side of the map, the downslopes surrounding
Sukkertoppen, the mining dump slope on the right side of the main valley, the downslopes surrounding
Sarkofagen on the south of the map, and the contours delimitating the two terminal moraines. It
should be mentioned that comparing to the initial geological zone distribution illustrated in Figure
A.15, the current simplified version comprehends only 6 geotechnical zones, aspects which may affect
the accuracy of the stability assessment. Nevertheless, for the purpose of the regional analysis, the
obtained values represent a good indicator of the stability context. The reason behind the higher
intensity of safety factor amplitudes, describing the areas mentioned above, can be related to a series
of factors such as slope inclination, the shallow depth to bedrock characterizing the depositional soil
layer or the pore pressure induced by the steady state water inflow. The slope angle appears to have
a comperable amplitude with the internal friction angle of the soil material for the cases described
above, while the steady state flow was set as an initial condition, prior to the rainstorm initiation.
Furthermore, it can be outlined that most of the problematic regions appear to be situated within
geotechnical zone 3 and geotechnical zone 5, as shown in Figure 7.8.

The stability context at the end of the rainstorm event can be visualized in Figure 8.20b, indicating
multiple regions with local or extensive failure. As shown in Figure 8.20b, the most affected areas
appear to be the upper parts of the slope systems surrounding Longyeardalen, both on the left and
right side of the main valley, along with the mountain system in the south. The registered safety
factors can be expressed by values as low as 0.93. Apart from the collapse patterns observed in zone
5, the remaining geotechnical regions do not present significant damage inflicted by the infiltration
event. Consequently, the generated results seem to be aligned with the computational intention of
the TRIGRS model, leading to a satisfactory reconstruction of the mass movement event described
in [Christiansen et al., 2016]. A detailed local map, comprehending the most affected slope during
the infiltration circumstances in October 2016, is provided in Figure 8.21, outlining the amplitude of
the destabilization process. Furthermore, additional detail maps are included in Appendix A.6.5, in
Figure A.20a, A.20b, for a better visualization of the regional context. The simplified model, created
in the current report, can be further enhanced to include more complex scenarios and simulations,
with the condition that more field data can be accessed and interpreted.
Figure 8.21: Slope stability detail map, comprehending the area surrounding the most affected
slope during the rainstorm event explained in [Christiansen et al., 2016], presenting the safety factor
distribution following the infiltration phase in TRIGRS.

==PAGE:160==
Chapter 8. Results and discussions
8.4.2
Sensitivity analysis
Considering that a reasonable number of assumptions were integrated into the stability assessment
model, a sensitivity analysis can be a valuable method to evaluate the impact associated with distinct
input data. For the current simulation, it was considered relevant to evaluate the variation induced by
the soil strength parameters, cohesion and internal friction angle, along with the water table location,
over the final results of the analysis. Therefore, the deviation correlated with the computed safety
factors is quantified within this section, by interpreting different scenarios. Furthermore, according
to [Baum et al., 2008], it is specified that TRIGRS can be quite sensitive to the provided input
configuration, hence, the outcome of the sensitivity analysis can be accessed to improve the model
precision for future simulations.
The proposed scenarios for the investigation part consist of the individual reduction of soil cohesion
by 5%, 10%, 15%, the increase in soil cohesion by 5%, the individual reduction of soil friction angle
by 5%, 10%, 15%, the increase in friction angle by 5%, the decrease of both cohesion and friction
angle by 5%, 10%, respectively the uplift of the water table with 10cm, 20cm. All the generated
results from the sensitivity evaluation should be referenced to the safety factor distribution presented
in Figure 8.20, at the beginning and end of the infiltration event.
Both cohesion and friction angle have a beneficial contribution to the structural stability of the
slope systems, hence, by reducing their amplitude the safety factor decreases accordingly. The
opposite should be mentioned for the scenarios involving the increase of soil cohesion or friction
angle. Regarding the water table uprise, it can be responsible for the ascension of the pore pressure
within the soil layer, causing a destabilizing effect along the slope profile. Thereafter, the water
table uplift can be associated with a reduction of the safety factor amplitude. Starting with the
cases implying the decrease of soil cohesion, the results are provided in Figure 8.22, for both the
initial phase and the stability context after the rainstorm. It can be observed in Figure 8.22 that the
safety factor variation appears to have comparable maximum values between the initial and final
phase. Nevertheless, following the infiltration event the affected regions seem to be more extended,
when referenced to the stability context prior to the rainstorm. As shown in Figure 8.22a, 8.22b, by
reducing the cohesion with 5% the generated safety factor fluctuation can be close to a maximum
of 4.69% in the initial phase, 4.88% at the end of the analysis, a diminish of 10% in Figure 8.22c,
8.22d produces a maximum contrast of 9.37% in the beginning, with a 9.75% in the end, while a
deduction of 15% applied to the soil cohesion leads to a decrease of the safety factor up to 14.04%
initially, respectively 14.63% at the end of the stability assessment, as shown in Figure 8.22e and
8.22f. It can be emphasized that the variation correlated with the factor of safety appears to be
proportional with the percentage amplitude reduced from soil cohesion. A similar response appears
to be obtained when cohesion is increased by 5%, as it can be seen in Appendix A.6.6, Figure A.21a
and A.21b, resulting in a safety factor threshold improvement by approximately 4.67% in the initial
stage, respectively 4.89% after the rainstorm.
A similar trend can be observed for the sensitivity analysis conducted on soil friction angle, as it was
discussed in soil cohesion case. The maximum amplitudes reached by the safety factors, evaluated in
Figure 8.23, seem to be relatable to the values presented in Figure 8.22. Even though the values
spectrum can be classified as comparable, the effects appear to impact different zones along the
slope systems. While on the cohesion case, the upper part of the slope appears to comprehend
a more intense variation compared to the reference distribution in Figure 8.20, the friction angle
impact, indicated in Figure 8.23, can be expressed by a destabilizing surge on the lower half of the
slope profile, combined with still a significant fluctuation on the superior zones.

effects seem to be diminished after the infiltration event. The major impacts should be emphasized
on the upper side of the slope systems, along with the steep depositional channels between the
sandstone formations, as shown in Figure 8.25a and Figure 8.25c. The resulted maximum amplitudes,

in which the water table input influences the final computation of safety factor in TRIGRS.
In order to have a suitable visualization of the numerical output, evaluated from the stability
assessment, the spectrum of values characterizing each studied case can be observed in Table 8.6. As
indicated in Table 8.6, it appears that the individual reduction of soil cohesion or friction angle seem
to produce a similar maximum threshold for the safety factor computation as the water table gradual
uplift. Furthermore, combining the decrease in cohesion and friction angle can generate a more
complex stability response from the soil system, rather than modifying each component individually,
even though the intensity of the calculated safety factors does not appear to deviate considerably.

Chapter 9
Conclusions
The available information accessible for the conducted research, consisting of geotechnical and thermal
data interpretated from the drilled boreholes along Longyeardalen [Tveit, 2022], as shown in Figure
4.2, along with the additional data collected from previous investigations [DS-EntreprenГёr-AS, 2019],
[RambГёll, 2017], [NVE, 2017], can provide a relatively satisfactory overview of the soil behavior in
the region. Furthermore, as it was discussed in Section 8.1, the assessed soil properties appear to
have an adequate degree of correlation with the installed monitoring systems on the field, particularly
applicable for the recorded thermal regime. Thereafter, it can be concluded that the processed
amount of data matches the minimum input requirements, in order to generate a relatively qualitative
and reliable thermal numerical model, hydrological numerical model, stability assessment model.
Nevertheless, it should be specified that a considerable number of soil properties were approximated
or reproduced from technical literature, hence, more laboratory testing and field investigations should
be conducted in the region, particularly for the determination of complementary soil hydrological
parameters, thermal parameters, and strength parameters.
The implemented soil thermal properties for the thermal numerical model in Plaxis, including soil heat
capacity, soil thermal conductivity, unfrozen water content, were evaluated based on the available
geotechnical data, following the guiding procedure in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. Because of
this aspect, the calibration process required significant adjustments for the input parameters, as can
be contrasted between Table 5.3, Table 8.1 and Table 8.2. In addition, the provided data series
referenced for the calibration part, as shown in Figure A.5c, comprehend only temperature profiles
from the freezing season between October 2022 and January 2023, aspect which restricted the level
of precision intended for the simulation. Nevertheless, the targeted historical data reconstruction
was characterized by a satisfactory pattern, particularly for the approximation of the active layer
thickness in the region, as can be seen in Figure 8.9a for 2019, Figure 8.9b for 2020, Figure 8.10a
for 2021, Figure 8.10b for 2022, selecting the numerical model with the water table at the bottom
of the geometry. The maximum observed deviation between the active layer thickness simulated
with the thermal numerical model and the recorded data on the site was registered in 2022, with an
amplitude of 35cm, while the most precise evaluation was generated in 2021, consisting of a 10cm
variation. It should be noted that the location of the interpreted field thermal data differs from the
position of the borehole PMC-SB-05, used for the calibration process. Apart from validating the
results with the existing temperature measurements, the aim of the thermal model was to establish
the thermal regime for year 2016, converting the depth to permafrost amplitude into an initial input
for TRIGRS assessment. The maximum thickness was successfully identified in Figure 8.11 as 2.50m,
while a gradual daily base monitoring was generated as shown in Figure 8.12. According to the
interpretation from Figure 8.12, the active layer depth was established for 14th of October, being
the day of the mass movement event described in [Christiansen et al., 2016], with an amplitude of
2.42m. An alternative analytical solution was also selected with the purpose of validating the thermal
numerical output from Plaxis, in the form of modified Berggren equation illustrated in Equation 5.15.
147
==PAGE:167==
Chapter 9. Conclusions
It could be concluded that the correlation was quite satisfactory between the computed analytical
values, included in Table 8.3, and the amplitudes generated by the thermal numerical model in Figure
8.9, 8.10, and 8.11.
For the hydrological numerical model most of the input parameters were derived from existing
geotechnical data or interpreted from technical literature, as it was the case for the SWCC parameters
presented in Table 6.1 from HYPRES dataset, or soil permeability approximated with Hazen equation
from grain size distribution in Table 4.4. As the water percolation through permafrost does not pose
a target for the developed analysis, the upper limit of permafrost was assumed as an impermeable
boundary condition at the bottom of the model, as discussed in Section 6.4. Considering these
circumstances, a reasonable amount of uncertainty was induced in Plaxis simulation, especially due
to the lack of laboratory testing or actual field measurements. Nevertheless, the main focus of the
hydrological model was the interpretation of the water table location along the slope profile, behavior
generate in Figure 8.13, 8.14, 8.15 for the first scenario, while the second scenario provided the
output presented in Figure 8.16, 8.17, 8.18. From both simulations it can be concluded that the
underground water level should be encountered in the close proximity of the permafrost or bedrock
boundary for the upper side of the slope systems, while the water accumulates downslope close to
the river valley. The water table uplift along the steep slopes surrounding Longyeardalen can be
considered several centimeters at maximum. Therefore, it was decided for simplicity to set the initial
water table at the bedrock or permafrost level within the slope stability assessment implemented
in TRIGRS. The resulted saturation levels, along the top layers L1 and L2, were quantified around
45-60% during the analysis in the first scenario, while a range of 40-52% was interpreted from
the second case. However, without saturation profiles of reference from Longyeardalen, it can be
challenging to calibrate or validate the output precision.
The implementation of the regional stability assessment model for Longyeardalen, generated in
TRIGRS, required a consistent number of input grids, as discussed in Section 7.2 and 8.4.1. Most
of the grids were produced in QGIS, using the available functionality of the software. The results
from Plaxis computation, both thermal and hydrological, were integrated in the stability model, in
the form of depth to bedrock condition, shown in Figure 8.19, along with the initial water table
location discussed in Section 8.4.1. Because of the high distribution of sediment areas, as it can
be seen in Figure A.15, it was decided to reduce the model to 6 comprehensive geotechnical zones,
as indicated in Figure 7.8. By incorporating this assumption into the simplified stability model the
quality of the results was satisfactory, however, the precision was affected. Following the main
target of the simulation, the necessary conditions to reconstruct the landslide event described in
[Christiansen et al., 2016] were created by a series of adjustments to the input parameters, as it can be
compared between Table 7.2 and Table 8.5. The simulating process was quite successful, generating
the landslide susceptibility map shown in Figure 8.20, with an expected failure pattern. Moreover,
the detailed map presented in Figure 8.21 indicates the extensive collapse of the slope described
in [Christiansen et al., 2016], after the rainstorm event, while several areas around Longyeardalen
registered local failures. The failure criterion was expressed by safety factors with amplitudes below
1, as discussed in Section 7.1, with the landslide susceptibility maps being represented by a safety
factor distribution in the region of interest. Apart from the reconstruction purposes of the model, a
sensitivity analysis was performed in order to assess the influence of some input parameters, such
as soil cohesion, soil friction angle, water table uplift. The spectrum of values, assigned to the
safety factors, appeared to be affected in a relatively similar manner by the fluctuation of either soil
cohesion or friction angle. Nevertheless, cohesion variation seemed to manifest a greater influence on
the upper part of the slope profiles, as shown in Figure 8.22, while the friction angle fluctuation can
have a more significant impact on the lower part of the slope system, still influencing consistently the
superior side of the slope profile, as it can be seen in Figure 8.23. Furthermore, it was observed that
the water table oscillation can be quite problematic for the initial phase of the analysis, followed by a
moderate variation of the safety factor distribution in the final phase, see Figure 8.25. A summarizing
148
==PAGE:168==
Chapter 9. Conclusions
overview of the associated changes is provided in Table 8.6, providing a consistent support for further
modelling adaptations.
As a general conclusion, the steep slope systems surrounding Longyeardalen appear to present a
certain degree of vulnerability regarding landslide initialization, especially with the shifting climatic
changes which can imply a more pronounced active layer development and more frequent powerful
rainstorms in the region. Thereafter, it would be advisable to increase the number of installed
monitoring systems on the field, targeting temperature profiles, saturation profiles, along with more
laboratory investigations on the extracted samples and further development of the numerical models.
The simplified regional stability model presented in the current report can also be enhanced to
integrate scenarios with a higher degree of complexity.
9.1
Limitations
During the presented research, multiple limitations associated with the data interpretation process or
simulating numerical models were specified, in order to emphasize the manner in which the provided
results should be perceived. Nevertheless, the major limitation of the analysis can be visualized in
the list below:
вҖў Extrapolating the geotechnical data from individual boreholes to a regional scale implies a
certain degree of uncertainty, due to the spatial variability characterizing the soil materials.
вҖў The linear function designated to determine depth to bedrock for the entire area of Longyeardalen
can develop errors and unreliable results, when extrapolated to a large-scale analysis. The
cause can be related to the reduced amount of data incorporated into its definition, along with
the dependency on the slope angle parameter.
вҖў The temperature profiles used for the calibration of the thermal numerical model in Plaxis
comprehend only the freezing period, allowing for uncertainties regarding the thermal evolution
in the soil during the melting season.
вҖў Each soil layer, composing the model stratification, is represented by invariable geotechnical
properties along the designed slope profile, within its geometrical boundaries.
вҖў For the thermal transient flow analysis, instead of the surface temperature, the air temperature
was implemented as a surface thermal boundary condition because of the absence of field
measurements.
вҖў The thermal properties characterizing thawed soil and frozen soil state cannot be implemented
simultaneously in Plaxis software. Therefore, the top two meters of the soil column were
designated with thawed state parameters, while for the bottom layers the frozen state thermal
properties were selected.
вҖў The implementation of air temperature as a surface boundary condition in the model can
generate an excessive heat flow on upper part of the soil due to the lack of thermal functionalities
incorporated in Plaxis such as surface solar radiation coefficient, snow cover depth, vegetation
thickness.
вҖў The hydrological model illustrates a 2D configuration for the slope profile, emphasizing a
one directional gravitational flow through the soil medium. A more realistic hydrological
investigation should include a 3D model, also allowing the possibility for water to percolate
laterally, in the perpendicular direction to the slope profile.
149
==PAGE:169==
Chapter 9. Conclusions
вҖў The permafrost upper boundary was considered an impermeable barrier, even though infiltration
can occur to a certain extent.
вҖў The extrapolation of soil geotechnical information from individual boreholes to the entire region
of Longyeardalen in TRIGRS analysis. The conditions applied for one side of the valley may
not fit the stability context describing the opposite side.
вҖў The stability assessment in TRIGRS was performed with tension saturated conditions integrated
in the unsaturated zones, simplifying the evaluation process.
вҖў The regional stability model relies on the infinite slope method to compute the safety factor
within the defined grid. The homogeneity and isotropy of the soil material is assumed in
TRIGRS.
вҖў Water temperature is not considered within the infiltration model, detail which can influence
the stability associated with the interface between permafrost and thawed soil.
9.2
Further work to be developed
The methodology and numerical approach, discussed within the presented analysis, relied on a series
of assumptions and uncertainties in order to generate the regional stability model for Longyeardalen.
The assessment can be enhanced by performing some of the following tasks:
вҖў A wider range of laboratory experiments to be conducted on the existing samples, originating
from the drilled boreholes around Longyeardalen, especially for the evaluation of soil cohesion,
soil friction angle, soil thermal conductivity, soil heat capacity, unfrozen water content, soil
permeability, soil salinity concentration.
вҖў To perform a more complete calibration process for the thermal numerical model, with the
condition that a full year cycle of thermal data is provided from the field measurements,
incorporating both the freezing and thawing season.
вҖў Further investigations regarding the most suitable method to implement the modified Berggren
equation on a daily basis, supplementing the numerical computational analysis with an analytical
solution, aiming to monitor the active layer development during the thawing season.
вҖў Installing saturation monitoring systems around Longyeardalen, with the purpose of establishing
the water table location within the slope systems, along with the saturation profiles during
infiltration events.
вҖў To incorporate the unsaturated computational method for the stability assessment developed
in TRIGRS, when enough field data is interpreted for this purpose.
вҖў To define more geotechnical zones in the stability model, allowing for a more precise estimation
of landslide susceptibility level around Longyeardalen, in the form of computed safety factors in
TRIGRS.
вҖў To couple the thermal numerical analysis with the hydrological numerical analysis for multiple
slope profiles surrounding the valley, integrating the water flow pattern through permafrost.

Map of Geomorphology and surface sediments / Landformer og lГёsmasser, 
in Longyeardalen, Svalbard. 
The symbology follows the Norwegian SOSI-standard for Quaternary geological maps, which is based on genetic interpretation of process
origin of landforms and sediments. 
The map is based on aerial photographs and elevation models from the Norwegian Polar Institute  from 2008 and 2009, with some updated 
information aquired from drone imagery in 2020 along the active river in the valley bottom. Field checks have been conducted within 
different projects between 2012 and 2021. The valley bottom is mapped in lower detail than the slopes, due to the lack of updated baseline
data together with large and ongoing antropogenic changes in this part of the valley. Recent snow-avalanche mitigation on and below 
Sukkertoppen mountain is also omitted.
Reference to this map:
Rubensdotter, L. 2022. Geomorphology and surface sediments / Landformer og lГёsmasser, Longyeardalen, Svalbard. 
Scale 1:10 000. Map. Geological Survey of Norway (NGU)

Longyeardalen, Svalbard
Scale 1:10 000
Lena Rubensdotter

The water content of the samples was estimated as a fraction between the water mass recorded for
the studied samples and the weighted mass of the dry sediment. As it was already mentioned, the
water mass measurement between 0-5cm generates an unreliable computation of the bulk density,
with an amplitude close to the density of pure water. Thereafter, the processed value for the total
density of the sample between 0-9.5cm should be perceived with reservation. Table A.3 illustrates
the required values necessary for the calculation of water content.

Thermal numerical modelling
A.3.1
Water physical properties dependent on temperature
Water physical properties register an important variation along with the changes in temperature, as
it can be seen in Figure A.6 and A.7. The main parameters of interest for the current project can be
considered as density, in Figure A.6a, thermal conductivity in Figure A.6b, heat capacity in Fiure
A.7a, respectively water viscosity illustrated in Figure A.7b.

(b)

Water properties fluctuation as a function dependent on temperature variation
inside the soil system, including the behavior pattern for (a) density; (b) thermal conductiv-
ity, based on the information discussed in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004], [Kholmanskiy, 2020],
[Duangthongsuk and Wongwises, 2009].

A.3.3
Soil unfrozen water content curves
The unfrozen water content can be expressed as a temperature dependent function, including the soil
characteristic parameters Оұ and ОІ as described in Equation 4.14 according to the theory included
in [Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004]. In Figure A.8 the vertical axis represent the ratio between the
unfrozen water content (wu) and the average water content of the layer (w0). Furthermore, the
curves illustrated in Figure A.8 should be perceived as an anticipation of the unfrozen water content
existent in the soil at a specified temperature value.

A.3.4
Soil thermal conductivity interpretation
Thermal conductivity can be evaluated according to the graphical method developed for both
frozen and unfrozen states by [Harlan and Nixon, 1978], as a function of soil water content and the
registered degree of saturation. Hence, Figure A.9 illustrates the determination process for all the
layers included in the thermal model. It should be observed that for the superior part of the soil
column the unfrozen diagram was selected, while for the bottom area the frozen soil phase was
chosen.
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
(e)
(f)
Figure A.9:
Thermal conductivity graphical method, adjusted from the diagrams created by
[Harlan and Nixon, 1978], including the approximated values for (a) Layer L1; (b) Layer L2; (c)
Layer L3; (d) Layer L4; (e) Layer L5; (f) Layer L6.

A.3.8
Computation of thermal conductivity for thawed soil
The thermal conductivity for the thawed soil material, requested for the modified Berggren equation
illustrated in Table 5.7, can be evaluated based on a graphical interpretation as a function of particle
thermal conductivity, dry density of the soil, the degree of saturation and the soil granulometry, as
it can be seen in Figure A.12, based on [Johansen, 1975]. It should be mentioned that the visual

The initial temperature distribution for the thermal model in 2016 should be obtained by simulating

A.6.3
Hydrological infiltration mode
As discussed within Section 6.4, regarding the hydrological numerical model implemented in Plaxis,
an infiltration function was defined at the surface level of the slope profile in order to generate the
water inflow representing the registered rainstorm on October 2016. Plaxis apply the infiltration on
the perpendicular direction to the model surface, as it can be seen in Figure A.18. Furthermore,
the intensity of the water gravitational percolation can be observed at the bottom of the model,
illustrated in a vectorial format.
Figure A.18: Infiltration pattern incorporated in the hydrological analysis by the surface boundary
condition, at the beginning of the simulated rainstorm, illustration generated with Plaxis viewport
functionality.
A.6.4
Suction distribution in the hydrological model
It was observed in Section 8.3 that the saturation levels generated by the numerical simulation
appear to exceed the expected amplitudes for the designated soil material. Thereafter, it can be
beneficial to visualize the suction distribution along the slope profile, aiming to create a better
understanding of the water flow behavior withing the geometrical boundaries imposed for the model.
A calibration process could not be performed because of the lack of available field data, nevertheless,
the observation of suction levels can provide additional information for improving the numerical
model in future analysis. The suction distribution can be seen in Figure A.19a for the lower part of
the slope, while Figure A.19b comprehend the upslope area. The time selected for representation
indicates the end of the rainstorm inflow period.

A.6.6
Safety factor variation within the sensitivity analysis
As it was observed during the sensitivity analysis discussed in Section 8.4.2, the applied increase to
soil cohesion or friction angle seems to have a comparable impact over the safety factor fluctuation as
the scenarios represented by the percentage decrease. For the case consisting of 5% increase of soil
cohesion the results are illustrated in Figure A.21a and A.21b, indicating the two studied timesteps,
while for the output generated by a 5% amplification of friction angle, the results can be seen in
Figure A.22a and A.22b. Contrasting the two simulated cases for soil cohesion, represented by the
reduction in Figure 8.22a, 8.22b with the increase in Figure A.21a and A.21b, it can be noticed that
the safety factor amplitudes and distribution appear to be similar. An identical approach can be
emphasized for soil friction angle, by comparing Figure 8.23a, 8.23b and Figure A.22a, A.22b.

(b)
